THE BED PAGE.
The Czar of Customs.
I COMMONWEALTH legislation is always
] subject to amendment; and there are two
k / points (among others) in which the
Customs Act of 1901 should be amended
! soon as possible. The first is (c) of clause
i, which includes among prohibited imports
blasphemous indecent or obscene works or
-tides.” The word “blasphemous,” at least,
xould be struck out. It is practically
leaningless, except as a tool for the use of
;norance or bigotry. In Ingersoll’s phrase,
' ‘ blasphemy ’ is what a last year’s leaf says to
iis year’s bud: it marks the point where argu-
lent stops and slander begins.” The blasphemy
E one generation may be the faith of the next;
xe blasphemy of one country the religion of
nother country. It is an offence against liberty to
rohibit the importation of books, for example, on
ne ground that their contents are “ blasphemous.”
Vbo, in the progress of thought, in the continual
nlightenment of the human race, can arrogate the
ight to put a barrier to what shall honestly be
written ?
But the Australian Czar of Customs arrogates
he right. Upon his officials, in the first
nstanee, devolves the duty to raise the “blas-
,hemy ’’ ban. And what follows when a
Customs officer classes books as “blasphemous”
>r “ indecent ” ? By clause 229 ( b) they are
‘forfeited”; and by clause 208 “all forfeited
hips and goods shall be disposed of or destroyed
n such manner as may be prescribed or as the
Domptroller may direct.” Suppose, for example,
t gross of Strauss’s Das Leben Jesu are sent to
Australia. By clause 52 the Czar of Customs may
rank them as “prohibited imports”; by clause
329 he may forfeit them ; and by clause 208 he
nay forthwith burn them. If the owner appeals
so the High Court he may get redress ; though
she Customs Act says nothing of ways and means
ff obtaining redress in such a case. But in prac-
tice there is no redress ; for no person, unless for
the sake of the principle, could afford to take
action. 1
In the ease of a rare book, the matter is worse;
for the Czar of Customs can practically destroy
all evidence against himself.
It is not to the point to argue that “we are
quite safe; the power would ,never be abused.”
The point is that the executive of a Govt, has no
right to claim such power: even sleeping tyranny
is a menace.
The Australian Customs Act should provide a
clear and easy method of remitting disputes to an
Australian court of justice; and the. goods in
dispute should be held for a limited time, during
which the owner could place his appeal before
justice. It is not in the public interest to permit
any executive officer, even the Czar of Customs,
to e ercise a judicial authority which, as it
extends to tlfe arbitrary destruction of imports, is
practically final.
A second point in which the Customs Act
urgently requires amendment is clause 52 (a),
prohibiting the importation of
Any reproduction except by permission of the pro-
prietor of the copyright of any work copyrighted in the
King’s dominions, and of the existence of which copy-
right and date of its expiration notice has been given to
the Minister by or on behalf of the proprietor of Such
Copyright.
This is so intensely stupid that one wonders
how, in the name of Ignorance, the Common-
wealth Parliament came to pass it. Was there
not one of all the lawyers in the House of Repre-
sentatives or in the Senate able to tell Parliament
.that copyright is a matter of proof ? Registration
of copyright is an essential formula, nothing
more. At any time the Australian claimant of
copyright may be called upon to defend his claim
from attack. Then he has to prove, first, that his
claim is duly registered ; second, that it is justi-
fied: he has to prove that his work is a proper
subject of copyright. The Court takes no hear-
says : it takes sworn evidence only.
But the foreign claimant of copyright—what
does he do? He gives the Czar of Customs
“written notice” —not even an affidavit! but
“written notice,” which may apparently come on
a half-sheet of notepaper; and upon that act,
without proof of registry, without justifica-
tion of claim, without anything but the mere
say-so of some unknown person at the other
end of the world, the Czar of Customs
may prohibit—and forfeit—and burn—all in-
fringements of this say-so copyright which ask for
entrance ! Is it not prodigious ? And a Parlia-
ment of lawyers and others, elected to protect
I Australian interests, actually passed that into law !
Clause 57 provides that lists of all works, in
which copyright is notified in this off-hand fashion
to exist, shall be kept for public inspection at the
principal Customs Houses —so that the importer
has at least a chance to know the risk he runs.
But who ever heard of administration proceeding
on those lines—with ‘'the claimant’s affirmation
taking the place of evidence and proof? It is
more than prodigious—it is monstrous. And for
redress: there is none. For all the Act contains
to the contrary, any John Smith in England may
announce to the Czar 1 of Customs-
Sir,—l have the honour to inform you that I am the
proprietor of the works of Shakespeare, and that the
copyright expires on Ist April, 1925
and the works of Shakespeare will be immediately
listed in the principal Customs Houses as ‘ ‘ pro-
hibited imports.”
Of- course, even an official of the Czar of
Customs would not swallow that; though
the law would justify him if he did swallow.
But go into the byways of literature, and
see what avenues of chicanery are opened by
this astounding system of accepting state-
ment without sworn evidence, copyright claims
without proof. What will the Czar of Customs
do if he receives notice from John Smith that he
is the proprietor of Maxime Gorky’s works ?
John Smith may even back his notice by a copy
of the register at Stationers’ Hall; since Sta-
tioners’ Hall registers practically whatever is put
before, it—because registry means nothing, is
' nierely an essential preliminary to action. In
that case the Czar will assuredly announce that
Maxime Gqrky’s. works, other than those published
by John Smith, are “prohibited imports”; and
• Other editions can be forfeited and burnt without
further trouble.
Never mind whether this is likely : the fool of
an Act makes it possible; and there should be no
Open Door to roguery.
Again : if copyright is in dispute ; John Smith
who is lying on his claim, may notify that he is
pwner; and Thomas Brown, who publishes, will
find his books “prohibited imports” in Australia.
Forfeiture. Funeral.
Mark that the tea-man is shod, while the book-
man goes barefoot. Clause 54 provides that the
prohibited tea-man gets notice and 14 days to
show cause why his tea should not be forfeited •
the tea-man is on velvet. The book-man gets no
notice ; no time ; the Act gives the Czar power
to forfeit and destroy incontinently : the book-
man is on rocks and knife-edges.
Take it any. way you like, clause 52 (a) is
stupid. And, if it is based on a European model,
kindly remember that Europe is at most one week
from protest and explanation by European or
American book-exporters; and 'Australia is four
weeks.
■ v, :
Finally, the whole system of copyright wants
revision. Australia takes international copyright
as a legacy from Britain. We gain practically
nothing by it; we lose practically everything.
Australian copyright should be given to no book
that is not printed and produced in Australia
at least as soon as elsewhere. This means, for
readers : that they will get the best books quick-
est. It means, for artisans, a new impetus, a per-
manent support, to the whole of the book-pro-
ducing trades—printer, binder, type-founder,
paper-maker, ink-maker, cloth-maker—every-
body. It means, for authors, the cession of a
foreign right which is of little or no value, in
exchange for a securer home monopoly which is at
present the only thing they have to live by. And,
if a book is obviously fitted to benefit by interna-
tional copyright, we can take it to Maoriland,
and get international copyright.
Therefore, in literature as in other matters, will
the Commonwealth Parliament please legislate to
keep the brains, the work, and the money in the
country ?
Crumbs Swept Up.
THE volume of Transactions and Proceedings
of the Library Association of Australasia
is pub’ ished late (second meeting held
Oct., 1900 ; report, printed, end of 1901) ;
it is reviewed late ; for the vol. has been a month
in waiting ; and there is n’t much to say about it
anyway. S.A. taxpayer apparently pays for the
printing ; and se might spend sis money in worse
ways, though this'constant effort to make Govt,
pay the printers’ bills of private and quasi-public
organisations should meet constant resistance.
The Library Association is a society of public
librarians and others—not many others—for the
purpose of cultivating the science of libraries and
for the better fulfilment of the Library’s educa-
tional mission. The Transactions mix the
papers read at the 1900 meeting with a 1900 loan
exhibition catalogue and decorative programmes
(having decided to pay, the S.A. taxpayer ap-
parently thought se might as well pay for a loan-
catalogue and decorative programmes). Several of
the papers give us confidence in our public
librarians: possibly the best is an essay by A. W.
Brazier on the principles and practice of literary
classification: it is very open-minded, if you
can understand that; it leaves the impression of a
Brazier lit and glowing, ready to catch newideas
and set them aflame.
E. La T. Armstrong talks sensibly on the great
indecent-book question. He says—
Quite recently a bookseller in a neighbouring colony
[Dvmock, deceased, before the N.S.W. Commission] took
upon himself to say that certain books should not be in a
Public Library on the ground of morality. His arguments
were good only as against the indiscriminate use of the
books in question. The censorship of a Circulating
Library is simple. Even doubtful books should be left
out. But in a great Reference Library this does not
apply. The standard of one age is not the standard of
another. On our drawing-room tables to-day we do not put
Boccaccio, nor the Elizabethan dramatists, nor Smollett,
nor Sterne, nor even Richardson. Without these books,
and countless others to which exception might be taken,
a Reference Library could not be. There should be dis-
crimination as to whom such books are issued, but that
is all. Champagne is good, but champagne is not for
children.
And Balzac and Casanova are good, but Balzac
and Casanova are not for Prof. Morris—who
writes heavily of Matthew Flinders in the Trans-
actions. Prof. Morris relates also his own literary
experience. It appears that he has compiled two
books—the Higinbotham memoir and the Austral
English dictionary ; and in each case the publisher
has had financial reason for regret. Prof. Morris
seems to complain that few Australian public
libraries bought these books. Well, both books
should find a place in Australian reference
libraries; but they are hardly reader-bring-
ing assets. And the reason why their
Australian sale was not greater is not,
as Prof. Morris suggests, because libraries and
readers do not know what is good for them ; but
because, on the whole, his work was badly done.
He has accumulated good material; but he has
not the gift of selection and expression. Besides
being a literary Fish, he is too often a literary
Bore. And Australian readers (more credit to
them !) hate Bores.
R. D. Boys tells us that “during Mafeking
week there was a ‘ boom ’ in lost books at Melb.
public lending library.” . . . Dr. Leeper quotes a
Cambridge librarian’s remark that “he had not
found in twenty years five Englishmen, either
librarians or booksellers, who knew how to distin-
guish a folio from a quarto, or an octavo from a
duodecimo.” Very likely; since those labels have
become practically meaningless descriptions of
book-sizes, as Dr. Leeper ought to know.
With the Transactions comes the Oct. number of
the Library Association’s magazine— The Lffyrary
Record. It has interest. We learn that Dr.
Badham, as trustee of Sydney Library, transferred
“ first editions of Dickens, Thackeray, Lytton,
Bronte, and others,” to the lending branch, “to
be worn out by the fingers of toil-stained-bor-
rowers,” because he did not approve of fiction as a
component of a reference library ! . . . We learn
that “ the list of new books recommended in each
issue of the Record should be alone well wortli the
subscription ” —and turn over to find a very imper-
fect list of “new books,” and a list of juvenile
literature (recommended) which (we appeal to Mr.
Brazier for confirmation) shows but poor judg-
ment. ... We learn that the members
of N.S.W. country libraries (State-aided)
read fiction and play billiards.* And
considering how much Govt, money is given to
Sydney, and how N.S.W. country districts want
cheering up, we make no complaint. . . . There
is a good article on early Victorian magazines bv
H. G. Turner, which spells McCrae “ M‘Crae,”
and refers to R. H. Horne as “ the conceited
little poet, with his mock heroics and his theatri-
cal attitudinising.” . . . And there are numerous
misprints.

The Prime Minister’s English.
S. A. Rosa writes—
Mr. Barton’s recent speech at the Sydney pianoforte
factory (reported verbatim in S.M. Herald) was not
entirely devoid of errors in the use of English. For
instance
“ I would like to say a word also in defence of the tone
of debates in the Commonwealth Parliament. . . . We
have had one or more all-night sittings, and I would like
to say none was provoked by me.” In each case “ would ”
ought to be “should.” “Should” and “would” are
governed by the same rules as “ shall ” and “ will ” (see
Bed Page, 26/10/1), and it is, therefore, just as ungram-
matical to say “I 'would like ” as to say “ I will like.”
Liking is a psychical process that is independent of the
will.
“ We had a vast number of applications for employmen t
in the Public Service.” “I have done my best to prevent
it [disorder] in the Speaker’s chair in this State.” The
“ had ” should be “have had ” ; the “ have done” should
be “did.” In the first example the context shows that
Mr. Barton was dealing with events intimately connected
with the time of the delivering of his speech, which
events would cease to occur, he went on to say, upon in-
dustries being “ undertaken under a sound policy.”
Here, clearly, was a case for the use of the perfect form
of the verb, i.e., “we have had.” As to “I have done,”
Mr. Barton’s preventing disorder in the Speaker’s chair
had nothing to do with the present. Wherefore, the
perfect should not have been used.
“You will see the other absurdity of asserting that
those whose careers have been utterly scathless in the
past, who proved themselves worthy before the new nation,
could seizethe first occasion under Federation . .” In each
of these cases the pluperfect should have been used, be-
cause Mr. Barton spoke of the past, not the indefinite
past (which would have required a preterit) but the past
prior to Federation. “Had been” and “had proved”
should have been used.
“While my colleagues, Mr. William Lyne and Mr.
B. E. O’Connor, sat in the chair with myself.” The use
of “myself ” for “ me,” or, still worse, for “ I,” is a very
common error. “Myself” may be used either for
emphasis, following a noun or pronoun, as in “ I myself,”
or it may be used reflexively. In Mr. Barton’s sentence
“myself” is not used emphatically (the preposition
“ with ” should be emphasised), nor is it used reflexively.
Therefore “ myself ” should have been “ me.”
“We shall have gone to a vast deal of expenditure and
wasted a vast deal of time. "Vast deal” is somewhat
pleonastic; “deal” generally implies that the amount is
considerable. 1 Mr. Barton should have said “ vast
amount.” In accordance with the idiom of our language,
“ expenditure ” should have been “ expense.”
“ I have no fear but, that she [N.S.W.] will retain that
proud lead which the acts of nature and the brairis of
mart have given her.” “That,” in this sentence, is
awkward, redundant, and ambiguous. “But” some-
times means “exeept.” Wherefore the sentence might
mean “ I have no fear except that she will retain.” “ I
have no fear but she will retain,” without the “ that,” is
correct; but “ I have no fear that she will not retain ’’ is
to be preferred.,
" I do not want to blame those who barred Sydney ; it
may have beep considered as an astute step to take
measures which barred both Sydney and Melbourne.”
“Want” sometimes means “ need.” Such words should
generally be avoided when it is possible to choose a word
having only one meaning. “Wish” or “desire” would
be better than “ want.” “As” is redundant.
Mr. Barton’s sentences are somewhat Johnsonian—-with
most of Johnson’s rhetorical defects, but with few, if any,
of Johnson’s antithetical merits. Nevertheless, the
grammar and rhetoric of his speeches are not so bad as
those of Mr. Turner’s speeches. Both these gentlemen
would greatly benefit by a course of instruction in
grammar and composition. Mr. Barton, in particular,
would certainly make a promising pupil.

And Maeterlinck was to marry Mile. Leblanc
in England last month —a concord of Silence and
Sweet Sound. “Etre sage,” dit-il, “ce n’est

prendre a purifier celles qu’on a.” To filter Mar-
riage through Music: what an ideal for Wisdom
surveying Destiny! And the cricket on the

qui vaut mieux qu’aimer sop prochain eomme soi-
meme, e’est de s’aimer soi-meme en ” —Leblanc,
naturally.

A. Meston, Q. Protector of Aboriginals, writes
regarding Kendall’s use of Uloola —
Your correspondent, the Bev. J. L. Mathew, would
appear to have only a limited knowledge of aboriginal
vocabularies. The word Uloola was obtained by Kendall
from the Clarence Biver dialect, and is perfectly accu-
rate.
The word is also found in other dialects. I recently
brought in from Cooper’s Creek a couple of men whose
names are “Uloola” and “Caloola.” On Fraser’s Island
the eel was yooloo, or uloo, either spelljng being correct.
In the Cabbi-Cabbi dialect uloola was one of the names
of the porpoise.
On the Clarence the Wadji-Wadji tribes gave the name
of Ulodri to the oldest man, who was given authority to
advise the tribe on questions of fights and corrobbories
and bora ceremonies, none of which were undertaken
without his sanction. When he died, his successor,
some old man famous for his wisdom, went away
to the mountains to an interview with the Great
Spirit, Ulao-tahua, and returned to the camp en-
dowed with all the virtues of his predecessor. The
young wife, combunnigwon, had also to interview Uloo-
tahua before she had a prospect of becoming a mother.
In the Beeawav-ambo dialect of the Ooombangarie tribe,
south of the Clarence, Uloo-tahua became Uloola ; and
in the Awabakal dialect of Port Macquarie Uloola was a
name for the sunrise, though I find no mention of it in
Threlkeld’s vocabulary. Therefore, Kendall was quite
correct in bestowing the name on the hero of his poem.
The best living authority on the Clarence Biver
language, for the last 35 years, is J. F. Small, now, I
believe, at Kempse.y, on the Macleay. He was absolute
master of the Ucum-Ucum dialect in his younger days,
and may be willing to supply a little interesting informa-
tion on the subject.
“ Humour is a sense of the proportion of
things,” wrote Andy Lang— explaining why a
Labour member’s top - hat strikes people as
humourous. And the Amer. Christian Mis-
sionary Society brings fresh parsley to Mark
Twain—
At the annual convention, the treasurer announced
that the receipts for the past year were only some
£24,000. This showed a considerable falling off from the
average of the preceding years. The explanation given
by the treasurer was that Mark Twain had been directly
responsible for the disproportion in the receipts. An
acrimonious controversy was recently waged; in the public
press between the famous American humourist and the
now famous American missionary, Dr. Ament, whose
“looting” exploits in. China have been the subject
bitter comment by Mr. Clemens. The Society agreed
that Mr. Clemens had done immense harm.
So a sense of the proportion of things accounts
for a disproportion iof missionaries’ incomes. How
these little things dovetail in 1
Australian writers in London. Becke, the Inde-
fatigable, has produced another book, Breachley,
Black Sheep. Lawson is not relishing the winter,
and talks of returning to Australia. Some of his
recent work in Blackwood would hardly pass
muster here. L. Mack’s book, The Life and
Letters of an Australian Girl, will be published
by Unwin, probably in the Eng. spring season.

seems plainly this verse in Basil Hood’s new comic opera,
The Emerald Isle (from the patter song, “ Imitations'”)—
Oh ! the age in which we ’re living strikes a man of any
sense
As an age of make-believe, of imitation, and pretence ;
And it’s gradually growing more.impossible to see
The difference between what people are —and seem to be!
Our ladies grow more youthful now the longer they ’re

(But for such miscalculations they, will always make

“Werrimee’s” idea—
Do not we need an Aboriginal Gazetteer of Australia—
with Aboriginal-Australian jmaps? Every creek, river,
lagoon,, mountain, and town site in Australia has an
aboriginal name, relating to some local peculiarity or
incident. A compilation of such names, with meanings
and origin, together with native historical items, legends,
racial customs, and other matter, would be interesting.
In many places the names in a district have some peculiar
affix or prefix, which, to the aboriginal student, would be
often sufficient to locate them. About the S.W. border
of Queensland, for instance, many names begin with Noe,
as Nockatunga, Nocundra, Noekaburara, Noeabareena,
&c. ; while in the neighbourhood of the Darling we have
the termination Gam'd, as in Wilcannia, Bancania, Yan-
oania, &c. The terminals ar, ah, 00, ee are common all
over Australia, but the prefixes are generally terri-
torially distinctive, a matter overlooked by most writers
when coining Australian names. Queensland, to my
fancy, possesses the most beautiful aboriginal' names—
sweet-sounding, poetic names that have begotten many a
bush bard, for the names are an inspiration in themselves.
The mighty White-man cannot fashion anything to equal
them ; and all lovers of Australia would like many of his
idiotic names struck off the map, and those of the Black-
man preserved.
Older and newer stories of Lord Salisbury, our
reputed Bendigo digger of the fifties —
A verbose bishop rose to address the House of Lords.
“ I will divide my speech under twelve heads,” he said.
Thereupon Salisbury begged to be allowed to interpose.
“ A friend of mine,” the Marquis said, “ was returning
home late one night, when, opposite St. Paul’s, he saw an
intoxicated man trying to ascertain the time on the big
clock there. Jhst then it began to strike, and slowly
tolled out twelve. The drunken man listened, looked
hard at the clock, and said: ‘ Confound you 1 why
could n’t you have said that all at once ?’ ” The bishop
took the hint.
Another. Salisbury often lunches at the Athenseum
Club, of which many bishops are members. Once, on a
rainy day, one of his secretaries, observing that his chief
was starting out to lunch at that club, rushed for an
umbrella and offered it to him. “ No, no,” said Lord
Salisbury; “I’ve lost too many in the Athenaeum. I
cannot trust these bishops.”
Yet another. After a heated discussion in his presence
on a current topic, an emphatic member of the party
exclaimed : “ I shan’t get any of you to agree with me ;
you are such a complete set of Philistines.” Salisbury
asked him whether he recollected what happened to the
Philistines. The reply was “ No.” “ They were smitten
with the jawbone of an ass,” said the Marquis.
This seems/lecisive. Kendall, however, does not
apply tbeHvord TJloola to “ the hero of his poem,”
but writes: “Uloola, behold him!”—as who
should say “Great Spirit of his tribe! behold
him, the last of his tribe ! ” And Kendall, of
course, as boy and man, passed years in the
Clarence R, district and its neighbourhood.
Man is to Woman in the solemn hour
As the great tree that bends across the stream,
Whose likeness on her breast has been her dream
Since that first twilight broken by the shower.
So the great roots that suck her moisture forth
Are as the kisses that he showers at dawn,
And from her body as he draws his worth
So his fair likeness in her soul is born.
And as the pollard tree, decayed and sere,
Sinks back to earth and crumbles where he stood.
New saplings rise, new youth and strength appear,
And once again the maiden stream is woo’d.
Oli, how her beauties in all times that are,
Lie fair revealed unto the morning star !
Louisa S. Baker, greatly , daring-, in The Thrush.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mates of Mine.
Brown as a wallaby, lithe as a whip,
Wisp of red silk on his sun-withered neck,
Thong on his shoulder, and oath on his lip ;
Every week putting two pound on his cheque,
Tough as a bull, and as ready for battle ;
“ Gor' bli’ me ! ”
Cattle !
Dusty and sulky, and down-at-the-mouth,
Torn of the legging, and worn of the spur ;
Slugging along in the heel of the drouth,
Cursing his loitering escort of cur ;
Backward and forward and back, half-asleep;
“ Blast ’em all! ”
Sheep /
ENCORE VERSE.
Bent of the shoulder, and lean of the thigh,
Loud in his dressing, and flash in his walk ;
Horse-shoe of silver to fasten his tie,
Sprinkling of “blanky” to garnish his talk ;
Bow-legged and bumptious and common and
coarse,
“ S’ help me Bob 1 ”
Horse I
U.K.
WILL OGILVIE.
Come Back to the Bush!
I’m what they call a “ solid man,”
I’ve made a decent pile;
So I brought my folks to London,
And we’ve settled down in style.
We wear clothes that don’t quite suit us,
Go to balls, and shows, and plays,
And we’re striking out for happiness
In various kinds of ways.
But a Voice keeps on calling me back
To the bush and the wallaby-track,
To the home in the clearing,
The sheep and the shearing —
The voice keeps on saying, “ Come back / ”
We’re living in Belgravia,
In the midst of Fashion’s whirl.
On my left there lives a marquis,
And right opposite an earl.
We’ve a kerridge and a coachman,
And a footman dressed in plush,
Whose calves stick out so prominent
They make my daughters blush.
But the Voice keeps on saying, “ Come back
To the bush and the wallaby-track,
Where the bright sun is glowing
And cattle are lowing,
Come back to your freedom, come back! ”
My wife she holds “ receptions ”
While my daughters study “art,”
And my son he drives a tandem,
Though his father druv a cart.
We have dinner in the evening,
And we always dress to dine ;
We eat strange food with foreign names,
And wash it down with wine.
Yet, the Voice keeps on calling me back
To the bush and the wallaby-track,
Where the wool-bales are packing,
And stockwhips are cracking,
It whispers for ever —“ Come back / ”
And the voice keeps calling, calling
Till I see the iron-bark ridge,
Till I scent the gum-logs burning
In the gully near the bridge;
I can hear the teamsters swearing
As they drive the bullock-dray,
And my spirit’s branding cattle
In the paddocks far away.
And the Voice keeps on saying, “ Come back
To the bush and the wallaby-track ! ”
To the parrots and ’possums
And sweet wattle-blossoms,
It keeps on inviting me back.
And my heart seems crushed and stifled
’Mid the teeming city’s rush,
And my eager soul is panting
For the freedom of the bush ;
And I feel that though I wander
Where illustrious feet have trod,
I’m near the world’s great throbbing heart,
But I’m not so close to God.
And the Voice keeps on saying, “ Come back
To the bush and the wallaby-track —
To the bush, with its sadness,
Its grandeur and gladness,
Its space and its freedom. Come back ! ”

BRISBANE, Q.
We are often asked if Ruberoid will burn
readily ? Here's your answer in three words —
“would not burn." Don't think for a moment
however that it's fire proof. That’s not so,
but it won't burn readily, not as readily as
wood for instance, and it's insured by the
Fire Underwriters’ Association at I/- % more
than they ask for a slate roof.
“WOULD NOT BURN.”

SATURDAY, JANUARY 11, 1902.
Our Argentine Rival.
Nowadays the Argentine spectre is the one
which looms up most persistently before our
primary producer—the man who tears raw
material off the earth in one shape or another,
and sends it to England and elsewhere be-
cause we have no large manufacturing popu-
lation to consume it locally. Argentina has
been a coming spectre for so long that people
began to doubt if it would ever really arrive.
But it certainly has arrived. The rival is no
very large one, comparatively speaking, and
as a matter of mere acreage. Even including
its recent annexation of three-fourths of
Patagonia (much of which new territory is
sodden and frost-bitten and of doubtful
value) the Argentine republic is only a little
over one-third the area of Australia and
Tasmania, and its population is only a little
larger than that of the Commonwealth. But,
all the same, it has now 5,000,000 more
sheep, 13,000,000 more cattle and 3,000,000
more horses than the Commonwealth pos-
sesses. It has almost twice as much land
under cultivation, as the Commonwealth
can show-, and it is making very
respectable progress in manufactures. For
the 10 months to 31st October, 1901,
Argentina exported to England twice as
many carcases of sheep and lambs as the
Commonwealth did, and three times as much
frozen beef, and its products command
higher prices than ours. It is now far ahead
of us in wool, and it is starting to grow
sugar on a large scale. The Argentine has a
better-watered country than ours, and he
has less freight to pay in order to place his
goods on the English market. It is largely
through his competition that the price of all
our agricultural and pastoral products has
dropped so heavily, and this great competi-
tor appears to have room for a vast amount
of further expansion. Under these cir-
cumstances there is something extra ridi-
culous about the stereotyped advice of
the Foreign-trade theorist—to trust to our
“natural industries,” to depend almost
wholly on dragging agricultural and pastoral
products off a barren and drought-cursed
land, and on selling them in the most distant
and most glutted market on earth, and to
make no effort to grow a local manufacturing
population which will supply the farmer
with a big market here—not 16,000 miles
away.
* • * *
The Fat Man of this country naturally
gets up at this emergency to state that the
rising of Argentina to the position of a
dangerous and possibly disastrous rival is
owing to private enterprise, private railway-
management, the absence of a Labor Party
in politics, the absence of an eight-hour
movement, and the fact that the Argentine
doesn’t strike very often, works considerable
hours for moderate wages, and has no concilia-
tion or arbitration court or similar institution.
To some extent, of churse, the Fat Man’s
case looks right. A country where man
works ten hours for a low wage can pro-
duce more cheaply than, and undersell,
the one where he works eight hours for a
decent wage. But the bulk of the trade
which Australia is losing, and which Argen-
tina is gaining, is a trade in articles which
are produced by miserably cheap labor even
in Australia. Nobody suspects the cocky,
or the cocky’s employee, or the man who
works for the insolvent pastoralist, of re-
ceiving a large and regal remuneration for a
small day’s work. His pay has never been
fixed by Act of Parliament; no Conciliation
or Arbitration Court has ever reached him—
in the Commonwealth, at all events ; and
the last time a small section of him went on
strike was ten long years ago. As regards
the railway question, about half, or rather
more than half, the Argentine railways are
in private hands, and they carry goods, on
the whole, pretty cheaply, not because private
enterprise can run things any more cheaply
than State management, but because the
companies have been aided and pauper-
ised by enormous State subsidies, and
because, in many cases, the Govern-
ment was diddled into a disastrous
agreement whereby it guaranteed a dividend.
Australia could reduce its freights 25 or 50
or 75 per cent, in the sarnq fashion—by the
general revenue making good the loss. Still,
the Argentine railways, as an example of the
incapacity of private enterprise to stand
without State assistance, or, at least, of its
rapacious desire to lean on the State at every
possible opportunity, are certainly all there.
* * * *
The advantages of better soil, better rain-
fall and a far shorter sea-voyage to carry its
products to the European market, are suf-
ficient in themselves to account for the
Argentine boom. But, in addition to this,
that land, despite revolutions and civil and
foreign wars, never had quite the same in-
sensate gang of Fat Men whose foreign bor-
rowing policy has ruined Australia. The
Argentines certainly did hair-raising things
in the way of finance, but, after all,
they only owe about £80,000,000 of
external debt, against the Common-
wealth’s £200,000,000 or thereabouts. There
is nearly £4,000,000 less going out every year
to pay interest to the foreign Cohen,
and that makes a material difference in the
drain on the community’s resources. The
Argentine, with his rudimentary Spanish
ideas of finance, raised most of his loans
locally, much of them by compulsory issues
of paper money. The circulating medium is
mostly paper, and the paper doesn’t cost
per cent, interest per annum. Argentina
doesn’t keep £20,000,000 of borrowed gold
lying constantly idle in its banks at a dead
loss of about £700,000 a year in interest, and
a further large amount in circulation at a
further loss in interest, as Australia does,
merely for the sake of being able to say that
it lives up to the British theories on the
subject of a gold currency. Its paper dollar,
nominally worth 45., is depreciated to less
than 25., and Argentina takes the matter
philosophically. When an Argentine sells
£lOOO worth of produce in England it fetches
him in over £2OOO in his own depreciated
currency, and that booms the export trade.
When another Argentine buys £lOOO worth
of goods in England he has to pay over £2OOO
of his paper currency for them, and that
helps to keep down imports, and assists local
industries along. During the last seven
years —the years of the Argentine boom—
exports have risen from being £2,000,000
more than imports to being £14,000,000
more than imports, and by reason of the
difference A rgenHna is beginning to reduce
its foreign debt a little and hold up its head
again among the nations.
* * • *
When Argentina struck a really bad time
after its borrowing boom it didn’t borrow
more money at any cost in order to keep
itself apparently solvent—it simply went
temporarily insolvent. The result* of this
easy, but dishonest, process was that things
came round quickly, and it was soon able to
resume payment. Australia would also have
cotne round quiekly in ’93 if it had saved a
few millions a year by partially suspending
payment of its interest bill. Argentine
credit received such a shock through these
happenings that it became practically im-
possible to float more foreign loans, and that
saved it from getting deeper in the mire.
It didn’t raise loans at great expense to meet
its deficits in the bad times so long as it could
issue more paper. It had no British traditions
to live up to, and it didn’t borrow merely
to support its beastly moral dignity. Its
paper money may be dirty and depreciated,
but a debtor country with a gold circulation,
and with big gold reserves in its banks, is
making a stupendous annual loss in interest,
and the dirty paper money involves no loss
in interest. Its depreciated condition helps,
indirectly, to protect Argentine industries.
Meanwhile, Australia tries to live up to
Britain’s ideas without Britain’s money.
Because Britain has a gold circulation of
its own, Australia must needs spend some-
thing like£l,ooo,oooayearinterest in borrow-
ing the money to have a gold circulation also.
And Australia is going downhill. Argentina,
having pulled through its worst troubles by
a certain amount of cheerful dishonesty, and
an off-hand disregard of lofty traditions
when it couldn’t afford to keep them up, is
distinctly on the up-grade. Australia is
ready to make any sacrifice in the way of
borrowing and faking the public accounts in
order that it may keep faith with the public
creditor by paying the interest regularly.
The blessed result is that it is able to borrow
more money, and get into worse difficulties.
* * * *
On the top of everything else, Argentina
has the immense advantage of being an
independent State—a shabby one it is true —
and not a mere colony. When a man makes
a huge pile in Australia, and acquires much
Australian property, he generally lights out
for London to look for a shoddy title, and to
put his sons in the British army, and to
marry his daughters into the British aris-
tocracy. Then his income goes out of the
country, and his fortune, invested in Aus-
tralia, becomes as much a debt owing by
Australia to England as if Australia had
borrowed the money from England and spent
it in beer. When £30,000 a year of Aus-
tralian rents begin to go to England because
the owner of the property has gone there to
look for a poor, mean, twopenny decoration
and be a hanger-on in British society, the
burden on this country is just the same
as if it had borrowed another million
at 3 per cent, and had to send
£30,000 a year interest to England and
it hasn’t the benefit and advantage of spend-
ing the million. The fact that London is
the centre of the Empire—the place where
the unpatriotic Henniker Heatons and
Samuel Wilsons and Daniel Coopers go,
and the place from which we receive many
inane small - beer cablegrams about their
doings—has cost this country probably some-
thing like a hundred millions already in
absentees’ incomes, and there is no visible
end to the drain. The wealthy Argentine
has no “mother-country ” to go to save his
own, and the saving thus effected makes up
for a vast amount of misgovernment and
corruption. This Commonwealth is trying to
live by fierce competition with a State that has
greater natural advantages, fewer scruples,
and fewer silly imported traditions, and
which, despite the wild-cat nature of many
Spanish-American Governments, is, in some
respects, far more wisely governed than
Australia. And as Britain, at all events,
has no sentimental ideas about giving a
commercial preference to the country which
sent all the four-and-sixpenny contingents,
the situation grows interesting.
The Money Power.
No man can earn a million of money honestly.
By the rarest of accidents, or by the luck of birth,
he may become possessed of millions without
actually wrecking other men’s fortunes or robbing
other men of their savings. But the exceptional
instances of honest millionairism are so rare that
it is a social axiom that because a man is a mil-
lionaire, therefore he is dishonest as a trader or
unscrupulous as a speculator. The average pluto-
crat is a pirate by profession. His accumulation
of millions is the sum of the losses of thousands of
his fellow-men. The wreck of a railway company,
the gerrymandering of a mining enterprise, the
cornering of a staple, the watering of a stock—
those are the operations out of which men make
millions, and in each case it is by strewing their
path with wrecks. Out of a port sail a hundred
rich merchantmen, and one pirate ; the haven of
prosperity is reached by the pirate alone. He has
made his millions—and his wrecks.
* * * *
When wealth tends to accumulate in the hands
of a few, it is a dangerous national symptom.
When to the hands of those few flows not only the
wealth, but the political and social power of a
community, it-is'a mark of doom. As an ex-
crescence on a nation, a superficial cancer, a
Elutocracy may be tolerable —unsightly, noxious,
ut not actually mortal. When it reaches to the
heart, invades the executive functions of National
Life, the nation must in time perish. For money
has no patriotism and no conscience. It regards
not duty, but dividends ; and its one aim is loot.
It is cosmopolitan, brushing asideall considerations
of race and religion. Love of country, courage,
justice, honor, mercy—as words it may juggle
with, as principles it knows nothing of. In the
pursuit of a high percentage it is relentlessly
savage; in the fear of a loss contemptibly
timorous.
* * » ♦
It is because every conclusion of reason and
every page of history, every result of deductive or
inductive reasoning points to a sure doom for the
nation which is dominated by a plutocracy, that
the outlook for England is at the present moment
so gloomy. She, conspicuous among all the
nations, is ruled by the money power. Her cities
are the offices, her fields the playgrounds, her
Cabinets the puppets of the world’s millionaires
—British, German, French, South African,
American millionaires. Even her memorable
churches are crowded by the world’s money-
changers. Millionaire Rhodes buys the policy
of a British Government on the Egyptian question
with a money gift. The same man of money,
with his German coadjutor Beit, embarks another
Government on the S’African war. Millionaires
from everywhere and anywhere dominate London
society and London journalism—such men as
the renegade Astor, Carnegie (whose iron works
in America flowed with the blood of
his laborers), Boss Croker (the Tammany
thief), Rothschild (the financial friend
°f. the present King). Sometime British
millionaire Hooley figures as the donor of com-
munion plate to the established English Church ;
American millionaire, Pierpont Morgan, pre-
sents to it an electric-lighting service for its chief
cathedral Millionaire Lipton represents the
Empire in international sport. In politics,
religion, society, art, sport, the millionaire is
supreme. It is not necessary for him to be a
British millionaire ; any sort of millionaire can
rule in England, devastate her fields for deer
parks, degrade her social relations, direct her
armies and navies to unjust murders, and desecrate
her churches. The typical English squire is Boss
Croker, the typical English society king Astor,
the typical English cabinet-maker Rhodes, the
typical English hierarch Pierpont Morgan. The
foreign millionaire rules in every department of
British life ; to British men is left only the
drudgery of obeying, of fighting, and wage-earn-
ing for their moneyed masters.
• * * •
And it is on this gold-cankered nation that Aus-
tralia is asked to pattern Tier life, to model her
politics, to fashion her national ideals. She
cannot do it and live.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Our Dear Fellow-Subject.
Dear Bulletin.—Bruce Smith’s United Aus-
tralia, which started off with such good inten-
tions, and has lately become violently Tory,
prints Judas Chamberlain’s speech to the
colonial Premiers whilst Record-Reigning in 1897
as a reason why we should still keep our arms wide
open to our dear friends and fellow-subjects, Ram
Chutney, Tin Yow, and co. Among Chamber-
lain’s reasons are the following, with italic
embellishments by Bruce Smith :
. . . We quite sympathise with the determination of
the white inhabitants of these colonies, which are in
comparatively close proximity to millions and hundreds of
millions of Asiatics, that there shall not be an influx of
people alien in civilisation, alien in religion, alien in cus-
toms, whose influx, moreover, would most seriously
interfere with the legitimate rights of the existing labor
population. An immigration of that kind must, I quite
understand, in the interests of the colonies, be prevented
at all hazards, and we shall not offer any opposition to
proposals intended with that object; but we ask you also
to bear in mind the traditions of the Empire, which
makes no distinction in favor of or against race or color ;
and to exclude, by reason of their color, or by reason of
their race, all Her Majesty’s Indian subjects, or even a f l
Asiatics, would be an act so offensive to them- that it
The Cardinal’s Capital.
At the opening of St. Francis’ bazaar Cardinal
Moran said:—
I observe that there are some people who desire to have
a capital established at Tumut and elsewhere, but for my
part I look forward to seeing it established at Eden-
Bombala, and will not be satisfied until it is placed there.
The Bulletin speaks gently—
Though it might take offence,
And speak irreverently
About your Eminence.
The fact is that its staff is
At very great expense
For building sites : the laugh is
Still with your Eminence.
The sites are at Bombala ;
The staff makes solemn vow,
By Odin and by Allah,
Someone shall suffer now.
“And now”—the staff is speaking—
“ We paid him, days and nights,
The Agent who went seeking
For pretty building-sites.
He found them—oh, he found them —
And they were bright and fair,
A green ring was around them,
Above them—golden air.
And everybody will side
With people in the know—
A church was on the hillside,
A convent down below.
So Editor, and Foreman,
And Little Office Boy,
And Office Poet—poor man—
Planked down their coin with joy.
We were a cheerful party,
Baptists and Anglicans,
And Papists blithe and hearty,
And Presbyterians.
We might have —yes, by thunder —
Achieved a loot immense,
Gone braceletted with plunder—
But for your Eminence.
You said that at Bombala
The capital should be —
By Odin and by Allah
That very thing said we.
You little know what Fate meant
When we should thus agree ;
Your Cardinalesque statement
Is charged with Destiny.
The Manager looks starkly
On bards in his employ;
The Editor glares darkly
Upon the Office-Boy.
And in our prideful office
No man has confidence;
No man will trust another—
Your fault, O Eminence !
You spoke your speech divinely—
The keen old saints spoke thus—
You praised Bombala finely—
But you have ruined Us.
We see a black crowd raving
Around in Wynyard-square,
And Dill Macky a-waving
An Orange flag in air.
‘Make it,’ the crowd is shrieking,
‘ In Hell or Booligal;
But never in Bombala—
The Cardinal’s Capital.’”
CREEVE ROE.


would be most painful, I am quite certain, to Her
Majesty to have to sanction it. Consider what has been
Drought to your notice during your visit to this country.
The United Kingdom owns as its brightest and greatest
dependency that enormous Empire of India, with
300,000,000 of subjects, who are as loyal to the Crown as
you are yourselves, and among them are hundreds and
thousands of men who are every wliit as civilised, as we
are ourselves, who are, if that be anything, better born, in
the sense that they have older traditions and older
families, who are men of wealth, men of cultivation,
men of distinguished valor, men who have brought whole
armies and placed them at the service of the Queen, and
have, in times of great difficulty and trouble—such, for
instance, as on the occasion of the Indian Mutiny— saved
the Empire by their loyalty. I say—you, who have seen
all this, cannot be willing to put upon’ those men a slight
which I think is absolutely unnecessary for your purpose,
and which would be calculated to provoke ill-feeling, dis-
content, irritation, and would be most unpalatable to the
feelings, not only of Her Majesty, but of all Her people.
And why, for the two hundred dozenth time,
doesn’t the brown Indian fellow-subject have the
same self-governing rights as the white English-
man, Canadian, Australian, Tasmanian, and
Maorilander, if he is as loyal as they, and as
civilised ? Why doesn’t he have a vote and a Par-
liament ? Why isn’t he allowed to be a general or
a colonel in the Imperial army, or even the Indian
army, instead of being confined to the inferior
grades of the service? Why does the Bi-itish
Government treat him with contempt, by reason
of his color and his race, seeing that he is such a
beautiful character, and seeing it would break
Chamberlain’s heart to put a slight on him in any
way, or regard him in any way as if he was of lower
degree than the white Britisher? If Chamber-
lain treated the Englishman as the Indian
fellow-subject is treated—well, the English people
would hang Chamberlain. They beheaded
Charles I, but beheading has gone out of fashion.
—X.B.

The Hindu as Harvester.
The wisdom of the Federal Labor party in agree-
ing to accept a system of alien immigration
restriction which was somewhat lacking in brutal
candor, but had the merit of coming into force at
once, is shown very clearly by the appearance of
the Hindu as a harvester in the mallee districts
of Victoria. At the township of Beulah there are
now from 100 to 150 Hindus in regular employ-
ment on the farms; and other townships in the
Victorian mallee are almost as badly affected by
the colored labor curse. The Hindu works from
early morn to dewy eve, on two “ meals ” a day—
the meals being wheaten pancakes occasionally
relieved by curried rice ; he sleeps anywhere and
accepts a wage which makes white labor competi-
tion impossible. So the white laborer tramps the
country idle, whilst the swart, sweated Hindu
toils for leave to live in the cowshed on a diet of
Johnny cake, and the hope of acquiring, after
years of patience, a fortune of £l4 11s. 2|d, and
retiring to his own country to live a life of dignified
ease. The head man of the Hindu workers at
Beulah stated to a newspaper interviewer that
life there was so pleasant to the Hindu mind
“that he had apprised his countrymen in India of
this new field of labor, and as a consequence, an
influx of them may be expected in the near
future.” But if the Federal Alien Immigration
Restriction Act is administered fairly (and the
Labor party may be expected to see to that, even
if the Cabinet which includes C. O. Kingston
should incline to be wobbly) this influx will not
be. Every Hindu will be politely refused per-
mission to land because of his lamentable and
unfortunate ignorance of the language of William
Shakespeare and the philosophical terms of
Herbert Spencer. And, as the saddened men
of color turn back to India, white Australians can
congratulate themselves that they are able to
stem the black tide, even though it is with a polite
pretence; and that they are not obliged to allow
the Hindu harvester to enter in hordes whilst
arguing with Mr. Chamberlain about the right
to reject a colored fellow-subject with a rude
remark instead of a gentle equivocation.

‘ The Bulletin’s” Foreign-Trade
Correspondent.
The Bulletin receives this letter from an old and
valued but needlessly excited correspondent:
Re Bulletin remarks on Westralian Worker (B.
21/12/’Ol, page 9, cols. 3 and 4): — . . . “ how we are to
have . . . fair play to all industries unless we keep out
cheap “ foreign goods by a Protective tariff.” The foreign
traders don’t believe that a Protective tariff does the
workers any good. And they think more revenue should
be derived from the land, and less from the Customs.
They think a Protective tariff is an exploded fallacy, and
are lost in wonder that. The Bulletin should still cling to
it —amazed that so astute a paper should not see through
the highly artistic method of plucking the geese without
making them sing out. Foreign traders are foreign
traders because they don’t see any means of profitably
getting rid of our surplus produce otherwise than by
means of foreign trade.
The handy and obvious gibe that when the valued
correspondent becomes as astute as The Bulletin
he will also be a Protectionist may be taken as
read. ■ The above letter shows the Foreign-trader
at his own valuation, as the unselfish person who
isn’t seeking his own profit in any way, but is
solely concerned in finding a profitable market for
this country’s surplus products. And if it was the
fact that the countries from which we buy largely
under a Foreign-trade policy were necessarily the
countries which buy largely from us, and that
they buy from us because, under a blessed Foreign-
trade system, we buy from them, then the argu-
ment wouldn’t have the big leak it now possesses.
But taking Coghlan’s figures for 1899, it appears
that we sold over £6,100,000 worth of goods
in that year to France and Belgium, and
only took from them £900,000 worth. Also,
we bought nearly £400,000 worth from Scan-
dinavia, and only sold to Scandinavia £BO worth,
and so on. The list is dotted all over with
similar discrepancies. Also, according to the
latest returns, the United States bought only
£174,000,000 worth in the year from the rest of the
world, yet sold it £283,000,000 worth ; the States
have done this sort of thing for years, and have
paid off their external debts with the differences,
and are now beginning to be great lenders of
money to other nations. A country doesn’t buy
from another because the other buys from it; it
buys because the other country has something to
sell that it wants. The Foreign-trade principle
doesn’t improve the outlet for our products in the
least. N.S.W. certainly had Freetrade (of a
kind), and so had England. But Jones, the
wool-spinner of Bradford, didn’t buy N.S.W. wool
out of gratitude because N.S.W. took ironwork
from Brown, of Middlesboro’, whom he had
most likely never heard of. Probably '99 per
cent, of the French manufacturers who take so
much of our wool don’t even know whether
we buy anything from France or not—their
funeral is that there is a market in France for
woollen goods, and our wool is good for the pur-
pose. Britain buys from the United States six
times as much as it sells to the States ; and it sells
to Java eight times as much as it buys from Java,
and it is so all the world over. The boss Foreign-
trader, who is generally an importer pure and
simple, and doesn’t care a straw how. the export
trade goes, is given to professing that he doesn’t
want a system which will enable him to fill the
country with foreign boots because he lives by it
but because, when we purchase boots from Brown
of Nottingham, Jones, the Bradford wool-spinner,
gets to hear of it, and buys our wool out of grati-
tude, when he would otherwise rather do without
wool than have anything to do with us. It is a
tender little legend of pati'iotism, good-feeling,
and tearful gratitude, but it is too foolish to bear
inspection.

“ Leading ” Clergymen and Other
Clergymen.
A great effort is being made in England to dis-
count the importance of the protest against the
present stage of the Boer war signed by over
5000 clergymen. It is explained that whilst these
clergymen, representing presumably the bulk of
their 5000 congregations, signed a solemn protest
against the war, a gz-eater number of parsons
didn’t sign it, and this greater number included
all the “leading men.” The last part of the
statement may well be believed. The “leading
parson ” is generally the parson who goes with the
crowd, the fat, shiny-faced parson with a gift
of eloquence, or a capacity for small-beer
humor, and a profound respect for person
ages. These sort have formed the Sa’nhedrin
of every sect since the days when Pontius
Pilate was Procurator in Judaea. The really
earnest parson rarely, by any chance, be-
comes a “leading” man. But the argument
that the weight of a petition’ is to be gauged by
the number of persons who do not sign it is of
value in showing how desperate is the effort on
the pro-Gore side to muster up even a semblance
of a reason for its attitude. That a parson signed
the petition in question showed that he had come
to a stern conclusion —probably with a great deal
of pain and reluctance—that in this matter his
country was in the wrong, and must be told so,
despite the contumely and insults of Jingoes.
That a parson did not sign it might show that he
approved thoroughly of the farm-burning, and
the concentration camps, and the hangings,
and so on; on the other hand, it might
show that he was too easy-going or lazy
to bother about the painful issue ; or that he.
agreed with the petition but hadn’t the courage to
sign it; or that he had made up his mind to sign
it, and had put the matter off until it was too late :
matters which can be done any day are often done
no day. In short, the parson who didn’t sign
proves nothing conclusively. The only possible
answer to the declaration of conscience by those
who did sign would be a memorial by an equal
number stating that they cordially approved of
Ketchener and all his works. And even that
would prove nothing that is not already known.
There is no claim from the side of humanity in
this controversy that England is united in opposi-
tion to the war ; it is fully recognised that what
Ibsen called “ the damned, compact, foolish
majority ” is still pro-Gore. What is claimed is
that there is a vast, intelligent, patriotic minority,
a minority led by distinguished English states-
men, philosophers, soldiers, and clergymen,
utterly opposed to the disastrous Chamberlain
policy. There can be no explaining away of the
clergymen’s petition to make it of no value in
relation to that fact.

The Fountain of Honor.
‘‘There is reason to believe,” says a daily
newspaper, “that the State Governments will
offer a stout resistance to the Commonwealth
Government should that authority attempt to ar-
rogate to itself the sole right of nominations for
titular distinction, as it has been suggested it will
do.” For once The Bulletin is heart and soul
with the State Governments against this effort at
trespass on the part of the Federal administration.
The State Governments have had to relegate to the
Australian legislature much of the really important
and national work of governing. They have Mien
left, certainly, with many great duties and oppor-
tunities. A State Government can still establish
a sinking-fund for the repayment of the public
debt, a public works fund for the construction
of future works out of revenue, a State Bank,
a decent Local Government system, a depart-
ment for the supervision of exports, a sane and
honest system for the resumption of large estates,
a State Life Assurance Office, and many
other things. It can tax absentees, it can
abolish the deficit, it can rebuild Sydney,
it can obliterate its own idiotic Upper
House. But, as nobody dreams of doing these
things, the State Government is now, by its own
choice, an obscure potterer among drains and
J’s.P., and the business of urging the Imperial
Government to decorate stodgy citizens with un-
meaning gauds should be left among the drains
and J’s.P., where it naturally belongs. Exactly
what share Australian politicians have in recom-
mending the shedding on this country of C.M.G’s.,
K.C.M.G’s. and other titular distinctions (of
value in distinguishing commonplace politicians
from others) we have not the means of knowing ;
but, whether small or great, it should certainly
be kept in the hands of the State Governments
in preference to handing it over to the Federal
authority. The transference would involve an
infection at the heart of the nation’s political
system, with the truckling meanness, the petty
parochialism, the slobbery snobbishness, and the
disloyalty to Australia which mark the intrigues
for English “titular distinction.” It is better to
keep the poison at the limbs. In future, perhaps,
under a sound system of Local Government, the
K.C.M.G. business may be still more fittingly
relegated to the municipal councils. Then the
suburban mayor will recommend his town clerk
for the C.M.G., and his nuisances inspector to be
a Privy Councillor, and titular distinctions will
have reached their proper level.

The Helot Contingent.
Premier Barton, as a sort of balm to his con-
science, smarting at the thought of a broken pro-
mise, is surrounding the Henniker Heaton
Federalcohentingent with asmuch of tardiness and
reluctance and mean condition as possible. Evi-
dently his heart is not in the work, and by some
curious process of reasoning he takes comfort from
the thought that if he is sending—at Henniker
Heaton’s dictation—another cohentingent, he is
at any rate sending it ungraciously and surlily, as
if it were any relief to the Boers to have their
farms burned down with a scowl instead of
with a cheerful whoop. And the painful
efforts of Premier Barton to show that his heart
is not in the work, and that he is now kicking
himself vigorously because he had not the courage
to absolutely repudiate the Heaton impertinence,
are operating to make this last cohentingent
about the meanest and most degrading of the lot.
Australia is not to pay a penny piece to the men
—out of borrowed or any other money ; she is not
to find so much as a shirt for them, though she
will pocket the profits made out of fitting them up
at England’s expense; she expressly and speci-
fically repudiates all responsibility for finding
wooden legs and glass eyes for those who are
maimed —they must look to England for their
glass eyes and wooden legs. There is absolutely
no pretence of an “Imperial spirit” prompting
this contingent; the men go to fight England’s
battles, not “the Empire’s,” and are told so
with brutal directness. They occupy precisely
the.same position as the German mercenaries re-
cruited to fight against the American colonists,
and probably they will mostly be actuated by
somewhat the same motive. Life as a soldier
promises 355. a week with rations and clothes, and
the prospect of loot, whilst life as a civilian
doesn’t hold out half those material advant-
ages. When the Australian States sent con-
tingents which they equipped themselves, and
paid for (out of borrowed money) themselves,
there was some show of “ patriotism ” and “Im-
perialism ” —misguided sentiments, perhaps, in
the circumstances, but still having some semblance
of dignity. Now, Australia is put in the same
position as India, a place where helot soldiers may
be recruited for England’s army, soldiers in
whose fate we expressly repudiate all responsi-
bility, and to whom we don’t even hold out the
prospect of a billet on the trams when they have
helped to save the Empire.

Calico Jimmy on the Tariff.
An editor of Sydney D.T. was once called to
accouzit by an indignant theatrical manager who
complained that on his reviving a certain melo-
drama it had been condemned as just trash
—badly conceived and badly executed—whilst
three months before, when lie produced the same
play with the same caste, it had been described
as “from a literary point of view admirable,
strong in dialogue, effective in plot.” The
weary editor finally murmured—“ Well, we’ve
said the thing is utterly bad, and we’ve said
it is admirable. I don’t see how you can accuse
us of having any prejudice on the subject! ” The
same sort of “lack of prejudice” marks the
paper’s comments on the tariff, which change
with each succeeding day. Quotes :
Dec. 3 : “The actual experience of the tariff for the
months of October and November indicates that it would,
if passed in its present form, yield a million and a half
more than the Govt, estimated for the current year, and
between two and three millions more than the estimate
for what George Turner calls a normal year. These
are miscalculations worthy of the financial capacity of
this Ministry of lawyers.”
Deo. 14 (after changes which the D.T. generally de-
scribed as likely to bring in more revenue) : “ There is
no possibility of the tariff yielding what had been ex-
pected from it.”
Dec. 24 : “ Although the tariff isbeing very considerably
cut down it does not follow that the revenue will be
materially less than that predicted by George Turner.”
Dec. 30 : The coming conference of Premiers can hardly
pass without reference to the estimates framed by
George Turner, and the manner in which these estimates
have already been jeopardised by the tariff changes in-
introduced.
Yes ! No ! ! Yes !!! No !!!!
M'Culloch, Victoria’s Minister of Public Works,
whose efforts to grab a title are responsible for the
Commonwealth contingent for hen-roost robbing
in South Africa, is the individual who plumes
himself on the fact that he threatened to leave
George Turner’s Cabinet if that shabbily-
clothed politician did not send off the first Vic.
contingent. He probably thought he was due for
a title over that piece of miserable business. It
didn’t come, and then he got himself appointed
chairman of the Cabinet committee that made
preparations for the ducal grovel. The com-
mittee developed a charwoman’s capacity for
muddle, and was a conspicuous failure, and
the whole of the work had to be borne by
a Parliamentary clerk. Peacock and Turner
pushed McCulloch’s little barrow to some
extent, but still the silly bauble did not turn
up. M'Culloch has long been burning to send
another contingent; it is the easiest way
nowadays to catch the eye of Chamberlain.
For months past he has been urging Forrest
and Barton to offer more troops to carry on the
wonderful capture of poultry in South Africa, but
those wobbly Ministers held out against the idea.
Then McCulloch invoked the aid of his pal,
David Syme. Letters appeared regularly in the
Age from the pen of McCulloch. Everything
failed until Henniker Heaton put in an appear-
ance. McCulloch at once froze on to him, and
Heaton sent a cablegram to Chamberlain,
setting out that McCulloch had informed him
Australia was bursting to send another contingent.
McCulloch’s authority to speak for Australia
may be judged from the fact that Heaton had
to spend half a sov. explaining who McCulloch
was.

It has been proved conclusively that the Second
N.S.W. Contingent was only promised 4s. 6d. per
day in all (3s. 4d. per day from N.S.W., and
Is. 2d. from Britain), and as the fact that the
First Contingent got ss. Bd. per day by an error
has nothing to do with the case, Premier See
finally refuses to give the second batch of farm-
burners anything more than the price agreed
upon. Wherefore some individuals are now ti-ying
a supremely silly and impudent bluff by threaten-
ing legal proceedings. And Walsh, M.L.A., is re-
ported as having said that “if it is intended to
deprive returned soldiers of the pay they expected
to get the Government deserves condemnation.”
Why does it deserve condemnation ? The State
spends immense sums on police, gaols, and law
officers for the express purpose of depriving people
of what they expected to get, and very often
when a man is coming out of a window with less
than half of what he expected to get, he has even
that taken from him by the “ cop ” outside. Yet
nobody condemns the Government for depriving
these persons of what they expected to get.
♦
The Victorian Assembly allowed Peacock’s
Ministry to dribble into recess. There is a sort of
understanding that he is to reconstruct before
meeting Parliament again. After a year’s experi-
ence of a silly like Gurk, a mental loafer like
Trenwith, a dull mediocrity like M'Culloch,
and the rest, it can easily be imagined that poor
Peacock is only too anxious to reconstruct.
While one of the attacks against the Government
was being organised there was much talk of
Ministers handing in their resignations to their
chief. They actually signed a document setting
out their willingness to do so ; but that is all
ancient history, and the men who rushed to tell
Peacock that lie could have their portfolios are
now the ones who will be most difficult of remo-
val. For the past month three at least of the
Cabinet have been holding little conferences on
their own in order to formulate a scheme for
frustrating the reconstruction idea. They see that
if they are thrown out of the Ministry they will
be politically dead. “Let us all go down to-
gether” is their cry. Of course Peacock could
hand in the joint Ministerial resignation, but if he
does that he is politically damned. Probably he
will meet Parliament in May with the same old
incompetent crowd, and probably he will go under
about the second week of the new session. Then,
even if he reconstructs, there is always the
question whether any Ministry led by Peacock
could be competent—whether the head isn’t just
as foolish as the tail. And incompetence at the
thick end of the animal is a vastly more serious
matter than a similar affliction at the thin end.
A profound inability to understand their true
position appears to afflict some State politicians
and newspapers nowadays. Within the last few
days we have heard of State Premiers who said,
or are credited with having said, that if the
Federal Government didn’t move in the matter of
despatching another contingent, they would per-
sonally take the matter up—apparently not realis-
ing that any person outside of, or without the
authority of, the Federal Government, who goes
raising troops in the Commonwealth is just an
ordinary law-breaker, like any other drunk. And
the Brisbane Courier is still under the impres-
sion that, though the Kanaka Bill was passed by
the Australian Parliament, and though the
Queensland members were mostly in its favor,
still some kind of outrage was committed in not
postponing the Royal assent in order that the
State Government, which has no earthly
concern with the matter, had time to argue
the point and urge the Crown to overrule the
people’s representatives. The Courier, is quite
violent over this matter, and lashes out blindly
with its umbrella in all directions. Yet suppose
it had been a case of the Brisbane Legislature
passing a shindykit railway job by a huge
majority, and one or two Queensland electorates,
or a few municipal councils, demanding that the
royal assent should be withheld till they had time
to see Edward YII. about the matter! What
kind of reception would the Courier have given
to that modest proposal ?

One Sincook has been writing in an exultant
strain to Melbourne Age, pointing out that in
Victoria only one person out of every 1031 is in
gaol, while in N.S.W. one person in 721 occupies
that undesirable public building. The figures are
correct enough, but the person with a name like
an offence and a rooster would do well to say as
little as possible about it. The administration of
the law in N.S.W. is lax enough, but there is a
suspicion that in Victoria it is very much worse.
The push which terrorises Carlton would make a
considerable addition to tbe gaol population if it
was where it ought to be, but so long as
the J.P. fines it ss. it won’t do so. A certain
influential solicitor would have probably in-
creased the gaol population by one per
million for a good many years if the Crown
hadn’t dropped a large number of glaring
cases against him; as it is, his release will pre-
sently reduce the gaol population somewhat. If,
say, 100 of the notorious characters of ’93 had got
their deserts, and were still getting them, the
Vic. gaol population would go up to one in 920
right off. In Victoria, the slipshod J.P. has
powers which his N.S.W. brother doesn’t possess,
to release the offender without sentence, and lie
uses them so extensively—often for the purpose of
placating people who buy groceries from him —
that one-third of the persons accused in Victoria
are turned loose by the J.P., while only one-sixth
get away in that semi-official fashion in N.S.W.
There are two ways of keeping down the gaol
population—by not having criminals, or by having
them and not punishing them. Victoria," largely
by reason of its J.P., and to some small extent
by reason of the breakable character of Pentridge,
has got the second way very badly.

As a gaol the Geelong (Vic.) institution seems to
have some humorous features. Here is a list of
happenings there, printed by a Melbourne paper :
Prisoner Ramage assaults a sick wardsman in the
hospital; no punishment.
Ramage throws a candle at the Governor, and uses
obscene language on the 16th of October. Candle again
given to Ramage, but no punishment.
On the 4th of November, 1900, Ramage called warder
and Governor robbers ; no punishment.
Ramage throws his boot at the Governor ; no punish-
ment.
Ramage, at tobacco distribution, uses obscene language
to chief warder, but no notice taken.
Prisoners Gamble and Brown rang the alarm bell and
jumped the fence, but warders were ordered not to inter-
fere with them, as it only annoyed them.
Four prisoners carrying on unmentionable practices ;
a fight takes place between two of them, but they are
let off, to hush the matter up.
Prisoners Morgan, Walker, Hassett, Ramage, and
another partially intoxicated nearly every night for a
couple of months.
Geelong gaol is the place where two men have
lately committed suicide and others attempted it,
but which Inspector-General Evans says is
“ quite in its normal condition, though there was
bound to be a little grumbling among invalids in
confinement.” Evidently its great object in life
just now is to humor Ramage so that he won’t be
tempted to depart to a better world. Prisoner
Ramage seems to be essential to Geelong gaol’s
happiness.

From a sketch published in Sydney Telegraph,
it seems that the O’Sullivan contemplates erect-
ing as the new Sydney railway-station a building
which will combine all the salient features of the
Coliseum, St. Paul’s, the Kremlin, and a Yankee
sky-scraper in one magnificent, impressive, and
O’Sullivanesque whole. The cost of a decent
railway-station without any extraordinary frills
was originally estimated at £233,000. The O’Sul-
livan “ monumental work of stateliness and
beauty,” carrying out on lines of proper grandeur
the idea of Sydney Facing the Botany Boiling-
down Works, is reckoned to cost from £400,000 to
.£500,000. Of course, the money will come out of
loan votes, and, as the Railway Commissioners
are careful to explain that the new station will not
add a penny-piece to the railway revenue, it will
be of the co ientingentors’ tombstone order of
permanent and reproductive work. But, then, it
will show what a great country N.S. Wales is.
The question of exchange is one which greatly
excites the N.S.W. country’ civil servant. At
present the Crown Lands agents, C.’sP.S,, and
other public officials banished into the holes and
corners of the State have little prospect but that
of a transfer to a still more dreadful post than
the one occupied. They all dream of an exchange
to the metropolis, but the dream is seldom
realised. The wail from the wilderness evokes
little save barren sympathy, for the city officers
know when they are well off, and but few will
consent to take a country billet, even with promo-
tion thrown in. And the city officer is right up
against the ear of the powers that be, while the
country one can only appeal by letter. The Public
Service Association is largely supported by the
country offices, and a delegation of these dried-up
exiles recently met the council of the Association
on the subject. The country men want a compul-
sory system of exchanges whereby the public ser-
vants of Broken Hill, Walgett, Balranald, Mil-
parinka and other scenes of horror may periodi-
cally exchange the cold goat and onions of those
parts for the flesh pots of Sydney. The
idea has much to recommend it. It would
greatly reduce the number of lunatics in the ser-
vice ; nobody can be a public servant or anybody
else for many years in the faz-thest Out-Back
without wrecking his intellect. It would spread
the blessings of the newest metropolitan collar
and cuff and boiled shirt in an uncivilised land ;
it would enable city public officials to improve
their knowledge of the country, and country ones
to get their beer once in their lives for 3d. All
these are matters worth consideration.
