On New Year’s Eve such of the population
as had not bought a squeaker and a bag of flour,
and which refrained from making night and
Sydney hideous with weird noises and mixed
drinks, watched “La Boheme” at Her Majesty’s.
“La Boheme” is what is known as the “modern
opera ” —a tale of squalid poverty, mirthless, piti-
ful humor, and ghastly disease, with glorious
orchestral accompaniments distributed through it
like gems scattered on a sandhill. As Marcel, the
Eainter, Bozzoli both acted and sang well. Dani’s
igh, ringing notes were all right, but his
rotundity did not harmonise with the lack of sus-
tenance that yawned right through the piece.
There was an infinite tenderness about his love
scene with Mimi in the Bohemians’ garret. As
the love-sick, consumption-stricken young woman,
Miss Dalia.Bassich was calculated to rouse public
opinion more effectively on the necessity of grap-
pling at once with the tuberculosis fiend than a
whole college of lecturers and experts crying in a
wilderness of daily papers. N.S.W. Board of
Health would, if it were not a dead ass dreaming
it is a Pegasus, subsidise Manager Williamson to
play “Boheme” for a hundred nights. Miss
Dalia’s make-up would do the rest, and the
country would be dotted over with sanatoria.
** » *
Opera “ La Gioconda ” (the Ballad Singer)
staged for the first time in Sydney this generation,
ought to be seen at least twice. The vocal music,
the unfolding of the play, its environment, and
more than all else, the ghosts that arise of that
mysterious, proud, treacherous Bride of the
Adriatic, demand such attention that one loses
some of the splendid orchestral music. And
Signor Hazon’s orchestra deserves an undis-
tracted, undivided hearing. The libretto was
inspired by Hugo’s “Tyrant of Syracuse,” but it
is typically mediaeval and Italian in its love and
lust, its superstition, and its demoniacal revenges.
The music has nothing of the heaviness or
sonibreness of the tragedy. To many it will recall
Gounod, and it is full of the brightness, strength,
and emotionalism of Italian melody. Out in the
water-streets a regatta is taking place, and up and
down against the background of Ducal Palace and
Giant’s Staircase a spy of thelnquisition is striding
and singing mischief. The Lion’s Mouth gapes in
the courtyard, inviting letters that may take friend
or foe across the Bridge of Sighs. A ballad singer
(La Gioconda) comes along, leading her blind
mother; and the spy, who loves the girl, grows
oppressive in bis love-making. The blind woman
calls upon the Virgin for help. The courtyard
fills with the populace, who carry the man of the
winning gondola shoulder-high. In the ruck is
the vanquished man, carrying an oar and an awful
frown. No defeat in those days could be fair, and
the spy whispers to the ex-champion something
like tins: “It wasn’t the sliding-seat of your out-
rigger that stuck : it was the blind woma#who
bewitched you.” And the ex-champion’s backers
rush to kill the “witch.” She is saved by Laura,
the wife of the Grand Inquisitor, who accepts in
acknowledgment the blind woman’s rosary. Enzo
Grimaldi, the betrothed of La Gioconda, is a prince
(under the ban of the Holy Inquisition), who
has had to turn fisherman. In better days he
was to have wedded Laura, and now
when he sees her again be desires to be off
with the new love and on with the old. That
night the spy, Bernaba, brings the willing Laura
to the prince-fisherman’s boat (after he has
thoughtfully dropped a letter into the Lion’s
Mouth, acquainting the duped husband with his
wife’s rendezvous). He has also invited LaGioconda
to this water-party, to let her see her lover em-
bracing another woman. The street-singer would
have ended the frisk with a stiletto, but she sees
her rosary on Laura’s breast, and, instead of a
knife, she gives warning of the husband’s ap-
proach. Laura flees, gets home and into gorgeous
raiment. With deadly politeness her husband
hands her a phial of poison, and says, in effect,
“ I have always anticipated your every want.
Now, just look at this elegant coffin ! (he draws
aside a curtain, displaying a catafalque). Oblige
me by filling it.” He goes out and the street-
singer slips in and exchanges the poison for the
equivalent of a mediaeval sulphonal powder. In
this, the third act, is the famous ballet, “Dance
of the Hours ”; the Hall of Gold in the Ducal
Palace is full of guests, and a grand ball is in pro-
gress. The Grand Inquisitor draws aside the cur-
tain, and shows the horrified guests the (ap-
parently) dead body of his wife. This is one of
the very few grand operas in which a deserving
person gets a morsel of happiness at the close.
The fisherman-prince and the Grand Inquisitor’s
wife elope joyfully together. La Gioconda stabs
herself in order to avoid keeping a promise to the
spy, but the spy laughs last, and says “ I strangled
your mother ! ”
« • • *
Signora Benedetto, as La Gioconda, was a
little foggy-voiced in the first act, but as the opera
grew her volume of voice increased, and her acting
of the part was so good that one forgot her failure
to look it. Her singing of the “ Suicide’s Aria ”
was the triumph of the evening. Bozzoli and
Larizza, as the Spy and Enzo Grimaldi respec-
tively, acted the strong men under the weight
of the opera. In the great duet in the first act
this pair of musical Sandows had long rests while
the audience applauded. Signorina Marcomini
looked and acted the noble’s bride to a finish, and
her mezzo-soprano was in good order. Signorina
Marchi was so pathetic a figure as the blind
woman that many of the audience will drop
pennies to blind street musicians whilst the
memory of “La Gioconda ” remains.
** * *
Certain additions have been made to
“ Alice in Wonderland,” at Sydney Royal, since
this darkly condemnatory paper wrote of it last.
In its original state it gave the impression of being
very brief in the tail, but it has been expanded by
the addition of a harlequinade and the introduc-
tion of two irrelevant but active brethren, Kelly
and Ashby, late of the Lee-Rial company. One
result is that the Gryphon and the deserving
Mock Turtle, and the Mad Hatter, are to some
extent, forced into the background, and it seems
possible that new and irrelevant items will con-
tinue gathering round them till they and the
ninetieth Lobster and the 116th Rabbit are
almost lost to view. At all events, “ Alice in
Wonderland ” looks a considerably more enduring
show than it did when The Bulletin turned the
tap of reprobation on it in last issue.
** * *
The children, and the big dog, and the
subordinate members of Mr. Arnold’s co. have
played to crowded houses since Boxing Night at
Sydney Palace, and the mystery of the good
behaviour of the two babies is darker than ever.
There is no record of their ever having howled, or
had a tooth, or otherwise misbehaved a single
night. Little Coquette has also maintained her
pristine charm, dancing and acting like a fairy.
But not to put too great a strain on these amiable
young persons, or perhaps to show that the adult
is not yet played out, “The Professor’s Love
Story ” will be staged this Saturday. This Barrie
comedy is new to Sydney, but all accounts of it,
as played by Mr. Arnold’s co., are highly favor-
able.
** # *
At Sydney Queen’s Hall, Edmund Mont-
gomery’s entertainment has a new item, “ A
Mexican Bull Fight.” Marguerite Dorris, Harry
Hall and others help along the variety part of the
performance. The illustrated song seems to have
come to stay. The usual pantomimic representa-
tion of ‘ ‘ Red Riding Hood ” concludes the
performance.
** * *
“ For the Sake of a Woman,” at the
Criterion, keeps its hold on public opinion by
means of its red and blue chair, an abduction or
two, and the breakage of the bank windows by
an enraged populace that can’t get its money
back. Laurence Dunbar continues to enact the
hero who crumples up slowly under the weight
of his Past, the rage of the populace, and the
bitter scorn of a heartbroken wife in an elaborate
frock. _ Miss Helene Burdette, as Lady Clare,
gets paid the Wages of Sin, as usual, by being
murdered, but as the Wages of Virtue in Ida
Gresham’s case consist in being nearly electro-
cuted, there is a lack of solid and reliable moral
in Holloway’s production.
** * *
At the Lyceum (Sydney) “ Greed of Gold ”
still holds the hoards. Mee Chow’s opium den
shows the Chinkie at his worst, and a monkey at
its best, and the sagacity of that latter animal in
refusing to kill the son of the man who gave it a
cup of water remains as unnatural as ever. So
does the intellectual acumen of the other char-
acters in understanding what the dumb man
means when he merely claws at the air in a futile
manner.
** * *
Charlie Campbell’s Minstrel and Vaude-
ville co., at the Alhambra Music Hall (Sydney),
gave a varied performance the night this writer
happened to be there, and handed round a pro-
gramme that didn’t match. That pink fragment
of reading matter stated, amongst other weather
prophecies, that Miss Eva Ward would sing
“The Skipper’s Daughter,” but instead she
gave “Please, Mr. Conductah” in a manner
that was not quite equal to Melba’s. The per-
formances were, as a rule, faint outline sketches
of bygone Tivoli items. The gentleman who in-
troduced the performers spoke in a loud and
bitter voice when he said “Miss Blank will now
favor us with Blank ”; it was like a condensed
chapter from the Lamentations of the low-spirited
prophet who did the Solomon Eagle business in
old Jerusalem.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has awakened to the fact
that a variety entertainment needs to be
entertaining, and last Saturday, to a packed
house, it set forth one ofjthe brightest programmes
on its record. Derenda and Breen are the great
stars, a pair of agile young persons who juggle
with clubs and enliven their dexterity with
comedy business in a really entertaining
fashion. America, land of freedom and patent
medicines, is evidently the true home of the
variety entertainer; there is not an atom of
stolidity in the performers from that country,
and even when their powers of singing, acting,
or juggling are not very wonderful, they eke them
out witli patient attention to detail. Derenda,
and Breen are, of course, Americans ; so are the
Allisons, who provide another welcome item in
the present Tivoli programme. A joyful re-
appearance is that of J. W. Winton, the clever
ventriloquist, who could, however, afford to do
without the laugh that he gets by making a
“dummy” Kruger sing “God Save the King.”
Kruger is a very small dog, and it is quite safe°to
pinch his tail; but, in view of present circum-
stances, it is hardly decent to drag an effigy of
the old man on to a music-hall stage to make fun
for the gallery. Except for the fact that the
“first part” is gradually slipping into the old
groove and calling for absolute abolition—not
merely new-dressing and setting—Sydney Tivoli
is just now a satisfactory place at which to
frivol away an evening.
“Robinson Crusoe,” at Sydney Empire,
is a pantomime in which the performers are scanty,
and the local allusions are many. The scene
where a wreck, a rock, and a Robinson Crusoe,
also some industrious waves, all manage to exist
on one small stage, is received nightly with
enthusiasm. It is the only attempt at spectacular
effect in the production. “ Robinson Crusoe ”is
an example of a panto, that keeps its house full
by not attempting anything too novel in the way
of wit, but rather sticking to the theory that an
old joke is the best for a hard road. Stevens
as a female in bloomers is very ancient fun.
The half-dozen girls who compose Will W. Atkins’
cut-throat band are all nice girls. Kearns and
M‘Kisson enliven the solitudes of Crusoe’s Island
by an aerial act. There would naturally be two or
more acrobats on an uninhabited island.
* * * sfc
At Melbourne Princess’s the beautiful
“Aladdin” show is now concluding with
Goatcher’s transformation scene at 11 p.m., the
four young men who bike in a basket having taken
their novelty elsewhere. George Musgrove’s
panto., or extravaganza, is about the handsomest
spectacle ever staged in Melbourne. Also, it is
bright and interesting as far as its narrative goes.
Abanazar, the little Dutch-Israelite magician,
transacts his peezness with Aladdin in a style
that gets away from pantomime tradition, and
deepens into realism when he grows angry.
Abanazar, urging his young agent to give him
the lamp what time that unsuspecting she-boy
picks up emerald and ruby dumplings in the Cave
is a faithful presentment of a legendary pawn-
broker racked with anxiety. And Nellie Stewart,
as the boy in question, plays her part in the Cave
scene as though she were taking Uncle Ab. quite
seriously. Everything worth doing at all—even
a tragic episode in nursery fiction—is worth doing
well, and the principal boy is the same con-
scientious Nellie as ever she was. Conscien-
tiousness, however, amounts to a sad mistake
in the matter of her much-mentioned snake
costume. The glittering reptile is a fat im-
pediment to the wearer’s gait, and a circular
protuberance upon her shapely figure, and as it is
said to weigh more than a sack of potatoes, The
Bulletin would advise Miss Stewart to apply for
the hand of the Princess in a more rational
garment. A pretty double duet in the Chinese
Emperor’s ante-chamber is a musical feature of
“Aladdin.” Likewise the artistic cake-walk of
the Perman trio, the singing of Ghe Gothams, the
Porcelain Procession, and the Pink Ballet are re-
ceived with an enthusiasm which foretells a long
run for the show. “Aladdin” has two matinees
per week, when a harlequinade is added for the
alleged entertainment of the young.
** * *
“ San Toy ” proceeds at Her Majesty’s,
with every appearance of being fated to stay on
the premises until the last echoes of Xmas have
died away. Lauri didn’t let himself go with con-
fidence for the first night or two, but he is now
going vigorously, and if his Chinaman seems much
the same Chow as heintroduced in “ The Geisha,”
nobody objects to the revival. Take it altogether,
“ San Toy ” is an improvement on “ The Geisha. ”
The lyrics are often quaint and snappy, the dance
music is charming, and Florence Young’s voice is
the best that Williamson’s opera co. has possessed
of recent years.
A* * * *
The colossal improbabilities of “The
Span of Life ” continue to serve B. Holt’s purpose
at Melb. Royal, and a crowded audience gloats
over Dunstan Leech’s discomfiture every evening.
Dunstan never wearies of ill-doing. Early in the
melodrama he removes a youthful step-brother
from his path by injecting morphia into a bunch
of grapes; after which he endeavours to achieve
the destruction of a mail steamer by removing
Walter Baker with a gun. Then he journeys to
Zambesia and becomes an Arab slave-trader, in
I order that he may seize Baker's gold mine, and
take Miss Ross captive. His intentions re the
lady are mingled with a casual brutality to her
cheeild —a very small boy who sometimes kisses
his hand to the stalls whilst his mother protests
that he will die of thirst if Dunstan refuses to give
him water. There are no redeeming points about
Dunstan that the present scribe can remember.
As a man he invites loathing, and as a villain he
earns general contempt for the failure of his
schemes. All the same, he is an indispensable
person. But for Dunstan there would be no occa-
sion for a boneless bridge in Africa, nor any
trouble at the lighthouse on the Coffin Rocks.
And if these things were omitted from Bland
Holt’s revival, it would never have been revived
at all.
*# * «
Friday of this week will be a rather
sorrowful evening at Melb. Opera House. The
funeral of the old-established “First Part” is
fixed for that occasion, and a number of small
ballad singers, and “sisters,” and people
who merely helped to make up “ the
whole of the famous company ” on the
right side of 9 p.m., will quit the Rickards
salary list for ever. An attempt to improve upon
the old order of things by removing the chairs,
and abolishing the burnt-cork corner men, was
not a success, so when another variety co. came
along and prospered without any sort of First
Part, the local Rickards took the hint. The First
Part was an expensive institution which had
practically ceased to show a profit.
** * *
Several new turns from America cropped
up at the Opera House, Melb., last Saturday, and,
with the exception of a Tired Tramp, who wafted
his weariness across the footlights, these latest
arrivals seemed to hit the target. Colby and
Way, in fact, scored a bull’s-eye. Colby is a good
enowh ventriloquist and an excellent puppet-
showman, and Alice Way looks more like a
dancing doll than any previous lady who
has professed to have a clockwork interior. The
Lundgreens are moderately humorous gymnasts
on a see-saw ladder arrangement, and Weston
and Yost dialogue through their noses under
conditions new to this country—just at present.
Unfortunately Weston or Yost uttered an ancient
“ gag ” with the word “Hell” in it. “ Hell ”is
a popular term among Yankee small boys and
their male parents, but the audience at Rickards’s
thought it a shocking word to come from the
mouth of an apocryphal youngster, so Weston or
Yost will probably bowdlerize “H 1” by
dropping the aspirate, or will substitute “Ge-
henna ” for the awful indiscretion.
** * *
At Melbourne Bijou, where Chas. Sweet,
the piccaninnies, Henry Lee, Nelstone, &c.,
happen as before, a couple of new turns are
Wallace Browillow and the Lotto cyclists.
Brownlow, the beloved baritone, sings Kipling’s
“Mandalay” and “ Gunga Din” for all they
are worth in their musical setting, which
is not a vast amount, anyhow. The Lottos,
mentioned in last issue of this family journal
as a sensational adjunct to Musgrove’s panto.,
perform around their precipitous basket track
with less effect on the Bijou stage than they pro-
duced at the Princess’s. They are brought nearer
to the audience in the smaller house, and, seem-
ingly, distance lends astonishment to the specta-
tators. Still the fact remains that the Lottos do
travel on an angle of 60 degrees at a x x x x of
a pace.
** * *
Maggie Moore and co. appear to have
created a want at the little Gaiety Theatre, under-
neath the Bijou. Any show that can flourish at
the unfamiliar Gaiety in its first week must be
likely to keep a hold on the floor for some time,
and the M.M. enterprise has flourished consider-
ably since Boxing Night. “ Struck Oil ” made a
good start on Saturday with Leo Du Chateau, J.
P. West, Vincent Scully, J. Ennis and Blanche
Lewis to look after the simple old drama whenever
Maggie Moore wasn’t performing her large share
of it for more than the 6000th time. Eighteen
solid years’ practice at the character have given
more than one sort of roundness to the original
Lizzie Stofel, but the tears get into her voice as
naturally as ever, and the public approves of
Lizzie all the time. Besides, “ Struck Oil ”is the
story of a self-sacrificing Dutchman, who goes to
war for the sake of his wife and child and family-
hearthstone, and its anti-jingo sentiment sounds
very up-to-date.
** # #
Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus concluded its
twelfth week in Melbourne by departing for Tas-
mania last Monday, under the direction of brother
Dan. Meanwhile brother Tom is running a
branch Circus in Adelaide with enormous success,
and the two members of the firm shake hands by
telegraph at regular intervals, and wire each other
gratifying news about the takings.
At Poverty Point.
Seems that the first night of “ Aladdin” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s was a terrible event for the clown who had con-
tracted to be “ funny ” for ten minutes whilst the Lotto
cycling-track was getting fixed behind the front scene.
The track fixings went wrong, and had to be taken to
pieces again, which meant a loss of 15 minutes or more,
so the person who had arranged to be funny for ten
minutes had to be a bright and spontaneous source of
mirth for nearly half-an-hour.
Albert Norman, the old-established villain and semi-
villain of Bland Holt melodrama, has lost four stone
weight within the last few months, and is unable to dis-
cover the secret of his wasting. The production of
“ The Span of Life ” happens opportunely for Norman.
He has no part in Bland Holt’s latest, so he can sample
one or tvvo of the Victorian health resorts and give the
local waters a chance. When a 15-stone man diminishes
to a middle-weight in a mysterious manner, it is time for
him to “ take the waters ” somewhere or other. That’s
what the dooks and earls do. And dooks, as a rule, are
awfully addicted to recovering their health.
George Musgrove seems to have engaged the Lotto
troupe of cyclists for his “ Aladdin ” panto, without con-
sidering the difficulties in the way of staging their sensa-
tional track. They were promptly passed over to the
World’s Entertainers’ show, and all the effect of G.M,’s
preliminary puffs, plus the advt. they got from appearing
at the Princess’s, went to benefit the Lee and Rial
management.
“ The turtle goes slowly, but he gets there in time for
the soup.” (The Mandarin in “ San Toy.”)
Percy Grainger, who seems to have been the first pianist
to get a fair start in London as a duly labelled Australian,
is the son of the Westralian Government architect. Melba
made a point of being present, and sent young Percy a
sweet message of congratulation. Melba’s interest in all
things Australian is growing more enthusiastic every
week as her tour-time approaches.
London criticisms of Percy Grainger’s first pianoforte
recitals in the Big Smoke show clearly that the grave,
golden-haired boy of eight years ago has developed his
talent on the lines laid down for him by Herr Pabst, who
“formed” him in Melbourne. Pabst was a teacher of
thoroughness. The Standard says of Percy Grainger
“ There was in all his renderings an absence of the
trickery and display that mark so many young exe-
cutants.”
A droll incident happened at Melb. Royal during the
recent revival of “ The War of Wealth.” The play has an
explosion scene, in which all the bang and smoke come
from the back of the stage, whilst the catastrophe seems
to happen in front. One night a girl in the reserved
stalls threw back her head and gave a suitable feminine
yell when she heard the noise. It was such a violent
throwback that the sharp rim of her straw hat cut the
cheek of the man seated next to her. A rough-edged
straw hat strikes the happy medium between a razor and
a tomahawk in such a case. The unhappy , victim, who
probably shut his eyes when the girl shrieked, went forth
into the vestibule with his handkerchief to his face,
protesting that a bit of the explosion had nearly been the
death of him.

The Hannans Oroya Gold Mining Co., Kal-
goorlie, have lately placed an order with the Austral
Otis Comp'any, South Melbourne, for a double drum first
motion winding engine. The cylinders are 18 inch
diameter by 36 inch stroke, and are fitted with balanced
piston valves. The drums are Bft. in diameter and fitted
with indicators. The engine is completely controlled
from the driver’s platform. In all respects is similar to
the engines working so satisfactorily at the Golden Horse-
shoe Mine, Boulder, Long Tunnel, Victoria, and the Cobar
Chesney Mine, N.S.W.*

See for yourself. The Australian Medical
Gazette of January, 1891, compares Zetz Spa to the cele-
brated Vichy waters of France, and states that it is
valuable for gout, rheumatism, gravel, catarrh of the
bladder, diabetes, dyspepsia, splenic and hepatic dis-
orders.

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Co.lins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

• * * •
During the trial, at Brisbane not long ago, of
one Hall for “ obtaining by false pretences,” a letter was
read which had been found in accused’s possession, and
which set forth that the writer, in order to induce a per-
son to believe that certain washdirt carried gold, got the
man to take out a dish of the dirt himself, and in the
meanwhile put in his own mouth a clay pill containing
about half a grain of gold. Then, when the other
man brought the dish of worthless dirt along, the
smarty was taken with a sneezing-fit, during which he
managed to jerk the little clay pellet into the dish.
The accused, to whom this letter was addressed, got a
“ sneeze ” on his own account in the shape of two years
“hard.” There are more ways of salting a mine than
killing a cat. That cabled of the niggers in West Africa
(salting mines by firing gold into the quartz) is so old that
it is 10 to I some London boomster made the bullets even
if the niggers fired them, which writer much doubts.
However, the Governor of the Gold Coast has reported
that quite a number of mining concessions received from
native chiefs are quite worthless. This comes a little too
late, as the boom had gone off long before the news was
received and millions have been lost in “ Jungle ” cats.
Warnings in all booms come too late, and they are seldom
heeded while the boom is on.
Mr. Sam Hordern has postponed erection
of his new Sydney shop buildings on the Goul-
burn, George, and Pitt streets block. The un-
settled state of the “ resumptions’ ” market is said
to be the cause of this step. Rumor has it that
the Government had an idea of purchasing the old
site.
Thus, solemnly, S.M. Herald :— “lfc is
incomprehensible to the civilian mind that the
British, with a quarter of a million soldiers in the
country, can be surprised and lose so heavily as
they did recently at Tafelkop.” Then Granny
proceeds to set the “civilian mind” right by
repeating the orthodox “military mind’s” ex-
planation : “It is not so much in the ability
of the commandants as in the mobility of
the Boer forces that the strength of the
movement in S’ Africa consists.” But “mo-
bility,” or any other physical quality, is use-
less without brains to direct it. “Mobility” is
the- quality which enables the Boer to get
there quickly, but if, through bad leadership,
he is captured or killed when he arrives, his
mobility may only be a misfortune. It doesn’t
matter how “mobile” Boers or British may be;
they will do themselves no good if they don’t
move in the right direction at the proper time.
Apparently the Boers do that, and the British
don’t, therefore it is a fair inference that the Boers
are guided by better brains than the British.
Whether they are the commandants’ brains is a
detail, which simply shows the absurdity of
the Herald’s sneer at “ the civilian mind.”
Some civilian minds are horribly deceiving.
Julius Caesar, a “civilian,” was at 44 given the
command in Gaul because his enemies were sure
he would make a mess of it. Spinola, the Genoese
banker, was at 37 put in command of the Spanish
army in the Netherlands for no conceivable reason
except that he had plenty of money ; yet he
earned a military reputation which survives as
one of the dozen greatest in history. Then there
was Cromwell, who, after he was 43, proved him-
self the best cavalry leader and one of the best
organisers and strategists England ever produced.
And now De Wet, the cocky-farmer, upsets all
tradition again.

COLUMN
The Queensland Government s praccically use-
less Agricultural Bank Bill was again heard of lately. Ihe
radica! defect of Queensland’s proposed State Bank, as of
Westralia’s, is that it is only intended to lend money to
be used for the purpose of making improvements, it a
man with £lOOO worth of security applies for a loan of
£lOO, and doesn’t want the money for the purpose of
making improvements, the bank will have nothing to do
with him—that business must be reserved for the Private
banks. And, as a rule, the man who wants to borrow
small sums for improvements is the struggling cocky who
desires to put up a fence or something of the kind and
whose security isn’t good enough for the P rivate
to look at. It is really a case of the State taking
the unprofitable dregs of the business, and le^'’ ln S
the good business for private institutions. A State
Bank which really means business should go in for lend-
ing on every kind of real property—city, suburban, o
country—where the security is good enough, for these
sufficient reasons I . , ,
(1) The State can lend at 4J or 5 per cent, and make a
profit, and the larger the business the greater the profit.
The State wants all the profits it can get.
(2) A large business can be worked at a far lower per-
centage of expense than a small one.. By doing a large
business, the State can charge lower interest and make
bi f) e The°State wants to gradually oust the absentee
money-lender who is a large section of the money-lending
fraternity. If a needlessly large amount is going out of
the country in interest, it is just the same loss to the
State whether the money goes out as interest on city pro-
perty or on any other property. ~ .
(41 if there is any foreclosing to be done, the State
should do it. The State should be the national landlord,
and if part of its lost heritage comes back bv foreclosure
that is as good a way to recover it as any other way.

On the strength of this the astounding Oronhyatekha
claims that his office is making better provision for pay-
ing its policies at maturity than the A.M.P. does, and is
really the safer institution. By his own showing the
A M.P. has a big margin of safety, inasmuch as it can
reduce its bonus if anything goes wrong, while the I. O.f.
has no such margin. And his theory seems to be that it
is safer not to have a margin.
Of course the whole statement is a misrepresenta-
tion, as almost everything connected with the bounder
IO F institution appears to be. The 1.0. F. doesn t in-
sure anybody, aged 25, for £2OO at £2 7s. per annum, or at
anything like that sum. It charges £2 7s. premium and
15s “ court dues ” which make up £3 25., which is quite a
different thing. And it doesn’t work its office on a real
expense rate at 5 per cent, or anything resembling it.
Here is the real state of things, taking the 1.0. F ba anee-
sheet for 1899— the latest one published in the latest
edition of the Insurance Blue Book which we have been
able to procure, and the latest available A3I.P. balance-
sheet ; and taking a £lOO policy in each office :

The favorite dodge of the 1.0. F. is to set its
policy without bonuses or surrender value against the
A.M.P.’s wifA-profit policy, carrying large bonuses and
large surrender value. Taking the A.M.P. s without-
profit policy, however, we get these results to set against

The result is that, to the small policy-holder, the
A.M.P., or any other Australian mutual office of any
■standing, charges less than the 1.0. F., spends far less of
'the policy-holder’s money in expenses and other items
which are not life assurance, and, because it sets aside far
'more of the money received from him to accumulate
'towards payment of his policy, gives him immeasurably
better security. The great Independent Order of
Foresters dodge comes mostly under these headings :
'(1) It alwavs talks of its remarkably low premiums, and
says nothing of the fact that it makes heavy charges in
-addition to the premium, which no Australian office
does. (2) It brags insufferably about how its expense
is only 5 per cent. —about the lowest on record and
>says nothing of the fact that it has been quite a common
thing for “ contingencies ” in its balance-sheet to amount
to another 5 per cent, (it apparently doesn’t reckon “ con-
tingencies” as expenses), making 10 percent., also that
the huge sum collected as “ Court dues ” must be added
to the expenses before the member can find out what his
policy really costs him. (3) It keeps pretty much in the
‘background the fact that its members may be called
upon at any time for extra premiums, and if so called
upon they have no remedy but to pay. The man who
can’t pay loses his policy and all the money he has already
paid in. (4) And it keeps very silent about the awful fact
that when its liabilities and assets were valued (at the
end of 1897) on the strict principles prevailing in England
the present value of the liabilities was over seven millions,
and the assets were very little over half-a-million. The
1.0. F. taking it all round, appears to be about the most
utterly impudent institution that ever struck this simple-
minded country. And its alleged cheapness appears to
be about the largest of the many delusions attached to it.

The profits represent £3 19s. 6d. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’funds as at the commencement of the
half-year (capital £385,187, and reserves £55,402 ; total,
£440,589). The paid-up capital has since been increased
a little, and now stands at £413,533. Six months ago it
was resolved to make a call of £1 per share, and also to
issue £300,000 worth of preference capital (30,000 shares
of £lO each). As no mention is made of any preference
capital being yet actually issued, however, the increase
probably arises from call payments.
The Royal Bank of Queensland has now paid off
the last of its reconstructed deposits, and it is
probably by wav of celebrating the event that
it increases its dividend to 3 per cent. Whether
the increase is yet fully justified is a question that
can only be judged later on. The business looks in
good condition, and the fact that the shareholders’ funds
now equal more than half the liabilities to the public
makes the Royal one of the strongest banks in Australia
from a depositor’s point of view. The reserves are all
separately invested in State securities, which is an
eminently wise precaution, and the reserve of cash and
State securities (£309,084) represents nearly 34 per cent
of all liabilities to the public, which is solid enough for all
purposes. Also, the increase in profits is still maintained
despite the bad times Queensland is going through.
* * • •
The bargain made for the sale of the United
Australian Mutual Fire Insurance Co. to the Colonial
Mutual apparently gives the former institution _ about
£24,000. Seemingly it sells for £24,000 assets which its
last balance-sheet declared to be worth ''41.000 over and
above all liabilities, and it throws the goodwill of a pretty
large business in gratis. This fully justifies everything
The Bulletin said when the U.A.M. Co. wrote up its
assets by £3672 in 1899—only two years ago. This paper
remarked then, and has kept remarking ever since, that
the assets apparently were much more in need of writing
down than up, and professed great suspicion about the
valuation which put on the additional £3762 —also
about all valuations where an embarrassed company sud-
denly finds its property increase in value by just the right
amount at just the right moment. The Bulletin was the
only paper which said things like these, and the event
justifies it. If a small sum is allowed for goodwill, then
the sale seems to imply that the U.A.M.’s properties are
only saleable at about one-half their alleged value.
• * * •
When the U.A.M. Insurance Co. was going
steadily to wreck through no cause except its absurdly
high expense-rate, one shareholder (Kerr, M.L.A.) tried
to draw attention to the need for reform. Among other
things, he called meetings of the shareholders. At the
final meeting, when it was agreed to sell the discredited
institution at a great sacrifice, Kerr wanted to know why
it was necessary to make the sacrifice if the assets were
worth their alleged value. He was promptly accused of
having done “all he could to damage the c 0.,” though
apparently he w r as the only man who tried to save
it; his accuser being the chairman of the board under the
management of which a most promising business had been
driven to sell off as if it was a quantity of old clo’. The
awful arrogance of belltopperedmenwhenabusinesswhich
is supposed to be under their charge has gone askew in a
very silly manner, and when questions are asked on the
subject, is an interesting study. Also, Godfrey, M.L.C.,
called out “ Shame ! ’’—not at the Board or the chairman,
but at the man who wanted to know.
* * « *
The Colonial Bank (Melbourne) repaid the last
of its reconstructed deposits on 31st December, 1901.
The Royal of Queensland has just done likewise.
This leaves five banks still in the reconstruction stage—
the Commercial of Australia (Melbourne), the A.J.S.
(Sydney), the Queensland National (Brisbane), and two
English banks, the E.S. and A. and the London.
• • * *
During 1901 Australasia paid to English banks
£133,020 in commission alone for the flotation of loans
issued in London. No Australasian bank received a penny
of the money. And these English banks made such a
sinful hash of the business that the money borrowed in
London last year cost this country, on an average,
£3 lls. 9d. per cent., while the loans floated in Sydney
and Melbourne only cost £3 10s. per cent.
* • • •
The once great St. James’s Building Society
(Melbourne) expects to get to the end of its long-dragged-
out liquidation very shortly. The depositors have al-
ready been paid 16s. in the £, and an offer has been
received for the final fragments of the institution that
will yield enough to give them in all 19s. in the
£. The shareholders are to get nothing, but they have
been fully aware of that fact fora long time, and expected
no better. They have nominally £215,000 in the concern,
but it evaporated long ago.
* ¥ • *
Not since the days of “atmospheric gas” has
there been such a rich idea as “ Byronite,” an explosive
just now before the Victorian Court. Byronite—a share
of the patent rights in which a confiding person purchased
—was made of pollard, beet-root, eucalyptus, chalk and
ti-tree, mixed with lime-water. The formula had the
advantage of being adaptable to circumstances: if you
hadn’t lime you could use oil, or molasses, or sugar syrup,
and most of the other items were just as readily convert-
ible. When you had mixed up the materials mentioned
(or any other materials having a certain resemblance, or no
resemblance at all, thereto), and baked them, you packed up
the dust produced, and placed it “in a generator, inside
which should be hanging a series of small sponges
saturated with oil of cloves. These sponges will extract
the gas and form a powerful explosive power for working
any kind of machinery. Steam or any other power may
be used.” The last sentence shows that this new power
agent is not of a jealous disposition, and will permit worn-
out and effete institutions, such as steam, to co-operate
with its labors. The inventor had also a means of
generating gas “ from the same mixture,” or a somewhat
different one. It was claimed in Court on his behalf that
he absolutely believed in the efficacy of his formula, and
he was certainly able to persuade someone (who is now
very angry) to buy a fourth share in the patent r ,uts.

Broken Hill mining directors are still without
satisfactory advices regarding metal market prospect?.
In effect they are told that almost anything may happen
—that nothing short of a big rise need surprise them.
The rapid recovery after Christmas that was talked about
is already despaired of. Meantime, the big town that has
grown around the mines is emptying itself as fast as it
knows how. In particular, the thoroughly good miners,
who can always command work, are getting away ; for a
long while past Broken Hill conditions have been hardly
good enough to retain them. This will make the struggle
of the poorer cos. harder when they do try to reopen.

Simultaneously with the threatened vvage-reduc-
tions at Broken Hill comes a similar notice with regard to
Moonta and Wallaroo (S.A.) copper-mines, in circum-
stances which make it appear as though the two sets of
directors are working together in this matter.
• » * •
The Broken Hill miners’ butcher’s bill for last
year has 167 items. Lives lost numbered 25 (there were
27 in 1900 and 16 in ’99) ; 37 other accidents are classed as
serious. In 1900, there was not a main-lode mine but had
its victim ; the B.H. Prop, had seven, and Central (Sul-
phide Corporation) three. Last year, the Prop, had 11,
while the well-managed Central had not one.
* • * *
“ Trotter ”: Fifteen months ago Chillagoe
(North Queensland) was a live town—not the corpse it is
to-day. Buildings were going up in all directions,
and tents were everywhere. On the road one con-
stantly met whole families in all manner of vehicles
making for the land of the great boundless “ cat,”
and there were hotels, stores —I think there was
even a newspaper —and general animation. Everybody
was “just waiting for the smelters to start.” Now
they are waiting for the rising of the dead. There
are very few people left in Chillagoe, and the chief con-
tents of the town seem to be empty shanties, unpaid
bills, goats, cranks and curses. Chillagoe has lost hope,
lost heart, lost its credit at the store, lost its boots in
many cases, and it looks as if it had lost its soul. It is a
decayed Lazarus, but it isn’t lying at the rich man’s gate,
for there is hardly anybody here rich enough to have a
gate, and the dogs don’t lick it, for the dog that can make
a living now in this howling, God-forsaken wilderness
has to be a mighty swift dog, and has no time to lick
anybody.

Croydon notes to Dec. 27,1901: Christmas gifts
to this goldfield included copious rainfalls—dams, creeks,
and watercourses overflowing. Also, a golden reef inter-
sected, at 280 ft. vertical, in Cuthbert’s Content Block ;
golden stone in the Baltic ; an important improvement
in the quality of the ore in the Golden Gate 10 and
11 South Block; a further discovery of rich stone
in the Golden Gate and Nancy Lee United; and other
promising developments. Golden Gate and South
Block crushed satisfactorily. King of Wallabadah, at
Golden Valley, is raising rich specimen stone, which is
being casked and bagged for safety. This mine is owned
by a party of working miners. Homeward Bound line
is coming to the front again, and in other outside
localities a revival is evident. Morgan’s Block pump
has been installed, and sinking resumed.
• * * *
“M‘G.” writes about a tin show: “Unique
and massive tin formation is now being developed at head
of Surprise Gully, Smith’s Creek, six miles from Mt. Garnet
(Q-), by the prospectors (Donoughue Bros, and Folkes)
and by J. Smith on their boundary. Already opened up a
surface (stripping to lode, Ift. to 18in.) for a length
of some 40(Jft., sunk on 6,8, and 12ft. in three
different shafts, trenched across from footwall
(hard granite, dipping towards hill-summit), for 18ft.
and hanging-wall not yet met with, and lode—nothing
but lode, and rich everywhere. Formation is
original, soft, red and micaceous, metal very uniform.
Already 12 loads tried by the Donoughues have returned
slightly over two tons of tin. Smith’s trench (or shaft)
on Donoughue’s boundary, down 12ft. when writer visited
the other day, shows no fault visible, and last couple of
feet average' fully 30 per cent, tin and all good soft
picking. Presumably a dyke formation. Sheddings of
rich alluvial have been worked below this for two miles
by a large number of men for the past three months,
and tin assays 73 per cent. Alluvial discovered
by M’Dermott when hunting for railway-sleepers (rail-
way runs alongside), but source of this only lately
unearthed. Neighborhood shows some high-grade copper
and silver leases being worked, and Smith’s Creek will
very likely (with copper market steadily sickening) be
the Garnet of the future.”

driven 119 ft. 6in. without having struck anything of
payable nature. . . . West drive : Advanced 21ft. ; passed
through formation containing good deal lime and iron
pyrites in mineral veins, which have nearly all cut out;
development having proved disappointing, this drive
stopped. North drive: Extended sft. on western lode ;
total, 10ft. No material change. Monkey shaft: South
drive extended sft.; total, 14ft.- Pug vein pinched to
2in., and ground tight. Crosscut east extended 16ft.
across gold-bearing country. Bulk of ground low grade ;
assay taken on cross section 12ft. long assaying 3dwt. 6gr.
gold per ton ; other assays from here being :—Face of
south drive 9ft. from bottom of Monkey shaft, 2ft. across,
including pug vein, loz. 4dwt. 20gr. ; east crosscut 9ft.
from shaft, loz. 6dwt, 3gr. ; face of crosscut, south side,
about 18in., loz. 4dwt. 20gr. The last 2ft. have been
through good-looking dark pug, which showed little free
gold in dish. .. . Stopes: Second south extended Bft.,
being 35ft. in jump up. This stope rather poor pug, only
averaging about 2in." *
For years one of the undeveloped resources of
Maoriland has been the iron-sand deposits of Taranaki.
The stuff is of excellent quality, and when it is fixed up
properly turns out a steel that, for general effectiveness,
is equal to anything on earth. But it_ costs an awful lot
to fix up, and the history of Taranaki is full of talk about
what might be done with that iron-sand. Edison made a
fortune out of similar deposits in the U.S.A. by a patent
process of his own invention ; and now Jerome Cadman,
who used to be a Minister, and HEE HEM Smith, who,
when he's not saving Maoriland, is lighting the lamps of
New Plymouth, have formed a co. (in Britain, of course),
with a capital of half-a-million, to make things hum. The
works that are to be started are said by the lion. Jerome
aforementioned, to mean the employment of a thousand
men. But a lot of castles in Spain have been built on
those same iron-sands.
Cyanide, caustic soda, chloride of lime, and all
mining and assay chemicals and apparatus, can be ob-
tained of Felton, Grimwade and (Jo., Melbourne, who
have issued a very handsome illustrated catalogue of all
mining requisites, price Is. 3d. post free. v

PENFOLDS
WINES
The Chamberlain tragedy at Port Mel-
bourne was more popular than a Christmas
pantomime with the crowd. The morgue,
that sombre institution, where damp Govern-
ment property lies on a slab, was rushed
by an eager populace, largely feminine. Two
girls in their early teens explained that their
mother had sene them to see a sight, “such as
they might never have a chance of seeing again.”
The men (and there were plenty of them) mostly
declared that their wives and their four daughters
had disappeared, and they were anxious to identify
this lot as the missing parties. The dragging for
the body of the eldest girl was the most successful
holiday attraction of the seaside suburb, and until
the poor remnant was found the piers were lined
with thousands. Most of the serried rows of
ghouls had brought their meals with them. They
picnicked on the spot, never taking more than
one eye off the police-boat while they ate their
cold roast goose and supervised the Christmas
gambols of their children. The latter had mostly
been promised a sight of the body if they were
good children and didn’t fall in the sea. If we
take our pleasures sadly, we square accounts by
taking our horrors in a cheerful spirit.
NOT THE RISE—THE FALL.
KINDLY SOUL (who gives advice): “So you were
hurt by being blown up in a blast, my man? ”
Cripple (sourly): “No—by cornin' down!”
Sergt. Davidson, the official who bosses
the Melbourne morgue, has rightly taken up a
stand against that institution being turned into a
place of popular amusement, and the other day,
when several belltoppered individuals who didn’t
even pretend to be looking for a lost wife and four
daughters, called to see the five unfortunates
drowned at Port Melb., Davidson promptly
blocked them. One ghoul passed out in a con-
dition of extreme indignation, threatening to see
“my friend, Mr. Peacock,” and get Davidson the
sack. Meanwhile the official has all decent Mel-
bourne behind him. At the time of the Boot-
Trunk affair, people were encouraged to go to the
morgue for the purpose of effecting an identifica-
tion, and availed them selves of the chance so
widely that “morgue parties,” picnicking on the
river bank, were to be seen daily in dozens.
Melbourne ’Erald, after spreading itself
for two days over six columns of sensationalism
about the drowning of a mother and her four
girls at Port Melbourne—picturing the purely-
hypothetical struggles of the children with their
insane parent —thoughtfully printed an interview
with “a well-known medical man,’’who said he
knew 30 or 40 women in a similar condition to the
drowned mother “ who were on the borderland of
insanity, and might at any time become possessed
of a spirit of frenzy.” The doctor who makes
such a remark for publication, and the paper which
prints it, are going the best way to bring about
more “sensations” of the same kind.
• * • *
Clement Wragge, Queensland’s weather-
maker, must keep up to date or bust. The other
day he predicted “further bush fires ” —which only
meant that he expected it to be hot and dry, just
as he might have predicted wet feet if he expected
rain, or large coal fires if he reckoned it was going
to be cold. The next outbreak will probably be :
“ Wet wickets may be expected under ‘Googoo.’
Captains winning the toss should note.”
* * * *
The sorrowful old omnibus has returned,
after many years’ absence, to Melb. streets, and
has commenced running in competition with the
trams. A Sydney speculator is at the bottom of
the trouble, and the vehicles come from that city.
The antiquated doings of the Mel'). Tramway Co.,
its refusal to establish penny sections according to
modern ideas, and its tendency to sacrifice comfort
to 24 per cent, dividends, are apparently respons-
ible. If the Tramway Co. falls no further behind
the times, the omnibus may be the oldest thing
that comes back to Melbourne ; but if the co.
grows much more venerable in its ways the
bullock-dray and the dinosaurus and the ptero-
dactyl may be expected almost any day.
* • « *
Very lately a married man (28) in the em-
ploy of the Australian Gaslight Co. (Sydney) was
found guilty of stealing from the company goods
to the value of £2. It transpired in evidence that
he was in receipt of £1 per week. It looks as if
the 15 per cent, monopoly got just as much
honesty as it paid for.

A country ston keeper, who was mayor,
churchwarden, and captain of the local volunteer
corps, visited the capital for some military dis-
play. He was ambitious, and managed to
arrange for a presentation to the then
Governor. His influential friend then
began—“ Allow me, Your Excellency, to
present General ” “Not General,”
interrupted the warrior excitedly. “Cap-
tain, captain!” “I beg Your Excel-
lency’s _ pardon, not General, Captain
Smith, of Bull’s Wool, general store-
keeper.”
* * * *
Since the depression struck Broken
Hill the miner naturally hasn’t had much
money for beer, and, to entice him to
spend a little more ready, some of the
publicans lowered the price. A hubbub
occurred. Hotelkeepers consulted with
brewers, and the result was an agree-
ment, in which all but one man joined,
that the price should be kept up. One
publican, however, gets over the agree-
ment by charging full rate, and then
filling ’em up again for nothing. The
trouble gave birth to several “beer clubs,”
where a citizen could get .his tanglefoot
at 3d. to 4d. a pint. These clubs, how-
ever, were formed too hurriedly, without
the requisite legal formalities, and the
police fell on them with great thud. The
point on which their bona fides broke
down was that a man could be nominated
as a member one minute and shout drinks
for the crowd the next. Also, the mode
of nomination was, in many instances,
altogether too premature. An old mem-
ber would bring in the candidate and
simply say, “This is Smith,” and the
thing was done. If no old member was
available the applicant could come in
alone and simply say he was Smith.
Broken Hill, by the way, has about 70
hotels, and when a number of clubs were
. added, it became almost the most-pubbed town
in the universe.

The sting of the war, as far as Aus-
tralia is concerned, seems to be in the tail.
Although the Boers had plenty of artillery,
well-served, at the start of hostilities, the
earlier cohentingenters who returned to
Australia arrived sound in wind and limb.
Those who have been coming back during
the past few weeks, however, include a
very large proportion of permanent crip-
ples. It is now aiommon t iling in any Aus-
tralian capital to meet a one-legged cohen-
tingenter hobbling on crutches. Does this
argue that the army medical corps is not so
well served as in the early days of the cam-
paign? Gun-shot wounds of the modern
type should seldom lead to the loss of a limb,
if properly treated. By the way, did not
Steeveils say in I is Khartoum book that, of
all departments under Kitchener, the only
one neglected was the ambulance ?
“ J.G.” : The servant, girl in a new
aspect. Have just heard of a ‘‘domestic”
who goes to the city for a year, carefully
saves all her earnings goes back to her own
little town, rents the local hall, and “gifs
a barty ” to all her friends and their friends.
&he does the thing in great style, and then
goes off to another 12 months’ toil. It is
her way of drinking her cheque. For one
night she is the hostess in a big house,
receiving her guests on a real carpet to
the music of a real band, and though the
hall is oidy wood she feels it is some kind of
apology for the marble corridors of her
ancestors. How many broken hopes,
broken hearts, and broken vanities ancestors
have caused by not leaving enough marble
corridors to go round !
* * * *
Latest mask of the bad Samaritans
who are willing to get woman out of her
little trouble—a suburban drapery shop.
Acland Oronhyatekha, of the 1.0. F. life-
assurance concern, has apparently only himself to
blame for being prosecuted on a charge of assault-
ing the lady type-writer. Though the girl
alleged certain things to one or two persons in the
office, she appears to have preferred to let the
matter drop, and, seemingly, her confidants said
nothing. But the Mohawk Indian gentleman
talked and talked, and through no other visible
cause than his flood of conversation the ’tecs at
last gathered that some kind of accusation had
been made. They started inquiring, and the
grease was in the fire. From which it would
appear that the splendid silence of the red man
whom Fenimore Cooper depicted has been con-
siderably weakened by civilisation.
Westralia has shaken off the
gyves of convict days but has still a few
weals on its limbs to remind it of the
recent horror. One of them is a
ticket-of-leave system dating back
from 1869. This seems to be adminis-
tered in a free-and-easy fashion ; any
sort of dangerous criminal with the
right sort of influence can get out of
gaol on ticket-of-leave, and very
many of those celebrate their liberty
by committing further crimes. On
the other hand the regulations under
the system can be effectively used
to hound back to gaol liberated pri-
soners who try to earn an honest
living. For instance an ex-prisoner,
who had been industrious and honest
ever since his liberation, was recently
hauled publicly before the court for
being out in the streets after 10. There
used to be an unfounded jibe in Perth,
by the way, that all society functions
had to end at 9.45, so as to avoid draw-
ing invidious distinctions between the
guest who got home voluntarily at 10
and the one who was liable to be ar-
rested and perhaps flogged if he didn’t.

“A.G.”: Used to be under the
impression that N.S.W. Railway Com
missioners kept men to clean their
carriages. T’other day, however, I
travelled first-class from Albion Park
to Wollongong, and am not likely to
forget the jaunt. The stench that arose
from under the seats, and from the
water bottle, was enough to lay-out a
Chow. A 14-stone lady fainted before
we had gone a mile, and two of us had
to hold her up to the window for
fresh air. She couldn’t stand ; nor could we
when we had done with her and the assorted
smells.
* * * *
It is quite a long time since there has been
in N.S. Wales a prosecution under the Public
Health Act. When the Board which administers
that Act started business, it proceeded against
quite a number of people for selling goods “ not
of the nature and substance demanded,” and,
among others, a very big shopkeeper was fined
over a matter relating to the quality of some con-
densed milk. Of late, such prosecutions have
been abandoned, and it is not because there no
longer remains reason for them. Every reform
seems to flicker out like that in Australia—through
sitting down and forgetting to rise up again.
HE HAD A PREFERENCE.
GROCER: “Now, my boy, you have been a week with
us. What part of the business do you like most? ”
NEW BOY : “ The shuttin ’ up, please, sir.”
“ Petronius ” : The annual exhibition of
students’ drawings in the Melb. National Gallery
makes a fine show of hands and big feet and ana-
tomical figures. By order of the trustees many of
the nudes have been removed. There is a remark-
able lack of individuality about the drawings ;
even all the noses are drawn, on scientific prin-
ciples, larger than life-size, so that they look like
bottles. This should gratify the Director, Bernard
Hall, who believes that technique cannot be good
unless it crushes out any intrusive personal ele-
ment. There is not a trace of beauty in the whole
show, but as an exhibition of ugliness it ap-
proaches the sublime.

TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gray Time resting on a stone—
Sad Time sitting all alone—-
Mumbled in an undertone
Of his endless weariness :
Sudden, with a sunbeam’s flight,
Came a cherub winged with light—
Came, as morning comes to night,
Brightening all his dreariness.
For the golden-headed hoy
Was the cherub men call Joy-
Golden Joy without alloy ;
And he laughed within Time’s ear.
Time forgot to turn his glass,
Left flowers blooming in the grass—
Could he tell the hour to pars
With Joy’s ruddy cheek so near?

CU r ftr anP Troubles.
Frequent ending to a foundation-
stone report : “ made an appeal,
with the result that £3O in cash was re-
ceived, together with another £lOO in
cheques.” Experience has taught pro-
moters of these affairs to differentiate
between cash and cheques. The £25
cheque which evokes a murmur of religious
applause is not always cashed ; sometimes
it is never intended to be, and the an-
nouncement of its existence is the price
of a genuine contribution of a fiver.
* * * *
“X.X.X.” in defence of the filthy
habit of using a common communion cup:
At every celebration the whitest of serviettes
are used to dry the rim of the cup before io is
passed on to the next participant.
But, as any doctor could tell you, a
whited sepulchre—we mean serviette—
or two doesn’t much help matters. What
protection is that when 30 or 40 people par-
take out of the same cup, and it is wiped
30 or 40 times with the same serviette?
The real reason for continuing the conta-
gious habit is that the church people
nave a vague, foolish belief that Provi-
dence will miraculously interfere to pre-
vent disease-communication. The R.G.’s,
though professing generally a robust faith
in miracles, long ago eschewed the wine-
cup. Other churches should follow suit,
or, if that is against their religious views,
use small wine-glasses, one for each com-
municant. There are no miracles; the
operations of Nature are unfaltering.
Rumor says that the next society divorce-
action is to be a military one, a N.S.W. officer
being petitioner. Heavy damages are claimed;
but an earnest attempt is being made to settle the
affair out of court.
• • • •
Since the outbreak of the Boer war Dawvid
Syme seems to have become imbued with the
wild patriotism which used to make the British
sailor of a century ago refer to every foreigner as
a sanguinary Dutchman. Only, in David’s case,
everybody is a German. Kruger, who, like all
burghers of the Boer States, has been known as
“Mister,” is termed “Herr” in the Age —not
even “ Mynheer ” —and recently in its theatre-
notices the Age came along with a notification
that Herr Napoleon Bouffard (!) —Herr Napoleon
of all names in the world —was about to appear in
the Bijou Theatre. When a Frenchman is called
“Herr” by a great daily, one may expect to find
the statement that Herr Ah Yip was fined for
keeping a dirty cabinet-making shop, or that Herr
Andropolus Demetrius has opened a new oyster
emporium. The views of Herr Ram Chunder
on the Alien Immigration Act will be carefully
given in the next column to the account of Herr
Antonio Garcia’s death in the bull-ring at Madrid.
It is only the Englishman who seems to rise above
the common Herred in the opinion of David
Syme.
* * * •
Lord Hopetoun’s arrival in Perth would
have been a positive frost but for Providence
sending a day so hot as to thaw “ most anythin’.”
The ’Gropers groped vainly after enthusiasm.
The streets were decorated with weatherbeaten
remnants of the York visit, The G.G. looked a
very hard-worked hack as he passed bareheaded
in the broiling sun, bowing for all the world like
a mechanical Xmas toy. The whole procession
reminded one forcibly of a funeral, especially
when the just-sworn-in-that-morning-to-be-in-time
Leake Ministry hove in sight, with. a gravity that
was somewhat dispelled by the optical illusion of
each member with his left closed eye and his tongue
in his cheek. The Ministry was only trying to
dodge the sun-glare, but it created the impression
above-mentioned. Bigjohn Forrest, with his har-
vest-moon face beaming as he recognised, and
was recognised by, his old friends, was the only
outwardly cheerful object there. . People said it
was a good-humored crowd ; which was wrong.
It was good-humored, but it wasn’t a crowd.
• * • *
On the Wednesday during the progress of
“the second test,” a touching sight was witnessed
in front of Melbourne Age office. Returns were
being posted for the convenience of a dusty crowd
that lined the kerb and clustered in the heat.
Just before the fall of the tenth Australian
wicket, three johnnies, who had been doing them-
selves proud, drove up in a hansom, and, seeing
the result, cheered hilariously and drunkenly,
while the dejected Australians sat with their feet
in the gutter, like sober mourners at a mother’s
funeral. Later in the day the same three
johnnies, who had put in the interval rounding up
the city hotels, drove up again in the same han-
som, and prepared to put up another cheer for
old England, when the shocking result of the Eng-
lish team’s first innings struck them full in the
eye.' They were dumb for a moment, and then
one of them—a large, fat, fair individual—dragged
his cap down over his eyes and wept aloud like a
child. This time it was the crowd’s turn to cheer,
and its howl of triumph stirred the town.

The inquest on the railway smash at
Walloway (S.A.) spasmodically eventuated and
peacefully subsided. Walloway is a siding devoid
of signals, telephone, telegraph—of everything,
except a lady caretaker, who supervises goods
and mails only. Seven miles south of Walloway
lies Orroroo, seven miles to north Eurelia. Each
of these places has a station fully manned and
equipped with every ordinary requirement. Yet
at all times, in all weathers, trains have been
allowed to cross at Walloway. The crossing has
to be done without signals, except such as are
made by the guard first on the spot, who
waves his lamp and yells if necessary. . The in-
cline there is one of the steepest in . S.A.,
and one damp, dark, boisterous night a train slid
past the dismal siding just in time to run into. the
other train approaching. It was the sort of night
when one might fail to find a cathedral without a
light—let alone a dog-box station in a black wil-
derness. S.A.- is too depressed on account of
grasshoppers, droughts, &c., to exactly locate the
responsibility, so it says the driver is to blame.

Frequent bursting of Sydney water-mains
(three big bursts in a fortnight) seems to suggest
that the supply has other weak points, in addition
to the leaky channel between Prospect and Potts
Hill.

Early on Christmas morning in Melbourne
a homeless woman, in the pangs of childbirth,
crawled into an outhouse. She was graciously
allowed to stay there, and, about an hour later,
there was found a new-born babe already dead
and the mother in extremis. The woman was
then rushed off to the hospital—but. too late to
save her life. This was in a Christian country
on Christmas morning.
The South Australian squabble between
Chief Justice- and University Chancellor Way
and musical Professor Ives shows the danger of
allowing judicial personages to be anything but
judges. As Chancellor of the University Mr.
Way is alleged by Mr. Ives to have attempted to
improperly influence, on behalf of his niece, the
award in a musical examination. Whether the
accusation is true or not, no Chief Justice should
be in a position which brings him within ten
leagues of such a squabble. The ideal Chief
Justice would have neither wife, nor child, and
would disown all his nieces, and his male relatives
would be exiled. But the world doesn’t grow
ideal Chief Justices. Still, there are limits.
Way’s outside billets have now placed him in this
position, that he couldn’t hang Ives (assuming the
impossible proposition that Ives required hanging)
without some people wondering if he felt glad to
do it, by reason of the old squabble ; and he
couldn’t instruct the jury to acquit Ives (if we
could imagine anything so impossible as Ives
requiring acquittal) without other people wonder-
ing if he wasn’t trying to propitiate an enemy who
might otherwise be nasty in the interval before
the execution.

The usual 22-year-old youth turned up to
fire the inevitable revolver at the perennial 18-
year-old girl at Fitzroy (Melbourne) the other
night. He proved a fair shot for an amateur, but,
the weapon being a cheap and nasty German
built affair, the three bullets he fired at the girl
and at himself merely bounced off their skulls and
left them with flesh wounds that might have been
caused by running against a clothes line. The
passionate lover in this case was a Broken Hill
lad, who had met the lady when she was per-
forming at the Barrier with a variety co., and
had followed her to Melb., vowing, after the man-
ner of Arthur Styan in a Blandolt melodrama,
that she should be his. He also left a letter
betraying gross ignorance of the ways of cheap
revolvers, for,in it he stated that “both Connie
and he would be dead ere it was received.” Before
even the letter was found, Connie had had her
wound dressed, and was confiding to a sympathetic
reporter how she would 11 never marry a man that
could not keep her, and, besides, she didn’t like
Frank well enough.” Frank is awaiting his
trial for attempted murder, and probably realises
by now that a seven-and-sixpenny revolver is only
intended to excite the persons it is fired at, and
make them write to the papers.
* * * *
Strictest watch and ward is kept nowadays
over the Victorian Parliamentary mace, to pre-
vent any more tricks with the elaborate bauble.
When members go out for dinner, an attendant is
left in the chamber, with strict instructions not to
take his eyes off it, and when Speaker Mason
leaves for the night, a myrmidon with a Jenkins
beard carefully stows it in the coal-cellar or some-
where. Also, it is alleged, all the approaches to
Little Lonsdale-street are watched by Parliamen-
tary detectives.

Silverton, once the hub of the Barrier, now
sans council, sans people, sans almost everything,
still has a brewery ! The streets to-day are chiefly
populated by goats ; the houses (what are left) by
crows and other birds. The town depends on a
few gasping “ outside shows” and those Broken
Hillites who are lured thither on Sundays and
holidays by the prospect of sitting under the gum-
trees or eating a little fresh fruit in the creek
gardens.
A Sydney evening newspaper, on Friday,
20th, had an article headed “Not a Drop this
Morning,” referring to the suburban water-famine,
and alongside an article on the execution of the
Queanbeyan criminal, Campbell.
* * * *
One of the recent Vic. bush-fires (in the
Kilmore district) started through some picnickers
leaving their fire alight. They were bathing gaily
in a creek when they saw that the fire had taken
possession, and rushed out, naked but valiant, to
battle with the flames. Their efforts were un-
successful, and when they left off and looked for
their clothes, they found only some scorched boots
and ashes. The Argus, which tells the story,
doesn’t explain how the picnic got home, but, if
it got home with a branch in front of it, and then
was severely reprimanded in the Town Hall by
the mayor and aldermen before its other suit was
sent for, it might have been a fair thing.
» * * *
Sydney’s system of misgovernment is just
now charmingly illustrated in Upper Hunter-sb.
Last month this street was put into thorough
repair at great expense and a steam roller gave it
devoted attention for some three days. The fine
new surface was admired for just one day when
the tramway authorities arrived and tore up the
street to lay an electric wire conduit. Now
the thoroughfare is a wreck, and the money spent
on its formation has been absolutely wasted. _ _Of
course, when half-a-dozen different authorities
have control of the streets, and don’t give each
other notice when they are coming to dig big
holes, things like this must happen.
* • * *
There have been travelling across N.S.W.
during the past few weeks sundry roving
gypsies—cadging if they can, and stealing if they
can’t cadge. They pitched their tents for a week
at Parramatta, and during that time householders
had to use a trifle more than words to drive them
off the premises—in fact, one man had to protect
his property with a poker. A more dirty, slovenly,
unwholesome set of beings would be hard to find.
The hand says it is hound for Bananaland. The
gypsy is one of the world’s mysteries, and -is
among the dozen or so races whom Biblical cranks
have identified as the lost Ten Tribes—though as
the people in Israel’s old stamping-ground around
Samaria say, and always have said, that they are
the Tribes and that they never were lost, this
isn’t a matter of much importance. If the
however, are the alleged ten tribes —who were
only nine after all, if not less —then a lot of the
dignity drops out of the fall of Hoshea’s kingdom.
Doubtless Shalmanezer and his Assyrians were
only looking for their stolen frying-pan.
* * * *
An East Melb. family awakened the other
day to find it had been burgled, and, on attempt-
ing to descend the stairs to give the alarm, it
found a strange bloodhound owning the lower
part of the house. The family spent a hungry
morning endeavoring to lure the dawg from his
post, but without avail ; and, at last, one strate-
gist hit upon, the idea of calling on the assistance
of the general public from the front balcony. For
the next half-hour, East Melb. was treated to
the sight of a respectable, staid family gathered
on the balcony yodeling to the world at large.
At length a passer-by asked the meaning of the
disturbance, and the upshot was that a force of
six neighbors armed with axes burst in at the back
door and drove the intruder growling into the
street. Seeing that the dog got for the burglar
about four hours start, it seems probable that the
animal knew more than he cared to mention.
OUTSIDE SYDNEY BLIND ASYLUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw three maids that stood, and wept to see
Blind faces in the long, grey balcony.
I said : “ Alas, poor blind 1 praise Heaven for sight,
My little maids ; for we at least have light.”
And through the languid gums and loitering wind
I heard a low, soft sigh: “ Alas, poor blind 1 ”
I turned, and saw strange other faces three,
Who gazed, in tears, at those three maids and me.

During the holiday season, with its pic-
nics and other opportunities for the harmless,
necessary cuddle, winsome girls and buxom
widows find the P.D. figure gets there every
time. No right-thinking man can resist it. To
own a waist whose beautiful curves exercise a
mesmeric influence over the male mind should
be every woman’s aim. A spinster who doesn’t
wear a P.D. corset is risking her fair chance to
reach the matrimonial goal.*

A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, January 7, 1902.
My Dear Arini,—
A Happy New Year to you. I take it that,
with the majority of persons, you started the first
day of a. D. 1902 by formulating a series of good
resolutions. You would work certain hours and
play at specified times, you would measure your
food, and walk six miles a day ; any crevices you
found in your life’s grand, sweet song you would
plug with high-thinking. It is easy enough to lay
out a path (leading to the stars) of good intentions,
hut try to walk in it and it turns to an iron string
—and greased at that. Hence you have relapsed
mto your habits of the last year and last century.
.now, just look at . No, don’t ; life’s too short,
our climate too soporific, and I want to speak of
things immediate.
of Sydneysiders spent their New
V ear’s Day at the A. J.C. races at Hand wick, and
lost money—the winning horses were so “dark.”
A huge crowd, and well-dressed. The lawns had
>een recently shaven and a strong wind carried
'! r -y grass shavings about all day in blinding pro-
fusion. This race-meeting is not one where you
expect to see anything fresh in the way of fashions,
but there were some new frocks. A couple of
mauve voiles looked winsome when in contrast
with the grass floor over which they trailed. A
red muslin with many tiny frills was a general
favorite, and a deep cream lace with raised flowers
and scrolls, worn with a blue plateau hat, must
have given its wearer the balmy peace that comes
«<f feeling well-dressed.
A tall, handsome woman who has hitherto worn
only Parisian-made clothes and emeralds showed
how attractive a white linen costume and gold
belt may look on wcunen of her build and blondi-
tude. "
At Highland Society’s concert on New Year’s
mght every seat was filled and every doorway
crowded. The Soc. will surely add a big sum to
the £l2OO it already has in its stocking. It is not
strictly Scotch in membership, foryou may belong
to it even with the name of Mick Gilhooley. A
small section of the audience hailing from Bally-
whackmaquew, in the south of Ireland, went to
this concert with a hope of hearing its two
favorite ballads, “ Logie o’ Buchan ” and the
Land o’ the Leal,” and when it found these
were not on the programme it left early, saying
that the programme should have been arranged
by Irishmen. But the air was electric with
Highland and Lowland enthusiasm—the audience
had spent the day at the Highland sports, and
taken several drops of barley bree for the
day s sake. Everyone looked as if he could hug
his neighbor, or lend money without an I O U.
There were kilts and tartans and sporrans and
bits of heather. f
When Mr. Sykes Avas fantasia-ing on Scotch
airs, how the patriotic feet tapped time on the
floor, and how face turned to face when the organ
gave out bars of “Scots Wha Hae” or “Blue
Bells of Scotland,” and there were nods of under-
standing exchanged between persons who knew not
each others’ names. It was a musical Freemasonry,
wu cer^a * n bars were the brotherhood’s grip.
When Mr> Mont gomery came along with the
Lregors Gathering” I looked round and
tried to spot some of the famous clan—that clan
that lived on a wedge of land between Highlands
and Lowlands the clan of Rob Roy, which was
the terror of its neighbors, lifting their maids and
cattle—the clan that said “ My troubles !” when
the English Government forbade it to carry a
knife, save one with a round point wherewith to
cut its food. “ If they rob us of name, and pursue
us noth beagles, give our roofs to the flame, and
our flesh to the eagles. ‘ Grigalach ! ’ ” sang Mr.
Montgomery (That’s one for the Sassenach), and
i l rt§T iri £f by the frantic applause, all present were
M Gregors, and were quite ready to “ Grigalach ”
there and then. (
.. Mr. Alec. Edward fought the “ Hit Ale of Stir-
ling m a niusicianly sense, and got great spoil
°u a Pp^ aus ? an d encore. The Scottish choir and
the Feldwick quartette gave some braw selec-
tions, and the foreign star, Dolores, shone over
the heather and the tartan. Everyone knows
the smiling, dimpled Dolores is one of the lead-
lng half-dozen singers of the world, and that
with her wonderful vocal mechanism she can get
effects of which only some of the six are capable,
but I don’t think she could sing a pathetic
Scotch song to leave a Scotch eyelash wet. It
is the lack of the “little more”—which, in
tt 3 • i case - means not being born a Celt.
Until Dolores sang “Cornin’ thro’ the Rye”
* thought I knew the body that was kissed
and didn’t cry. She wore a snood and kirtle and
a few freckles, and was winsomeness itself. Now
I see her as a high-born, polished coquette in a
Paris-made tailor costume. Mr. E. Stevenson’s
violin solo was a splendid haggis of national airs,
and was encored. Miss Kate Hancock in “ Banks
of Loch Lomond,” and Miss Cathie Duff in the
Auld Scotch Songs,” and Mr. George Grimm
(who conducted) in the “Kail Yard,” got a full
Scotch welcome and encores. The dancing exhi-
bition, by little girls Macdowell and Abbot and
little boys Sharpe and Bellamy, was capital; and
then the audience went home to tak’ a cup o’
kindness.
