Grand opera is an unhappy hunting-ground for
dress copy, if one excepts the ballet, which is
generally bewildering and undressy. The operatic
grande dame, as, for example, Signorina Marco-
mini, in La Giaconda,” mostly wears a long one-
piece white brocade, stiff with silver embroidery.
It she has to meet her lover by nightfall (a very
frequent situation), she dons a long one-piece
black velvet robe, with a wisp of funeral-black
round her head. Benedetto, the magnetic, in her
long, fair plaits, her short dress of black and
crimson, with her dilly-bag hanging near her toes,
her dagger and rosary at the waist, looks an
inspiration for an Italian poster. Dagger and
rosary seem as necessary a finish; to the Italian
opera as illicit love and religious passion. The In-
quisitor s wife calling for help to the Virgin when
she finds her husband knows her to be faithless,
recalls a story of poet Browning’s, told of a girl in
the Brownings’ lodgings somewhere in Italy. They
round that she regularly stole their tea, which
they bore with, but rebelled when they found
she likewise stole their candles. Yet they were
mollified when they found that she stole their
canales in order to burn them before a little
shrine in expiation of her sin in stealing their tea.
A crowded house at the opera on Saturday. The
new mayoral clan _ were in the vice-regal box,
and looked the kind to fill the public eye and
public billets to advantage. They have Roman-
esque noses, impassive faces and well-developed
figures.
Mr. Sid. Long has retired from the Society of
Artists presidency, and Mr. D. H. Sourer is
elected to that onerous, honorable, but moneyless
post. Both Souter and Long received autograph
Xmas and New Year greetings from ex-Governor
Beauchamp, wiio says he still retains much in-
terest m the Society of Artists.
Bananaland’s most prominent cricketer and
toe-balier “Paddy” Carew—came over to Syd-
ney Xmas week, and, at the bride’s home (The
Gleyes, Parramatta River), married the accom-
plished second daughter of Mr. Laurence Foley.
The other day a girl employed at one of the big
city dining-rooms suddenly left her pots and pans
and went off into hysterics of the highly-colored
scream-order. During the riot which ensued, four
others followed suit in milder form. For a space
the floor of the cook’s room was strewn with,
shrieking damsels, until the manager, exerting his
authority, had the “primary cause ” carried out
to the ambulance, wrapped in tablecloths—the
girl’s companions had torn off most of her clothes
and their own in the excitement. A crowd of
some hundreds had collected about the ambulance
waggon, and when it was explained that the
patient was only an. “hysteric ” they felt aggrieved,
for the commotion inside had justified grave hopes
that it was homicide at the very least. One
sufferer was locked up in a room by order of the
irate manager, till such time as she chose to get
over the hallucination that the electric tram had
just run over two men, her own mother, and a.
dog !
A gentleman who more or less recently married a
chorus-lady is proving the truth of the adage about,
love being blind. He is wandering from one swagger
hotel to another with his bride trying to get one
where people won’t stare at her. They stare her
blind, he complains. Every beautiful woman lias,
to put up with that kind of annoyance, but it can
go too far, and all the time it’s not the beauty that,
rivets attention, but the quantity of the make-up
on the bride’s face.
Now that skirts short enough to clear the
ground are the promised vogue, it is interesting
to mark the ingenious arguments that fashion-
writers from London a,re urging in their favor.
Says one, “the short skirt makes women look less
tall, and girls, whose husbands (“girls” is good) are
of less stature than themselves, will gladly choose
the opportunity of lessening the difference between
them.” Yet, the “ opportunity ” was there all
along for tall “girls” with short husbands. On
the same line of reasoning married “ girls ” whose
husbands are.taller than themselves ought to keep
to the long skirt. Another prophetess from the Big
Smoke tells of the simplicity and beauty of pre-
sent-day millinery. “Insight, comprehension,
and knack are imperative,” she says (thank
Heaven “Over Soul” isn’t mentioned). “A
swirl of the wrist to catch your fold of velvet at
the right place, a quill thrust through the founda-
tion, a flash of mauve to light up the face and tilt
the brim. At the back, a harmony of lace and
sable, and mauve or brown, arranged to suit your
individuality the whole, poised on a well-coiffured
head, and you won’t be out of harmony with the
symphonies around you.”
Young Australia hasn’t a reverential attitude
towards old Father Xmas as English picture-
books show him. A Sydney book-shop had hired
& F.X. to advertise the season’s cards. Dressed
in a Druid’s robe, a flowing white beard and hair,
holding a staff decorated with holly berries, the
man marched to and fro on the pavement for days.
At lunch-time Young Australia surrounded himi
with bread pellets and queries as to the hair
tonics he used, and entreaties to go and be shaved.
Finally, F. X. got wild, and fell upon the scoffers
with such fury that a blue uniform came andl
locked him up. I hope he’ll be tried by a hairy,
bearded Judge. Only from such will he get
sympathy.
Here is a delightful study of the convenances
(from a feminine point of view) hailing from
.Tasmania, where convenances grow like apples,
sturdily and luxuriantly ! He was dying; the
doctor had told him there was no hope ; and the
clergyman came with offers of consolation. “Yes,
sir, there’s one thing I’m a little troubled about.
Me and my missus have been rubbin’ along all
right these 35 years and never thought of getting
married. I think we might as well, for the
children’s sake, fix up things CQrrect now as I’m
gom’.” The parson went out in a shocked hurry
to arrange for the nuptials and brought back with
hirn an elderly deaconess, or something, to act as
witness. The children (youngest of them was 20)
were absent. At the conclusion of the ceremony
the blushing bride apologised to the witness : “I’m
sorry Miss I haven’t got a drop of wine or a
bit of cake to offer you.” So there was no
wedding-breakfast.
W rites my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ After the magnificence of the Maddens the
comfyness of newly-arrived Clarkes is most sur-
prising. Would-be toadies prepared to gush ad.
lib. are rather despondent at the quietness of the
sonsie trio, father, mother and daughter, all of
the quiet, steady-going, middle-class English type.
Croquet is their hobby, and, after the wild and
whirling raptures of ping-pong and golf, croquet
is slow. The game is ‘coming in’ in England,
because all the middle-aged dowagers who will
pose as young charmers find tennis and other
active sports rather too hard on their poor, stiff
old knees. At croquet one can go slow and
rest a bit now and then. But we ‘ down-
underers ’ are rather too frisky to care for an
out-of-door play of such tameness, and croquet
languishes. Still, perhaps, sassiety will begin to
knock about balls with mallets now that the
Clarkes are known to fancy the game.
“ In Melbourne sassiety several new faces have
come to the fore recently. Among the season’s
“ buds ” there are. two or three real beauties ready
to rival the knightly Judge’s daughter, Mel-
bourne’s acknowledged belle. And now they
have seen that the new Miss Clarke won’t make
them look faded or jaded they perk up still more.
In high-toned Hawthorn, Miss Maggie Lilley, a
connection of the numerous McMurtrie clan, and
niece of W. Knox of the astounding hats, is con-
sidered queen of the cake walk. Like the widow of
Cliveden, she is at her best mounted. Brighton holds
a good hand in the beauty pack, as the marine
suburb boasts handsome Miss D’Arcy Lane and
Miss Ruby Webb, also a coming-on daughter of
Albert Yuille, of the well-known horsey family.
11 There is a movement on foot to get up fashion-
able riding-parties for the summer. So far,
nothing has been decided upon. One remembers
the sudden termination of the last series. Then
the presiding Diana found her horse so fresh that,
at the first rustle of a leaf, the fiery steed bolted,
and fled with elevated tail over the rim of the
horizon. One of the Hotspurs of the party pur-
sued the runaway. He found the road strewn
with indications of the bolt —a handkerchief, a
hat and a certain amount of chestnut hair. He
gathered these tilings together, and, at last reach-
ing the exhausted steed and furious rider, pre-
sented the upholstering materials to their rightful
owner. She presided at no more riding parties.
Ihe hair broke her up—the other detachments
might have been overlooked.
“The recent marriage of Colonel Tom Price’s
only daughter to the eldest son of a N.S.W.
station-owner is quoted as a fresh instance of the
matrimonial luck vouchsafed to the girl-friends
and protegees of widow Clarke of Cliveden. The
cordial Dowager, herself, had a stroke of fortune
in her day, when, as an attractive governess, she
married her widower employer, the late William
Clarke, Bart. Unlike most women who get on in
the world, the ex-governess has always done her
best to boost she-friends into the front row via a
brilliant marriage, or, at all events, to suit them
happily in the husband line. Lady Charles Scott
(formerly Miss Ryan), the once Miss Murphy
(now a Countess), Mrs. Claud M‘Donald
(Melbourne’s sometime society costumiere), Mrs.
Prank Clarke, nee Cotton, are all cases in point.
Mrs. Ainslie Mills, nee Price, came under the
influence of the Mascotte’s lucky star when Pa
went to S’Africa and left his motherless chick at
hospitable Cliveden, to find her engaged on his
return.
“Nardry, the Mills’ fine place near Hay, is one
of a group of luxurious station homesteads,
reminiscent somewhat of the stately homes of
Camperdown. One of these ‘ desirable residences,’
to use auctioneer’s parlance, is the future home of
Canon Carlisle’s pretty daughter, who, visiting
the Hay district, met her fate in the person of
Squatter Learmouth, whom she recently married,
and with whom she is now honeymooning in
Europe.
“Patriarchal Dr. Bevan is seeing all his olive
branches safe in the Ark of matrimony. A chubby
daughter of his was married in the Congre-
gational Church the other day; and now
news comes of his parson son—Rev. Willett Bevan
—following suit in Shanghai. The bride, in this
instance, was Miss Beatrice Vale, of Hawthorn,
who took the trip northward for the purpose of
■doing the deed.
“ A mid-summer romance stirs Melbourne
■deeply. A party of rich and well-known people,
including an engaged couple, went off for
a short sojourn in a holiday district. The
quiet proceedings there were suddenly broken up
by the engaged girl eloping with the gardener
who tended the flower-beds of the hostelry
where all were domiciled. The fugitives passed
through Melbourne a few days after their flight,
and that is the latest news to date. Meanwhile,
the switched-off one hangs his hat on a weeping
willow tree.
“Dani, the youthful and inaccessible tenor of
•J. C. Williamson’s co., claims that cycling
is good for the voice, and attributes the
preservation of his thrilling high C to con-
stant exercise on his bike. Dani makes a
very graceful entrance in the last act of ‘Fedora’
on his bicycle, and to see an operatic hero wheel-
ing himBelf into the denouement of the tragedy is
refreshingly novel. He is still under 30, and his
wife is likewise. She is her husband’s assistant,
and often follows him to the wings with his cloak,
feathers, or any little stage property he may need.
And Dani never chats to anybody but madame
—when she is there.
“ The uncorseted figure is at large in Melbourne.
It has come to ‘ stay ’ for some time. A deep
inside belt serves as the line of the equator.
Above and below, the figure ‘ sinks and swells ’
like Nora Oreena’s, ‘as Heaven pleases. ’ A Rip
Van Winkle, used to the boned and moulded form
of long ago, would be horrified to witness some of
the perambulating joys of Collins-street—the
sloppy, jelly-like forms divine, or the billiard-
table kind that revel in a dead level and not an
elevation that would cast a shade fit for a hot and
fainting fly. In the case of the happy medium
figure, the absence of the whalebone-squeezer is
rather a success. All the same, corsets are seem-
ingly an indestructible institution.
“The gossamer fever rages. Every woman whose
face glows like roast mutton under the fierce
summer sun takes refuge in a gossamer, and every
school-girl of ambitious tastes envelopes her hat
and head in a protecting veil of the same. Through
the silky textures the beetroot complexion assumes
a soft, satiny look, and the sallowest skin borrows
a peach bloom. The effect is marvellous.
“The girl with the cricket ci-aze got very bad
as soon as Clem. Hill began to score. On Friday
morning the Australian colors flared on many a
dress and hat in Collins-street. The most splashy
thing in badges was a tie in yellow and red tones
with a kangaroo sprawling on the broad beam
end. At the last test in which the Australians
figured in England, a leading mercer made a
special line of these kangaroo ties, and all the
Australians in the Big Smoke attended Lords’
with them on. The particular one this scribe ob-
served on Friday morning had been kept in cam-
phor since then.

“Mrs. Fitzgerald, who trotted the globe a
while back in search of circus-novelties, testifies
that the circus as we know it—a horn of plenty
pouring out gee-gees, acrobats, wild beasts, and
clowns—is almost extinct in Europe. At London
Aquarium, or Paris Hippodrome, for instance, the
bill may include a living pedestal of performing
lions, a horse-act, or a handsome lady in hunting
pink putting trick terriers through their paces.
But most of the programme is devoted to gorgeous
mechanical spectacles—the arena is filled with
water; girls, got up to look like fish, with electric
stars on head and tail, swim and dive in a changing
blaze of colored light Barnum’s circus caters
for procession-loving people. Mounted ladies,
prancing ponies, ramping carnivora, combine in a
sensational street parade, to which the ensuing
performance plays second fiddle. Hagenbeck’s
circus at Berlin is something between a private
zoo and a school for educating animals, and that’s
the lot, not forgetting ‘ Circus Busch,’ another
German enterprise. The cleverest seen abroad
were orchestral seals, who—with cymbals, drum-
sticks, triangles, etc., tied to their flippers—
played in perfect time, and caught fish on their
noses between the beats.
“ In Parisian theatres, says Mrs. F., an unpro-
tected female standing at the back of the d.c.
must cling to her post like a limpet or be brushed
forcibly aside by sharp-elbowed male habitues,
greedy of space wherein to spread their shirt-
fronts and moustache-points. The returned
wanderer pronounces Berlin and Vienna the
finest cities in foreign parts, but neither there nor
anywhere else did she find her English tongue a
recommendation. In a French village someone
shouted through her keyhole ‘Dirty English,’ and
in Potsdam, a cabman, aged 95, or so told her
solemnly, “Ach ! Our Emperor Frederick, how
great, hqw noble was he till this Von Morell
Mackenzie came from England to murder him ! ’
Cabbie, evidently in sober earnest, regarded Dr.
Mac. as a hired assassin sent over by Britain to
undermine the Fatherland.
“She found John Bull in Whitechapel, &c.,
less drinky than she expected. The gin-shops
were so packed with women, and their doorways
so blocked with prams, of crying babies, that
thirsty men didn’t get a show. In Petticoat
Lane, women and girls, dancing to the music of
street organs, are worth watching. They shoulder
their legs, do the splits, and pirouette joyously,
careless of heaps of old clo’ and piles of aged
fish and fruit blocking the way and soiling the air.
“The volcanic Dr. Rentoul has a clever
daughter doing a chemical course at the Univer-
sity, dabbling in revolutionary verse at the same
time. The doctor and his daughter are great pals,
and very much akin. They discuss all kinds of
serious topics, and are much imbued with the same
notions of Oppression, Suffrage, Peace and War.
For a girl in her teens, Miss Rentoul has a strong
individuality, and has no greater admirer than her
father. There is another University prof, who
nurses the great grievance and sorrow that his
only son is exceptionally unwilling to follow in his
erudite steps.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Lady Helen Stewart has broken off her engagement
with Ketchener because of the atrocities and inhuman
treatment of the women and children in the concentration
camps in South Africa.

A.N.A. list of office-bearers for 1901:—M‘Guire,
Meagher, Doyle, Hanratty, Geraghty, Finnigan,
Murphy, Hogan, Brogan, O’Brien, Casey,

The Maoriland woman is advancing with a
vengeance. At Oamaru show lately a fair com-
petitor in the jumping and stock-horse competition
drove a pony tandem team round the ring, having
herself constructed the gig and harness.
Obit , the widow of John Davies, M.L.A.,
and afterwards M.L.C., one of the remarkable off-
shoots of N.S.W. political history. Davies was
largely responsible for getting the Council’s assent
to the Payment of Members Bill—a measure he
feared at the time and condemned all the years of
his life after.

Sam Smith, M.L. A. for Pyrmont(N.S. W.),
has announced his candidature as the trades
union representative on the Arbitration Court.
If Mr. Smith is elected, Allan Cameron, assistant
secretary to the Seamen’s Union, will contest the
Pyrmont electorate seat.
An old-age pensioner, charged recently
with drunkenness at Liverpool (N.S.W.), pleaded
“guilty,” adding, “ though I wasn’t drunk.” The
Jaypees fined him 2s. 6d., or the rising. George
Reid should see to this old-’un. He may be a
relative in the Yes-No trade.
Mr. S. H. Drew, who dropped dead at
Wanganui (M.L.), lately, was a noted naturalist
and Eellow of the Linnaean Society. Deceased
led an adventurous life in early days, and was one
of the chief hunters in the party that ran down
the Kelly-Burgess-Levy-Sullivan gang, who
slaughtered gold-seekers in Nelson district in ’6B.

Gov. Syd. Clarke was so nervous at the
prorogation of the Vic. Parliament that there
were a number of cracks in his voice. Twice he
made a false start and had to be put back by the
starter, Clerk Jenkins. Syd. in appearance, is a
queer cross between John Madden and Charlie
Ryan, of Plevna. He is said to be as poor as a
crow, and he will have to go very slow indeed.
Died, at Burwood (Sydney), on Saturday,
John Dawson, solicitor, aged 82 years. He was
admitted a solicitor in 1846, and was one of the
last four admitted in the forties. For many years
he was in partnership with the late B. C. Rodd,
who held the record as senior solicitor (1833), and
who died only a couple of years ago aged 93 years.
The law seems a healthy occupation—for those
who are in it.
The gentle, white-handed nurse breaks
out in strange places at times. Boston (U.S.A.)
has one in gaol on a charge of poisoning 12
patients. She used to borrow 6d. or some other
small sum, and then kill the patient to avoid re-
payment. Also, Dayton (U.S.A.) has a lady who
is said to have poisoned 14 people. These two
women averaged 13 murders apiece. The unlucky
number again !
A correspondent writing from Far North ;
“ Christie ” Palmerston, Australian bushman,
explorer and prospector, died t’other week
in Borneo. Christie was all over the Northern
Territory, and one township is named after him.
He was a son of Madame Caradini, the Australian
vocalist. His brother Victor (once the handsomest
man in Queensland) died suddenly in London
some years back.
“Melb.”: Died, in England, Professor
Morris, Higinbotham’s son-in-law and biographer.
Came of a clever family all round, the most
eminent member being his deceased brother,
Father Morris, a ’vert. Morris arrived in Melb.,
1875, as Church of England Grammar School
head-master, after Bromby, and proved a bril-
liant success. Natheless, the squatters ousted
him, because of his remarkable lecture at South
Melbourne, impugning private ownership of
land—lecture obviously inspired by Higinbotham.
Thenceforward the school had many dismal years,
till its resuscitation by the present head-master
Blanch. Morris founded the Shakespeare Society.

thing of a name for himself on
the English turf, is the eldest son
of ‘ Guppy ’ Robinson, commer-
cial editor of Melb. Age, also
a nephew of Prime Minister
Barton, and brother
to Arthur Robinson,
M. L. A. (Vic.).
After a brief
sojourn on

trekked to

where he
made a big

Westralian scrip,
buying Lake View
Consols, Ivanhoes and
Associateds when at their
lowest, after the ‘ Fashoda’
incident, and selling them
in the height of the ’99 boom.
Then he went to London, and
got a seat on the Stock Ex-
change there, and made another
rise out of Kaffir stocks. So
he keeps a swagger estab-
lishment at Hampstead. He
is married to a daughter
of Mr. ‘ Tom ’ James, for
many years chief official in
Melb. telegraph office, and
the gilded pair are said to
be coming soon to Melbourne
on a visit.

been denied a pension in N.S.W.
“ because the police had reported unfavor-
ably upon his moral character.” Wonderful
old man to have a moral, or immoral, character
at that age !
Rev. Tremayne Dunstan, the Sydney
“humorous parson” who, if he were not a parson,
would, he says, like to edit a daily paper (quite
the easiest thing in the world to his mind,
probably), is going to make a first venture in
journalism with an organ of “social, political and
religious progress.” There is no appearance, so
far, of the Boyne-water publication announced
some time ago by the Rev. Dill-Macky.
Tas. Bishop Montgomery, appointed Lon-
don secretary of the Society for the Propagation
of the Gospel, starts duty with a sordid row about
his screw, £lOOO a year. Certain Propagators
allege that the stipend is extravagant, though it
is said they really want another man appointed
to the billet. And the Enemy, who never stops
to grumble at his salary, is sowing tares all the
while. That is where the Enemy comes in.
One of the London Snips or Bits steal
papers announces that its special extra double
halfpenny Christmas number is edited by,
and produced under the supervision of, “ the
world’s greatest batsman, Prince Ranjitsinghi.”
Which would seem to indicate that the amateur
princeling is a Jack-of-all-trades when the cricket
season with its “expenses” is off and he isn’t
making an honest penny by whacking the globe
of leather.
Nicholas Lockyer, who left the Treasury
to be the head of N.S.W. Customs, has all his life
been a sort of relative-by-marriage or small
brother to that branch of the Public Service. His
first wife was a daughter of Geoffrey Eagar, one-
time Commissioner of N.S.W. Customs; his
second, married lately, is daughter of Dr.
Wollaston, head of Commonwealth Customs ; and
his father, Major Lockyer, had to do with the
Customs in Westralia.
Purkiss, who helped the downfall of
Westralian Premier Morgans by capturing Minis
ter Frank Wilson’s seat at Perth, is a Tasmanian
who spent 27 years in Maoriland. In 1879 he was
a candidate for Hokitika, but retired in favor of
the then unknown Seddon to avoid splitting the
democratic vote. As a result Seddon got his first
chance in Parliament. Purkiss has been wonder-
ing for 22 years whether he is the man who made
Richard of Kumara or not.

Speaker Jenkin Coles has now sat m the
chair of S.A. Assembly for 11 a years. Despite
the monotony of the billet, and the heat and bur-
den of the costume, Speakers last long. George
Kingston was Speaker in S.A. for 18 yeai sin aff,
Robert Officer lasted 16 yeans in Tasmania,
Francis Murphy (Vic.) and Maurice O’Rorke
(ML) 14 years each. Arthur M'Alister, of
Queensland, was the most temporary man in the
crowd; his tenancy of the stuffy chair and the
suffocating petticoat only lasted seven months.
Professor Ives, of the Elder Conserva-
torium of Music, Adelaide University, who has
charged Chief Justice Way with trying to induce,
him in ’97 to raise a niece of the latter from class
111. on the pass list to class 1., doesn’t look like a
fighting man. He is slight and brief of stature,
with a sallow complexion, a loose mouth, and
mutton-chop whiskers, and is devoid of any pro-
nounced mental drift or eccentricity. Unlike the
big, hairy, sarcastic, incomprehensible Marshall
Hall, he is not a “personality.” But he has a
square jaw among the colorless features above
enumerated, and there are always possibilities
about the man with the square jaw.
The secret of the stiff and starchy
deportment of the famous Madden frock-
coat seems to have been discovered by a
critical tailor, who watched His Ex.
leaving Melb. Cricket Ground the other
Saturday, and studied his appearance
from every point of view. The Wax.’s
walking waist is achieved, says this ex-
pert, by the womanly device of a band of
webbing, or elastic, passed through
loops inside the back of his immaculate
‘ frock ’ and fastened around the waist
underneath the vice-regal vest, if you
please. There must be cuts in the back
of the vest to admit of the band coming
next to his—linen garment. How easy
and comprehensible the Madden figure
looks when you know how ’tis done !
And how embarrassed the beautiful
potentate would be if he were suddenly
required to take off his coat and make
himself useful at a picnic, say !
“ Veteran ” : Re selection of General
Hutton as Federal commandant. What Australia
wants is an organiser and administrator to give
its army a fair start, and in neither capacity has
Hutton ever been tried. In one case—that of the
Close court-martial—he was more or less respon-
sible for a very bad failure. Apart from the
merits of the case, the court which tried Captain
Close was illegally constituted, in having for
president the Quartermaster-General, an officer
of the permanent staff. The (then) Queen’s
Regulations (which by an Act of the N.S.W.
Legislature have the force of law in that State)
provide that no officer whose name is not on the
roster for ordinary duties can serve on a court-
martial. No staff-officer’s name is on the duty-
roster, and, therefore, if a staff-officer takes part
in a court-martial its proceedings are illegal, and
its findings void. General Hutton apparently
didn’t know this. As for his few months’ experi-
ence in command of a brigade in S’Africa, merely
carrying out the plans of others without any
independent authority, this proves no fitness for
command upon “ his own.” [But think of his gift
of blanky ! — Ed. B.]

Victoria’s two new bishops—Langley, of
Bendigo, and Armstrong, of Wangaratta —are both
Irishmen, though early Australianised. They were
educated, the former at Moore College (N.S.W.),
latter at Geelong and Melb. Universities.
Langley first went to Bathurst, then Orange, next
Balmain, then to St. Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney.
Bishop Moorhouse brought him over to Prahran
(Vic.) in ’7B, and he left there in ’9O for the arch-
deaconry of Gippsland. Four years later he was
archdeacon of Melbourne. Armstrong’s rise was
more rapid. Leaving the Crown Law department
in ’76 for the Church, he started, in ’Bl, as curate
at the superfine Christ Church, St. Kilda;
became vicar of St. Colomb’s, Haw-haw-thprne,
in ’B3, and left that to succeed Langley in Gipps-
land. A brother of Langley’s is an archdeacon in
Sydney, with a good chance of a bishopric.
W. V. Robinson’s retirement from the
post of clerk of the Victorian Assembly was hailed
with delight by many metropolitan pressmen.
Originally a pressman, Robinson turned dog badly
on the members of his old calling. City reporters
in quest of news could not afford to throw the ink-
bottle at Robinson, but they seldom approached
his lordly “pussun” without being snubbed. In
his wig and knee-breeches, and with his stiff side-
whiskers, Robinson looked as if he had stepped
out of Thackeray’s “ Jeames’ Diary.” He came
out to join the Argus reporting staff from the
Yorkshire Post 40 years ago, and was of the old,
heavy school of newspaper men, who never had to
be reprimanded for spelling a name incorrectly or
putting in a wrong initial. Early in his Parlia-
mentary career Robinson quarrelled with the
somnolent Jenkins of the Leg. Council. Robinson,
being much oftener awake than Jenkins, was
responsible for the bigger half of the animosity
which ensued.

New South W ales Government is gradually
supplanting the male with female shorthand-
writers and typists ; the rate of pay, commencing
at £125 per annum for a competent girl, is good,
New positions in the Government are constantly
being advertised, and the remarkable fact is that
all the successful candidates have been trained at
one establishment, the Metropolitan School of
Shorthand of this city. This school is conducted
by t!,e United Typewriter and Supplies Co., of
114 Pitt-street, Sydney.*

KITCHENER
(From Whyte Melville's “ Centurion.”)
Of Rabbi, scribe, or written law
But little did he know or care ;
Believed in things alone he saw
And trusted harness more than prayer ;
Had lively faith in proven steel
When wielded by a practised hand,
But moral force could hardly feel
Or moral doubts could understand ;
Nor patience found for these, nor ruth,
Who loved on dogmas to refine,
And to the question—“ What is truth V’
Had learned to answer from his youth—
“ A thousand men in line.”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“Trotter” : Between Townsville and
Ingham I met recently a Kanaka and a white
woman stumping it barefooted. They only
carried a small bundle each, and the woman
struck me for flour, tea, sugar and tobacco.
Fancy battling for a nigger !

“Wild W.” : He a blind dog working
sheep (“ C.H.R.,” B. 20/7/’01). On- Naranghi
Park Station, Riverina, five years ago, 1 saw a
blind slut work sheep as well as any other dog on
the station. She would run-in the killing-sheep
any time they were wanted. I also saw her in the
yard when we were drafting sheep, and she would
always drive the sheep from the forcing yard
through the race, and keep them running all the
time.
Frank Allan, the Vic.
“bowler of century,” a good
bushman, on one occasion had
jumped down into the bed of
a sandy creek, and while in
mid-air saw a snake beneath
him. His feet landed on
what may be termed the
snake’s neck. He had the
presence of mind to press
down hard into the sand,
the reptile meanwhile
winding itself around
his leg with the idea
of gaining a purchase to draw its
head out. Allan quickly got out
his knife and severed the snake’s
head. His sister, when a child
on a Western district station,
used to go every morning to the
dairy and get a saucer of milk.
At last, out of curiosity, she was
followed and seen to lay the
saucer down beside a huge log; presently a
black snake emerged and began to drink the
milk, the child sitting beside it watching
with interest. There was intense grief when
the snake was dispatched to the home of its
fathers.

“ Jaynol ” : Be bell-birds. These will
dwell in the same locality for years, perhaps for
life. Fifteen years ago I heard bell-birds on the
road to Lake Tyers (Vic.), and when I re-visited
the place last year the birds were still piping in the
same place. Near Drouin, on the Jindivick-road,
I heard bell-birds in the same half-dozen bushes
for five years consecutively, and where I am now
living I have noted their haunt for four years.
They are only found in small bushy timber. In
South Gippsland they do not affect sapling-
country ; but, as it takes two men to see to the
top of one of our saplings, I would not make any
affidavit on the matter.
Dear Bulletin.— Re Norman Lindsay
having depicted a saddle lying on its flaps. F. P.
Mahony has done ditto, in your Xmas number, in
the picture of an old sundowner asking for a feed.
Again, re those Commonwealth Annual pictures,
what about the one wherein are seen two rows of
shearers hard at it on one floor, in a shed where,
to all appearances, there is room for only one lot
of men ? How are the men on the left to get their
sheep from the pens, and where do the men on the
right let their sheep go to when shorn ? The
shearers on the left are all right, and seem to be
doing good work, but those on the right should, I
think, be on the other side of the shed, and, of
course, out of sight. I may be wrong, as it is not
conceivable that the picture would be hung in the
National Art Gallery of N.S. Wales unless cor-
rect in every detail; yet I should very much like
to see a plan of that shed.—Scar

“ Werrimee ” : You say, “ When you get
the snapshot (of Too lapping water) send it along;
the picture will tell.” But the picture won’t tell.
It will show the ’roo apparently drinking as a
horse or cow does; for the ’roo holds his mouth on
the water, and the tongue darts in and out rapidly
and almost silently—not like a dog’s. Have often
sat, unobserved, close beside them at lignum
holes, and can swear to the lapping. M'Cooey
has probably watched the animal from far off.
[That clattering sound in the distance is the feet
of Mr. M‘Cooey rushing to explain that he has
watched the ’roo from much closer range than
“ Werrimee ” ever did, and that he doesn’t believe
“ Werrimee” ever watched it at all, except out of
a black eye and through the public-house window.!
* » * *
The recent experience of John Thomas
Edwards in Northern Queensland is a remarkable
instance of tough endurance. Edwards, on Dec.
12, left Blair Athol station to go to Alpha, a dis-
tance of 70 miles. There was no water on the
route. He lost the track, and next day was found
nearly mad for want of water and with deep
gashes in his throat and wrist: he had opened his
wrist to relieve his thirst with blood, and had
afterwards cut his throat in despair. What could
be, was done for Edwards, and he was then
carried in a buggy to Pentland, 120 miles distant,
the trip taking three days. He was fed on the
journey with raw eggs and condensed milk. After
a night at Pentland he was sent on to Charters
Towers by train, and he is now recovering in the
hospital there, The men who brought the patient
on to Pentland lost a week’s work, besides under-
going a good deal of privation on the journey.
“M.Q.” : Be bushmen not caring to
shoot “anything that talks.” It is a fact that
cockatoos and kookaburras seem to have got on
the right side of the bushmen. Nevertheless, if a
jackass should get a taste of your chickens he will
take the lot if you don’t shoot him. I recollect
when I was a kid the old man winging one that
had been raiding the chickens. Johnny dropped
into the creek, and the row he made was incredible.
We fished him out and left him on a tree-stump,
whereupon his companions came round him in
hundreds and argued the point from the surround-
ing trees. We watched him until called to dinner,
and when we came back there was not a jackass
to be seen. Even the one with the broken wing
was gone. I reckon they must have an ambu-
lance-society of their own.

“ Piccola ” : We were skiting, as usual,
round the fire—this time about dogs. Suddenly
said old Dan : “Don’t tork to me ’bout dawgs.
You or ter seen an old pointer I onct had. One

when I sees me beauty pointin’ out at the water.
Called ’im off, but ’e wouldn’t come; an’, knowin’
he had brains, I watches. Suddenly a big fish
jumps up outer the water, an’ I drops ’im. Dog
swims out and fetches him in. I opens him, and
finds a lovely fat quail inside. He was a dawg,
that! ” We tried another line.

“Gamma” : Saw this episode outside a
Westralian goldfields town. Three young men,
savoring strongly of the Salvation Army, had
returned (presumably from a futile endeavor to
obtain food or employment in the town) to the
smouldering heap of ashes that served as their
camp. The swags (with a conspicuous 8 affixed)
lay neatly rolled and strapped. The nose-bags
were slack with emptiness, except that a large
Bible bulged out of one of them. The Jast pinch
of sugar and tea disappeared into the billy, which
bristled like an angry porcupine with rags and
sticks, obviously to prevent the precious liquid
dropping thiough the holes. The mugs were
filled, but before drinking each man knelt down
and loudly prayed. In the middle of the suppli-
cation a blackfellow sneaked into the firelight and
stood over them. “0, Lord,” cried one, desperate
with hunger, “we want ” “Likem gin?”
queried the aboriginal. They paid no attention,
but continued, “food and thy blessings ”
“Gibetn damper?” demanded the unabashed nig-
ger. The eyes of the martyrs turned slowly
towards the barbarian and his feminine vandal,
who was clothed scantily in what had been a
wrapper, but was now a dish-rag. “Depart,
children of Beelzebub ! ” cried the boss
Salvationist. The gin approached, and fanned
their heads with her solitary garment. They
knelt down and prayed with extra vehem-
ence for the conversion of the two black
Philistines. While they prayed the aborigi-
nes drank the tea. I expected an explosion, but
was disappointed. The trio arose ; looked with
grief at the three empty pannikins ; looked with
sorrow at the aboriginal and his “ missus ” ; then
said, quietly, “The Lord’s will be done,” and
took up their swags and departed. And their
colored brother and sister looked after them, and
opined, in their own imperfect language, that they
were persons of deficient intellect.

“BobR.”: In eastern Riverina rabbits
are dying in thousands for want of green feed.
Grass is abundant, but it is as dry as a Presby-
terian sermon. Bunny is swarming into the gar-
dens, and even houses, in search of something
nutritious.

“ Wiljar ” : Me “ Trotter's ” choice bever-
age (8., 20/7/’01). I have inadvertently used
water out of a station tank in which 1500 sheep-
carcases were rotting, and didn’t notice anything
the matter. Most bushmen will drink anything.
Emptying a wheel-rut in midwinter is often the
preferred alternative to walking 10 chains to a
dam, or drinking out of your hat, to lying on your
belt-buckle with your whiskers in the pond. I
have even seen a gentleman, when dipping up a
load of domestio water once, stand on a bogged
dead bull to do so. But Destiny had a hand in
that.

“Waddy”: Ifc is related that away back
in the ’so’s one Bendigo mine was known as the
“Slaughter-house,” by reason of the enormous
mortality through “accidents.” A grue-
some equipment (in this case) was the
flag-pole whereon a half-masted flag
announced a “kill.” Timber
was loaded into the cage as it
moved past the surface, two
men up-ending the sticks.
About 50 per cent, stayed in the
cage, the rest missing it. “No
time to stop,” said the boss,
“and, anyway, it gets to the
bottom ! ” But even in these
days of inspection a huge dis-
tinction is made between, say,
Broken Hill and the usual pros-
pector’s hole. The mine-inspectors
pass anything in the latter case, and
the percentage of deaths of prospectors
from accident is about treble that of the
better-watched mines. Ninety per cent, of
accidents in mines come from familiarity with
danger, and will therefore always occur.
‘‘ Solo ” : Me big Murray cod (B. 15/6/’01).
In ’7B, a German fisherman near Albury used
every morning to find his cross-line (a rope
stretched from bank to bank) minus several hooks
and sinkers, while other hooks had only fragments
of fishes adhering, their remainders having evi-
dently been bitten off. The German concluded the
culprit must be a big fellow, and one evening
made preparations for his undoing : Tied a bell to
c.-l. and camped in boat close by. During night
the bell started ringing, and the German, setting
to work with his special tackling, succeeded, after
a protracted struggle, in getting a 1201 b. cod
aboard, the boat being nearly upset more than
once during the encounter. The big fish was
exhibited at the local show, and sold to the Chows
for £2 (4d. per lb.), but when, on dissecting, they
discovered the cod nearly half-full of old hooks
and sinkers, they loudly proclaimed, with many
“ whaffo’s,” that they’d been grievously diddled.
The same man, some time after, caught an 801 b.
fish that was bigger framed than the 120-pounder,
but stone-blind and poor as a parson. It had no
sinkers inside —nor anything else ! Its captor
reckoned that, but for loss of vision, “he vos go
tarn near 200-veight.” As it was, the fish’s only
hope of a feed must have been to cruise around
open-mouthed, on the off-chance of something
swimming in (there was plenty of room), but its
general aspect sufficiently indicated the poverty
of that game.

A curious feature of the big fires which
devastated the N.S.W. Blue Mountains—one of
the chief playgrounds of Australia—was the
method of attack. A fire would start by throwing
out skirmishers, little outposts of flame, in the
midst of the sombre forest depths. Then, with a
sudden rush, there would be an “ enveloping
movement,” and a big patch of country would be
surrounded by a ring of fire, the flames working
A SOFT DRINK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
By the Darling stood a shanty
Kept by well-known Jimmy Oanty
(Where the thirst was never scanty),
And the liquor,
So ’twas said,
Than the fiercest rays from Hades,
Where 800 centigrade is
Winter’s record, and no shade is,
Was far quicker
Striking dead—
Striking thirsty but unseasoned new-chums dead.
It had made the old hands shiver—
Men who’d tramped the Never-Never;
Some sought shelter in the river
When they’d had a
Glass or two.
There was someone always dying,
On the plains white bones were lying,
“ Ratty ” tramps from strange forms flying
Ever madder,
Madder grew,
From effects of Canty’s liquor madder grew.
But, one blazing day in summer,
Came a battered-looking bummer.
Canty eyed the old newcomer
With a frown and
Then a grin,
And he filled the dusty beaker
With his strongest, fiercest liquor,
And the parched one in a flicker
Reached it down and
Took it in,
With a grunt of satisfaction took it in.
Canty reckoned he’d look scary,
But he never turned a hair, he
Sighed ; then, resting on the bar, he
Leaned to Jimmy,
And said he :
“ I believe in bein’ frisky,
But them sort ’er jokes is risky,
An’ nex’ time I pay for whisky
Don’t you gimme
Bloomin’ tea;
You’ll regret it if you gimme station tea.”

inwards in the centre. Or a long, straight line of
flame would cut deep into the heart of a stretch of
valley and hill, following a path direct as the
flight of an arrow. If there were fences of dry
timber on the mountains an explanation would be
simple—that the fire followed the easily-combus-

lisation in these wild glens. In one house the
three children had been attacked by typhoid
fever; two died, and the third, whilst fighting
hard for life, had to be removed from the place
which was threatened by the forest fires. Many
gullies were turned to roaring furnaces, and much
of the mountain beauty of fern and flower des-
troyed. It will be a long time before the beauty-
spots, that are now mostly ashes, recover their old
loveliness.

“ Mulga Jack”: I see in last week’s
Bulletin where “Jack-a-Clubs” contradicts
“ Banjo ” about the calf. He says that any stock-
man with a grain of sense and a mouth can imitate
the plaintive calling of a young calf. That is
quite true ; any fool can do that, but they can’t
imitate it so as to draw the cow’s attention to it
I have seen it tried, and it is a failure. I have
seen another way tried which will sometimes fetch
the cow, if she is an inexperienced cow and it is
her first calf. Take a young calf to the place
where you think the calf is planted, then sool a
dog on to it and the decoy will start bellowing for
all it is worth. Then, as I said before, if it’s her
first calf she might come, but the old married cow
of long-standing won’t. Let a cow plant her calf
in a paddock where there is a thick scrub, and
“ Jack-a-Clubs” could go bellowing about for a
week and not find it. The best way to find it is
to watch the cow all day.

“ Canip ” : Have often seen gohannas feed-
ing on a living (bogged) bullock. The brutes
seem to get maddened with the taste of warm
blood, and very often show fight when disturbed.
Miss “ Unie ” Russell is probably the one
and only golden-haired girl in Melbourne who,
having been fully equipped for a start in theatrical
life, and accustomed to receive compliments,
verbal and vegetable, is not yet dying to go upon
the stage. As a juvenile in abbreviated frocks
she was a “ star ” among the multifarious members
of the Misses Hyams’ dancing classes, and the
accomplishment has grown on Miss Russell until
now that she is just out of her ’teens her every
pose and gesture is naturally graceful. The
ordinary stage-struck amateur of the same age
tries in vain to start where Miss Russell
leaves off. Mrs. G-. B. Lewis took the damsel
to task years ago, and a long course of
training has given strength and reach to her deli-
cate voice inflections. She has beautiful, silver-
plated tones. The other afternoon, when she
appeared in some scenes from “Romeo and
Juliet,” at Melb. Princess’s, for Mrs. Bracy s
benefit, Miss Russell looked and talked very like
an angel. Nevertheless she made what was prac-
tically her debut as an actress under the
most unfavorable conditions. It was easy tor
her to be charming, but impossible to be
quite convincing. No scenery to assist the illu-
sion, no time in which to begin to live the charac-
ter, and nothing but a little drawing-room chair to
fall against when agonies of despair were required
of her. Had she been in the mood for violent
agitation the fear of upsetting that light-comedy
chair must have given pause to the experienced
young amateur. As a matter of fact, Miss Russell
was within a few hours of falling seriously ill
when she appeared at the Bracy matinee, bhe
scored a success because her pluck is equal to her
talent, but at some future time, no doubt, this
dainty young Australian artist will reveal the
dramatic fire that is in her soul or her brain, or
wherever the dramatic fireplace may be.

KISSES and SIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AffISS now 1 while the girl is handy,—
C? You can’t when she’s far away;
Sin now! lest your life be sandy,
Oasisless and gray.
Kiss now! while the chance stands coyly,
And lingereth at your side ;
He who waits on the church step oily
May wait in vain for his bride.
Sin now ! before gloom and fatness,
And their greasy kith and hint
Have filled your years unth flatness,
And you know not how to sin.
Kiss now! while the girl's complacent;
Kiss now! before love is cold ;
Kiss now! while her mouth’s adjacent;
Kiss now! while her hair is gold.
Sin now 1 you are growing older;
Throw off all unmanly fears ;
The greyest ghosts that moulder
In the vale of our latter years
Are the haunting apparitions
Of the sins we contrived to miss,
The uncommitted transgressions—
The girls that we didn't hiss!

years ; also his employer, the cocky-farmer, is often con-
tented to make less than the pay of a city scavenger.
And the State protects or subsidises the industry by
carrying agricultural produce at about half the rate
charged on most goods, and at not over half cost.
With this State aid and miserably cheap labor, agriculture
has made headway of late without Protection

A Sportsman in Peril.
A sportsman well-known in Toolern and Melton
recently came near to losing his life in an extra-
ordinary manner (says a contemporary). The
gentleman in question, Mr. William Byrnes, of
Melton, when interviewed, said that trapping
and shooting had occupied most of his time till
two years ago when consumption and hip disease
came on. He gradually became pale and
emaciated and was weakened by a distressing
cough. At last he had to take to bed and was
subsequently removed to the Melbourne Hospital
where the diseased hip was operated upon, but he
still continued to decline and the phys'eians said
his disease was incurable. Indeed, on one
occasion a minister was called in as he seemed at
his last gasp ; but he lingered on. Then his
relatives, impressed by the number of local cures
effected by Dr. Williams’ pink pills, began
administering them to him. These pills enriched
his blood and in this way began to abate the
consumption ; little by little he gained strength
and by further steady pel-severance with Dr.
Williams’ pink pills he became cured. Now he is
actually free from every symptom of consumption
and is so well and strong that.he goes away camp-
ing and shooting in the bush for days together.
By replacing the lost blood and increasing the
resistent power of the lungs Dr. Williams’ pink
pills cure consumption and other lung troubles.
They also cure debility, neuraltria, rheumatism,
diarrhoea, kidney and liver troubles, insomnia,
nervous exhaustion in men, etc. ; sold by chem-
ists and storekeepers and by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., c/o Rocke, Tompsitt & Co. Flinders
St., Melb., two and nine; six boxes fifteen and
three, post free. When purchasing mind you
ask for Dr. Williams’ ; imitations dyed pink
cannot cure consumption or anything else.

There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who. wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low-
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on .deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45 Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Cardinal Moran, perhaps taking his tip
from The Bulletin, favors Eden-Bombala as the
site of the Federal capital.
Stated that owing to Melbourne printing
shops coming under the minimum wage law a lot
of Victorian work is coining now to cheap “ rat ”
shops in Sydney.
A N.S.W. Labor member talks of pro-
posing, at an early meeting of his party, that any
member who accepts any Ministerial hospitality
paid for out of the public money will be black-
listed for next elections.
Speaking at Wellington lately, Premier
Seddon is reported to have said, “We must
prosecute the war, because all our produce goes
past the Cape, and the route must be kept safe.”
Which, considering that every England-bound
boat from Maoriland goes via the Horn, is not a
bad sample of Jingo ignorance.
The Premier of Holland throws a reveal-
ing lijjht on the true nature of the Boer war when
he points out —
That the courts and aristocracy of Europe are all
friendly to England. The Boers have no friends in the
official world, but the sympathy of the masses with them
is increasing.
United Australia no longer flourishes
Mr. Barton’s name as a director, and, completely
under the Bruce Smith influence, is an avowed
freetrade and black labor organ. It is also very
dull and in places very silly, and there does
not seem to be much prospect of its providing an
exception to the rule with regard to Australian
magazine ventures.
Several Australian State Premiers are
said to have sent in long lists of obscure persons
said to be worthy to receive titles at the New
HOW, INDEED?
“Did you see his funny black face, mummy?”
“Hash, dear, you musn't say that. He is black all over." “How do you know, mummy?”
Year. This is the first time the regular date for
soldering handles on silly people has come round
since Edward VII. became King, and he has
straightway abolished the New Year foolery.
May he go on.abolishing.
How came it that Commandant Scheepers
was tried for ‘‘ murder, arson and train-wrecking,”
as alleged in the cables ? Murder is not an act of
fair war, but surely train-wrecking is an ordinary
enough incident. And, if the British had any
sense of humor,'they would hardly accuse any man
of arson, with lines of smouldering farms extend-
ing on all sides of the seat of judgment.
Official notification :
Mr. Frank Farneli, M.L.A., visiting magistrate to Lord
Howe Island, leaves this evening by the steamer Titus
on an official visit to the Island.
Of course, this work carries no salary, but it
carries expenses, and Mr. Farneli will be the
main, if not sole, judge as to what these expenses
should be.
Daily paper report :
The southern channel on the Clarence River bar has
again closed up, and the-eastern channel, which is not too
good, is the only one available.
How many hundreds of thousands of loan money
has been swallowed by the “ permanent and
reproductive work ” which shuts up in this off-
hand fashion?
It is reckoned, though the reckoning
seems a little doubtful, that as much as £60,000 a
year was involved in the dispute between the
Federal Customs department and the foreign
steamship companies. If the amount was even
half of that it represents a capital value of
£1,000,000, and this country can’t afford to drop
a million on anybody’s doorstep just now without
good cause.
Reply given by Imperial authority to
representations as to the advisableness of appoint-
ing Australians as State Governors is always, in
effect, a repetition of that conveyed to Canada iu
1882—“ That the appointment of a colonial would
weaken the tie with the Mother Country.” Yet
some local snobs are keen on having an English
peer as Australia’s High Commissioner ! When
we have an Australian Governor-General, but not
before.
Premier Peacock is still understood to say
that he will introduce his Bill for the reduction
and reform of_ Vic. Parliament “next session.”
And as it is to include a clause providing that all
Council electors shall be eligible to sit in the
Upper House, and thereby destroying the exclu-
sive privileges of the present gang of Landlords,
the Council will joyfully throw it out next
session. And Peacock, it is pretty safe to wager,
will take the insult quietly next session, if there is
any Peacock left by then.
Adelaide Critic asks indignantly “ what
chance is there for the unemployed to get work
during next year if the tramways” (i.e., Snow’s
privately-owned tramway scheme) “are not carried
out ?” Well, if the unemployed depend on build-
ing tramways for the Snow syndicate to keep
them alive next year, where do they come in the
year after ? If this country has got into such a
state that it can only keep a large section of its
workers temporarily out of the benevolent asylum
by getting them public works to build for foreign
money-lenders, where is this great country going
to?
The Australian cohentingent to go Boer-
baiting in a few weeks is so small that even
Jingoes admit its main purpose is to refute the
widely-printed Continental statement that Aus-
tralia is tired of the game. But when this coun-
try, after being specially requested, sends an
insignificant handful of men, and doesn’t pay
them, and Britain only gets them by giving more
than four times the wage of the best paid regular
soldier in the world, and more than most of them
could make at any other occupation, things
wear a tireder look than before the cohentingent
was offered.
At Grafton (N.S.W.) an old couple found
it couldn’t get a pension because the husband had
earned over £52 in the twelve months previous to
date of application. The Board acted strictly
according to law, and if auy old person could live
this year on the £1 a week he was earning last
year the law would be strictly logical. As things
are, when an ancient party loses his job at 30s. a
week, and is too venerable to get another, he has
to put in several months starvation before he
qualifies for a pension. And the mill, even if it
is a windmill, grinds very slowly with the water
that is past.
Kipling’s demand for conscription in Great
Britain is only a foreshadowing of what must
cpme. It takes many tons of Tommies to keep up
the Imperial theory nowadays, and Britain has
only cwts. Kipling, by the way, charges Britain
with “fawning” on its dependencies in order to
get reinforcements. Query: Does the outside
world look on all the contingents as a sign of the
strength and unity of the empire? Or does it
regard them as showing that the British army is
so run down that it can’t crush a handful of Boers
without assistance? Kip. seems to fear lest it
takes the latter view.
Judging by Marconi’s recent experiments
with wireless telegraphy between. America and
Europe the time is really not far off when the old
pole-and-wire telegraphs will go to their long
home in the scrap-heap. Then another £3,000,000
of this country’s loans for “permanent and re-
productive works ” will become temporary and
unproductive. Every debt should have a sinking
fund attached, because everything becomes, in
time, as obsolete as a dead civil servant’s salary,
or a contingenter’s 4s. 6d. Even the railway debt
shouldn’t be permanent; the railway will pro-
bably be deader than the ox-waggon before this
century is over.
Maoriland Farmers’ Union, the only Tory
organisation with any power in Maoriland, has
thrown away the one weapon with which it might
have done damage to the Seddon Govt. At the
Southland Conference some one arose to move that
“All foreign borrowing should cease.” He got a
seconder, and that was all. Hairy individuals
with . Scawteh names arose to point out that bor-
rowing, was a good thing, that it brought siller
and guid goold tae tha’ coontry, and in time the
lucre filtered intae the haunds o’ the farmers.
And then the meeting arose and waved its kilt
enthusiastically. Maoriland has begun to go it
madly in the way of loans. In 1891, its borrow-
ings were under £400,000. In 1901 they were
£1,746,141 —the worst record for 20 years.
While Seddon is
hurrying off another Maori-
land contingent to aid in the
war on women and children
in S’Africa, there are many
indications that the State
finances are drifting into a
bad condition. For in-
stance, Napier, one of the
Auckland city members,
has just pub 1 ished a re-
monstrance in connection
with the money still due to
the volunteers who par-
ticipated in the great mili-
tary display at Christchurch
in honor of the York visit
last June. Even the very
schoolboys were gathered
from all parts of the
country, and their promised
expenses are still unpaid.
Also, returned cohentin-
genters, it is alleged are
unable to get “squared-up.”
On top of the lot comes the
“sacking” of a thousand
co-operative workers, with-
out notice, at the very
time when they wanted to
earn their Xmas cheques. Jingoism is sickbed
oyer in all directions by the sordid row about the
bill and the shabby little quarrel about the odd
copper.
The proposal to hold a conference of State
Premiers and one or more Federal Ministers over
the Property Acquisition Bill moved one Sydney
paper to point out the grievous fact that no con-
stitutional channel is provided whereby the State
and Federal Ministers may communicate when
they have remarks to make to each other. This
is sad—it is almost heart-breaking, in fact. The
makers of the Constitution assumed that when
any question arose on which State and Federal
Ministers wanted to confer, they would write, or
ring each other up, or wire, or send a messenger
round to make an appointment. And tl ey
should have arranged that if Federal Trea-
surer Turner wanted to consult with N.S. W. State
Treasurer Waddell, he would instruct his secre-
tary to write to his chief, Barton, and Barton
would communicate with the Governor-General,
and he. would lay the matter before the N.S. W.
State Governor, and the latter would inform
N.S.W. Premier See, and See would inform
Waddell, and Waddell’s answer would ramble
back to Turner in the same fashion.
A Sydney correspondent :
Mr. Edwards, S.M., gave a woman recently
one month’s hard labor without the option
for assaulting a policeman. Mr. Edwards
ought to retire.
Wrong—Mr. Edwards probably did
his duty. There is no more cowardly
miscreant than the female who claws
and bites at a bobby with the know-
ledge that since he is a man and she a
woman he can’t handle her as he would
a man. As the policeman, under the
circumstances, can’t effectively help
himself, the law, when it gets a chance,
should be very emphatic indeed in
helping him.
Commonwealth Governor-
General Hopetoun has got a facer from
Judge Holroyd (Vic.), in the case of
Frederic Gerhard, alleged to be a
Swiss insolvent, who was detained in gaol under a
warrant signed by Panton, P. M., acting on the
authority of the G.-G. The Judge held that the
Vic. State Governor should have moved in the
matter, not the Gov.-Gen., and ordered the ac-
cused’s discharge.
The rumor goes around persistently in
Queensland that the Boodle Government will
dissolve Parliament this month, and go to the
country with a rush, in the hope of getting there
before the Labor party is ready. There are con-
stituencies where the Labor candidate is still to be
selected, and there are other difficulties to be
patched up. Meanwhile the Boodle party is
always ready. Its programme is simple. The
same old fossil stands again and promises every-
thing, and if anybody threatens to split the vote
he can, possibly, be persuaded to stand down by
making him a J.P. or his son a policeman, or by
some other means.
After years of bitter complaint about the
scarcity of trucks on the Victorian railways the
public begins to suspect that slipshod manage-
ment has most to do with the trouble. When a
truckful of produce is delivered the consignee is
required to take delivery within 24 hours, or else
pay demurrage. But it appears that the de-
murrage is never collected (one firm’s unpaid bill
ran to £1769 in seven months) unless the consignee
is very poor and uninfluential; so rolling stock
that is badly wanted elsewhere stands round full
of wheat or wool for a week or so till the consignee
feels inclined. In fact, he saves storage charges
by storing his goods in the Government’s trucks.
The crowd with the shorn upper-lip and
the moral mat of hair under its chin intends mak-
ing a great effort to ensure that the Federal
divorce law will amount to a practical repeal of
the divorce statutes now existing in N.S.W. and
Victoria, and a return to the old theory that those
whom the preacher has joined for a reasonable
cash consideration no man must put asunder.
Queensland, Westralia, S.A., and the small island
where the jam comes from all adhere pretty
closely to the old order of things. In ’9B—the
last year covered by Coghlan’s figures—N.S.W.
had 247 divorces; Victoria, 87; the other four
CQmmonwealth States, 21 among them. The

Sydney Board of Health, reviewing the
Parliamentary Committee report on the Coast-.
Hospital food supply scandal, “repudiates all
responsibility,” and endeavors to throw the blame-
on the medical authorities at the hospital. In
view of evidence that the Board of Health used to.
consistently'neglect the frequent complaints of
the medical superintendent, simply on the ground:
that they were frequent, this repudiation is rather
a solemn sort of joke. A Parliamentary com-
mittee of an inquiring turn of mind would get.
some valuable evidence if it were to start to con-
sider this Board and all its works, regarding
recent epidemics, food adulteration and other
matters.
Premier Seddon’s assurances that there is
no foolish sentiment re freedom of speech about
him, and that if the remarks of some of the-
English Liberal leaders had been made in Maori-
land the speakers would probably have been im-
prisoned, compare rather curiously with his bitter
hoMvls in the days just before the war about the
alleged sinfulness of the Boers in not permitting -
“freedom of speech.” By his own accounts, if
Oom Seddon, now that the mental dry rot of the -
tuft-hunter lias eaten him up, possessed the power
Oom Paul did, lie would be much the narrower-
minded tyrant of the two. Oom Paul allowed
freedom of speech to an extent that Oom Dick
says he wouldn’t do. The true Jingo is solid on
two points—that Britons shall never be denied the-
blessed privilege of freedom of speech, and that
anybody who is found speaking freely except on
the Jingo side should be in gaol.
More cheerful intelligence from Sydney
Telegraph
There is another reason for believing that the Federal
revenue will be maintained notwithstanding the reduc-
tion in the duties. It is a well-known fact that, within
due limits, low duties mean high revenue. Take, for in-
stance, the duties on woollens, which have been reduced *
from 20 to 15 per cent.' The Geelong- manufacturers com-
plain that the effect of the reduction will be to swamp the •
market with imported goods. If that is so, the revenue
must gain more from a 15 than from a 20 per cent. duty.
TheEoreign-trade paperis as full of valuable morals •
as a porcupine is of quills. (1) It says that the
proposed Federal tariff is an excessive burden on
the people ; (2) that it raises far more revenue than
is required ; (3) that the reduced tariff which the
Telegraph demands will raise more revenue, and
take more money from the people and therefore
aggravate alleged evils 1 and 2 ; (4) that it will do-
this by increasing imi orts and therefore reducing
local production and local employment. How
sharper than a serpent’s tooth it is to have a friend
that doesn’t know enough to come in out of the-
wet.


A Question
Answered.
If the Pianola ena-
bles those who
have absolutely no
musical knowledge
to play the piano,
of what interest is
it to those who are
able to play ?
This is often asked, and when asked in our showrooms, generally results in the
purchase of a Pianola ; but more often the question finds expression only in
thought, and offers no opportunity for an answer.
PADEREWSKI HAS A PIANOLA in his home.
ROSENTHAL HAS ONE.
SAUER HAS ONE.
and only recently has ordered two more for friends in Germany, who are themselves
eminent musical critics. This certainly disposes of the question, although it fails to
give the reason. The reason is equally clear.
The Pianola increases the
Pianola being placed in position to play Upright Piano.
repertory of even these musi-
cians from twenty-five to fifty
compositions which they can
play in concert form, to prac-
tically everything they may
desire to play. It increases
the repertory of every skilled
musician, and gives a reper-
tory to the novice.
The Pianola does not mar
the piano, as it may be moved
up to or away from the key-
board at will.
The Pianola does not mar
the playing, as artistic taste
receives full opportunity for
expression to w hich the player
can give his undivided atten-
tion.
The Pianola offers relaxa-
tion from the technical and
enjoyment of the musical.
It is true, since the player
controls the expression, the great musician obtains a more artistic result from the
Pianola than the novice, because he is more critical, but in this very point lies the
secret of the Pianola’s fascination.
The player, whoever he may be, gives his own. individuality to his rendition.
This causes the musical taste to develop, and it is not long before the veriest novice
finds his own playing preferable to many of the pianistic efforts in the average concert.
Expression-marks appear on the music as it unrolls, for the guidance of those who
may be unfamiliar with the selection.
In this article there has been little attempt to explain how the Pianola is manipu-
lated, the principal object being to show the desirability of the Pianola for every one
who owns a piano, whether he be skilled in its use or not.
We shall be pleased to mail a complete description of the instrument upon request,
although we urge all who have the opportunity to hear the Pianola for themselves.
Can & be bought by instalments if desired. Send for Catalogue C.
HENRY M. BIRGE & CO
Sole Agents for Australasia.
The Pianola is sold at no other
Address in Sydney.

499 Bourke-street, Surry Hills,
Sydney, Sept. 9, 1899.
Mr. PALMER.—
Dear Sir,— Four years ago I was very bad ; in fact, I
had to walk on crutches. The doctor told me I was
suffering from Kidney and Liver Complaint, and Weak,
ness in the mouth of the Bladder He gave me some,
medicine, but it did not do me any good. I saw in the
paper about a Mrs. Stranger having been cured by VITA-
DATIO, and I decided to try it. I took four small bottles
and one large one ; the result was that in six weeks I was
perfectly cured, and have thrown away my crutches.
After I commenced taking VITADATIO I gained one
stone in weight in six weeks. From that time to this I
have never taken a dose of medicine, and I am perfectly
cured of the old complaint. When I took your medicine
four years ago I was residing in Macquari e-street, Hobart.
I give this testimonial of my own free will, and you are at
liberty to publish it if you so desire.—Yours faithfully,
V. R. HURBURGH.

The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Co., Ltd., has
issued a very pretty booklet giving illustrations and
interesting facts about their business. The company
delivers some 24,000 quartz of milk daily. So if you only
mix it with whisky you can take a long, steady drink at
it, and then have plenty for your friends.*
Capillarine ! If you drop some of it on the
floor overnight, the floor will be covered with hair in the
morning, and you’ll want a lawn-mower to cut it off. We
knew a man who made a door-mat on his floor through
dropping Capillarine on it regularly. Of course the above
are exaggerations, but, seriously speaking, Craig and
Aitken, of 680 George-street, Sydney, have thousands of
testimonials from men who could never grow a moustache
until they used Capillarine. See Craig and Aitken s advt.
on page 27.*
An Australian cohentingenter to London
Morning Leader
I think that we lost more officers from our own rifles
in some cases than from the rifles of the enemy. A good
many, I feel sure, were shot “accidentally on purpose”
by men behind them. In fact, I have heard a number of
soldiers, when under the influence of drink, boasting that
they had committed deeds of this sort, and I think some
of them spoke the truth.
Government Notices.
Department of Taxation, Custom House,
Sydney, 20th December, 1901.
Land and Income Tax Assessment Act of 1895.
'\rOTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of the above
•I* Ant, that Returns of Income received during the
year 1901 for the purposes of the assessment of Income
Tax for the year 1902 are required to be furnished to the
Commissioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before
the eighth day of February, 1902, and all persons who
are in receipt of income, and all persons liable under the
provisions of the Act in any representative capacity to
furnish returns for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby in-
formed that the prescribed Forms of Returns are now
available, and may be obtained (except those for
Companies) on application at any Post 0(/ice, Police Office,
Custom House, Railway Station, and the Office of any
Clerk of Petty Sessions in New South Wales, Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House Sydney.
Forms for Companies can be obtained at the Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the Taxation
Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or
profession on their own account are required to furnish
Income Returns irrespective of the amount of their
income ; but no return of Income is required from any
person in receipt of salary or wages only if the gross
annual amount of such salary or wages does not exceed
£2OO.
All emplovers of labor, whether they themselves are
liable to make Income Tax Returns or not, are required
to furnish to the Commissioners of Taxation the name,
occupation, and address of all employees whose salaries
or wages for the vear 1901 aggregated £l6O and .upwards.
Public attention is called to the fact that a penalty
not exceeding Twenty Pounds is imposed on any
person neglecting to furnish the Return required of
him within the time above mentioned, and that persons
failing to furnish such Return are not relieved from
liability to the penalty by reason only that they may not
have received.a notice, to furnish the Return, or that the
prescribed Form may not have been delivered to them.
The attention of owners of taxable lands subject to
mortgage \s invited to the necessity for furnishing the
requisite particulars, in order that they may derive the
benefit of the deduction to which they are entitled in
respect of the interest on the mortgage of their lands,
including improvements thereon. Without these
particulars the Commissioners will be unable to make
the statutory deduction from the tax on the unimproved
values.
Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained on
application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the eighth day of
February, 1902, before mentioned.

HEALTH, STRENGTH, HAPPIHESS
HEALTH
Few recognise the BLISS of being
HEALTH, until SUDDENLY certain
noticed, slight at first, but gradually
causing annoyance and SUFFERING
in PERFECT
symptoms are
RECURRING,
, the individual
being forced to admit he is unwell, and finally compelled to acknowledge that PERFECT
HEALTH is a PRECIOUS BOON.
STRENGTH
What a blow to a man who discovers he is LOSING
his STRENGTH and VIGOR, and THAT perhaps at
a period when he most NEEDS ALL his physical
and mental capabilities to battle through life. They
are PRICELESS to him. He must by every means endeavor to REGAIN THEM.
HAPPINESS
Does happiness consist in Wealth? No. Who can
be happy without HEALTH and STRENGTH?
Wealth materially assists the endurance of suffering,
but PERFECT HEALTH, STRENGTH and VIGOR
should be the natural inheritance of EACH individual, and are invaluable gifts, tending to :
happy existence and long life. THE HEALTHY, STRONG MAN CAN contend, and SUCCESS
FULLY, with the billows of life. BUT HOW CAN THE WEAKLY AND SICKLY?
Health, Strength and Happiness
can be ALL REGAINED.

Why Women Fade.
How is it that women do not carry their years
as well as men? For a few years she blooms
like’ the most beautiful flowers, shedding a
radiance of light wherever she goes; the eye is
freshened by the sight of her. and everything
about her is touched by bewitching grace. But
her reign is as short as the flowers, and she is
soon left a care-worn, shambling wreck,. her
form bowed, her steps dragging, and her face
wrinkled. Complaining of the unhappy existence
and envying the man his vigor, she goes down
to an early grave, believing that her trials are a
heritage from Mother Eve. One woman’s
experience may prove a thousand women’s
salvation. “About six weeks ago I first used
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. I must say I
found them a most superior medicine,” writes
Mrs. Harrison, Eitzroy, Vic. “My complaint
was peculiar to women—languor, debility and
bilious attacks, which made my days perfectly
wretched. Indeed, there were spells when I
used to wonder in the morning how I could set
about my household duties, but they had to be
done, and I would struggle through the day
when I should have been in bed You can
imagine by all this how very agreeably sur-
prised I was to get such a very valuable
medicine, especially after the failure of many
others.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
are a perfect blood purifier and a positive
cure for biliousness, indigestion, constipation,
headaches, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples, boils, blotches, and
female ailments. Sold by chemists and store-
keepers, Is per bottle, or six bottles
6s. Gd. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.*
