SPORTING NOTIONS.
Conditions in second test match favored neither
side. Australians opened and Britishers finished on
sodden wickets. Each side lost 15 bats during % time
which bowlers revelled in. The Australians hit up 160,
while the English only notched 89. Former’s other five
wickets brought 305 ; latter’s, 147. This test produced
two records. Noble’s 7 for 17 has never been equalled in
like matches ; Turner’s 6 for 15 was the previous best
and had been so for years. Again, Noble’s 13
for 7/ wiped out Bates’s 13 for 102, also achieved
on the M. 0.0. ground nine years ago. At Kenning-
ton Oval, ten years back, Spotforth bagged 14 for 92.
Trumble’s “ hat trick ” in last match was the second in a
similar battle credited to an Australian. Spofforth did it
first in ’79 against Lord Harris’s lot.
* * * •
Relative superiority of Australian bowling un-
doubtedly brought about the undoing of the Englishmen.
Twas thought by some that omitting Laver and M'Leod,
for the purpose of strengthening the batting, would
result in disaster ; but Noble's top-rung form more than
balanced things. What the Englishmen thought .of it
may be gathered from Skipper Maelaren’s remarking after
being bowled for one: “A man cannot be expected to
Play jugglery.” The delivery that bamboozled Quaife
went to the leg first, then curled on to the wicket and
yorked the stonewaller. Happenings in this match
should, however, have little influence when the next team
is chosen ; there’s need for a slow bowler, and Gitfen,
Llarke or the left-hander, Saunders, might fill the bill.
The apparently delibeiate ignoring of the Wheat State
crack is arousing the curiositv of cricket-followers gener-
ally. Giffen did surprisingly well against the visitors in
their only match (so far) at Adelaide. What’s the matter,
anyhow ?
* * * •
An awful question arises from the bowling
averages in the two test matches. What is going to
happen to Maclaren and Co. if Barnes meets with an
accident, or loses his form.
The difference between the bad-wicket batting
of the second test match teams was very marked. The
Australians, one and all, seemed to lose their wickets
because they were bound to play a fast, risky game,
whereas the Englishmen, with the exceptions of Jessop,
.Maclaren, and Lilley (the latter made six runs in three
reckless lives), seemed incapable of an effort to get hold
o • the ball when Noble handled it. Noble took seven
wickets for nine runs after eight had heen scored off him
by Mac and Jessop. Moreover, he did it in a style that
made the English bowling look simple by comparison.
Two remarkable catches were credited to the
Englanders on the first day of the Melbourne test. In
the Australians’ first innings, Braund, in the slips, dis-
posed of Duff with marvellous agility. He threw himself
at tpe ball and took it left hand as a cat grabs a mouse,
fn the second innings Tyldesle.v must have run nearly 30
yards to take a skier from Darling on the bias. "The
£ a .ij ,' n T a ? btever. The speed and j'udgment
■ Idesley showed in getting to the right spot were
exceptional even in the best of cricket society.
As for the second innings of the Englishmen—
the innings in which they were called upon to j'ustify
their reputation as “a great run-getting
team --it was ruined at the outset by the nervous, care-
ful batting of Maclaren, Hayward, Quaife, and Lilley, who
made the howling more dangerous than, perhaps, it
really was by their policy of playing to stop in. On New
Years Day, when the Australians in their second innings
were playing for time, and the pitch was onlv beginning
to get hard, they lost five wickets for 48. On the Friday
the Englishmen started their trouble after luncheon, by
playing for Saturday’s gate, with the hope, of course, of
being able to win the game in due course. They calcu-
lated on making a fair score for the loss of not more than
two wickets, which would have left the issue sufficiently
doubtful to ensure a big attendance for a fine Saturday.
Major \\ ardill told his friends to expect two wickets
down for something like 200 runs. The board at the end
of the day showed five wickets down for 147, the result of
feeble cautiousness, tempered by an infusion of Jessop.
Six wickets were taken on the Friday for the
paltry total of 200, including Jessop’s quick and lively 32.
It was the dullest batting ever seen in any test match at
Melbourne, and it left an impression that Duff and
Armstrong in the first place, and five out of six English-
men in the next place, were endeavoring to make the
g^ e l ast as \ on £ as possible. But thunderstorms came,
with the result that the tail of Maclaren’s team went down
in the dirt, leaving Australia victorious by 229 runs.
Maclaren, as things happened, probably lost
the second test by choosing to give the Australians first
mnings on a wicket which was not at its worst when play
started, and which might have greased the ball sufficiently
to lessen Noble s effectiveness. But, anyway, the visitors,
were beaten on their merits, for although Darling’s policy
of keeping back his crack batsmen for the second day
w a ® a complete success, it needed the final stand
of Duff and Armstrong to do the trick. Had either
of these debutants failed, the Englishman would have
’® et l k to get (say) 300 runs, with more than a
whole day of dry wicket to do it in. But, presuming that
Maclaren had taken first innings, and been required to
start a second innings on New Year’s Day, like the Aus-
tralians, he would probably have followed the conserva-
Dve rule of sending his men to bat in the usual order, in
which case the “ best” wickets would have fallen before
6 o clock, and the Australians must have won.
Two illustrations of tbe hopelessness of poking
a £ ,^ le * n a second-thoughts way were given in the
Melbourne match. Clem Hill struck hastily at a no-ball
from Barnes on the off chance of scoring from it, and put
it into a fieldsman’s hands. Little Quaife, on the English
side, varied the monotony of his stonewall tactics with a
reckless little leg smite at a no-ball from Trumble. and
gave an easy catch to Darling.
♦ * * *
Occasional Visitor”:—“The more I see of
expert batting on a hard wicket the more I pity those
onlookers who sit .out a whole day’s play and call it
the only true cricket.’ Patient enthusiasts exch t
congratulations on the second day of the test-match* say-
ing Better than yesterday ! ” not because the Australians
were mastering the English bowlers, but because thev
were able to play ‘ the game.’ ‘ The game,’ as played by
first-class artists under perfect conditions, is a deadly dull
entertainment. Thousands of people at the Melbourne
ground last Thursday must have admitted to themselves
that it was much better fun to watch the downfall
of 25 wickets for 221 runs on the first day, than to admire
the careful scoring of 252 runs for a loss of 4 wickets on
the second. Cricket is not such a very fine art that the
public will ever learn to prefer a safe batsman who rarelv
lifts a ball to the dashing bat who skies it often. I wish
that -all matches were limited to two days, and every
wicket carefully soaked overnight.” 1

Re Melbourne crowd’s hooting of Noble. The
dropping of M‘Leod and Laver and inclusion of Duff and
Armstrong by the selection commitee (Noble, Trumble,
and was unanimous, so why Noble’should have been
singled out for special attention is unaccountable. Duff’s
manner of getting his runs, and his form in opposition to
the Victorians last and previous seasons, were ample justifi-
cation for choosing him. He also justified his selection by
topping" the scores in each inningrs ; while Armstrong*, the
other new man, carried out his bat for 4 and 45 respectively.
Duff was lucky in getting a chance at all, for had
Foidevin been available the North Sydney crack might
not have gone over to play against the Victorians.
* * * »
Contrary to most others, Darling believes that
there s much variety in the Australian bowling. Jones’s
is quite different from Noble’s in pace. Howell’s has a
peculiar flight and excellent length with an off-break
absolutely his own ; in the latest match Howell came
back very quickly as much as 6in. on a perfect pitch,
irumble keeps the batsmen busy, and Armstrong and
Trumper supply additional change of attack.

wdl be very successful, as he watches the hall right on to
the bat; his first innings (32) on the sticky wicket was
equal in merit to his 104 in the second—making allowance
for the much altered conditions.”

Duff and Armstrong’s 120 for last wicket in
second test match is a record for the series. Briggs and
Hunter’s 98 in Melbourne, January, ’B5, was the previous
best. Top notch figures for a first-class match are
Nicnolls and Roche’s 230 for Middlesex against Kent at
Lords in June, ’99. Next come Briggs’ and Pilling’s 173
for Lancashire against Surrey at Liverpool in June, ’B5

-P er^a P s the success which at-
tended the inclusion of young players in the second test
may have a beneficial effect on the selection committee
when choosing the men for the third test. There are in
the team some old players who stay there on their past
reputation, and not on their present form. Gregory for
instance, should be left out; his batting is Weak—in fact
h ® n °w shows no versatility of stroke at all. Jones
should be left out—his bowling of late having been of the
very second-rate order, and his batting simply shocking.
Trumper, also, should go ; his scores in the tests having
been 0,0, and 16. There are plenty of young players who
have shown respectable form for years, and never had a
good chance, owing to the presence of so many has-beens.
Hack, Saunders, Clarke, Stuckey, M’Beth, Farquhar, and
young Capp of Singleton, have all earned a place in the
test team, and should certainty be given a turn.
* * * •
Of 58 “tests” so far played between England
and Australia, former won 27, and latter 21; others were
drawn. Thirty-four have been contested in Australia,
resulting in 17 wins for Australia ; 15 wins for England,
and two draws. Twenty-four were played in England •
four won by the Australians; 12 by'the Englanders;
eight drawn through the three days’ limit.
* * * *
The Consecrated Press (which, of all papers,
should be able to get reliable tips about the Future)
writing before the second test match: “We will even
venture a little prophecy, and say that in the second test
match Gregory, Noble, and probably Duff will not make
a good performance against the Englishmen,” Now
Noble took nearly all the wickets, and Duff made top
score each time. A little more of such prophecy and
people will begin to doubt the efficacy of consecration.

Groulburn was shaken in its crease by the con-
duct of Maclaren’s XI. during the match Goulburn v.
England. Robson was put on to bowl down the Goul-
burn smiters with “grubbers,” which were regarded as
insults. After passing the Goulburn score the English
captain allowed himself to be run out, and the mighty
Jessop never showed up at all. A concert was got up for
the Bulls,” but they did not appear; and Maclaren,
on going away, characterised the wicket and outfield as
the worst he had ever seen. Maclaren should run his
show ” with a little more discretion ; if he could only
sit at the feet of Dan Barry for a season 1

Hoop Iron’s win of Tatt’s. Cup on New Year’s
Day was the biggest surprise of a meeting full of them.
Hundreds to four, or even three, might have been had,
but few risked a “ bean ” on this pronounced in-and-outer
who didn’t carry a cent of stable monev. At six furlongs
he galloped to the front, and in the next 220 yards held a
lead of a dozen lengths, and though he tired palpably and
swished his tail ominously down the straight while Blue
and co ' ra P idl y 'viped off the interveningspace the end
of the two miles saw him still in front. Time (3.3.)) equalled
the previous best (Nightingale’s in ’94) for the same race.
Hoop Iron was bred at Tucka Tucka (N.S.W.) by his
present owner (Mr. “ Jack ” Smith), and is by Metal'ffom
Nurse Girl (dam by Soldier), by Gozo from Carmen (dam
of Vocalist), by Goldsbrough from Dolly Varden, by Lord
of Linne (imp.) His first success was Warwick Farm
Handicap Dec. 7. Since then he ran second to Golden
King in A.J.C. Holiday Handicap, and nowhere in
bummer Cup on Boxing Day, though well backed. Hence
the turn-up for Mo. in an event which should have
given him a hard knock.

®;A. owners, being nearly unanimous,
wrecked the S.A.J.C. New Year’s Meeting* by scratching
the horses entered for it almost in a body. The “ fields ”
would have run from one to four starters, and the Club
postponed the whole meeting for 10 davs. It is said that
those owners who stood by the Club object to the post-
ponement, and will claim the various stakes. The others
had for grievance the non-granting by the S.A JC.
(which controls racing in the State) of three more davs in
the year, to be divided among the principal suburban
clubs. R. C. Baker, President of the Federal Senate, is
president of the committee, and would make no promise
whatever, so Dixon, State M.P., chairman of the dissen-
tients, put in all the scratching-papers entrusted to him
the day before the meeting.

Tut Ryan and Billy M'Coll gave a farcical
bruising exhibition of seven rounds at Sydney Gaiety
A. C. last week. Ryan—hog fat—brought the balance to
something in excess of 14st. though his ringside poundage
was announced as 12.4. Blubber literally hung over his hips
anrl round the ring like a jellyfish. First
round M Coll—trained to the moment—wore a scared
.ook, but subsequently forced the game and had much
the better of thing's, though hardly sending in a really
solid punch once. The affair looked a bit gory at one
stage through an old cut on Ryan’s left eye being re-
opened. M'Coll tired in the sixth and seventh rounds
but t other cnap was a lot worse, and the wipe went up in
his corner ere the eighth 3min. began. The crowd
hooted, but that was scant satisfaction for having to pay
to see a comparatively new hand and a to-all-appearance
absolutely untrained old has-been jig round each other
Ryan had underrated the Armidale piclc-and-shovel man
who, though not a Foley or Jackson, is a strong, gritty
fellow, full of fight, and always well, because he knows
no excesses.

Mile supremacy of N.S.W., at Otter S.C. gala
in Hellings’ Domain Baths, Sydney, Saturday, looked
Sure for Geo. Read. First 50yds. saw him lead, and ever
afterwards till the end, he added to his advantage
getting home with a margin of 25yds. in front of the only
Other starter—boy Dick Cavill—who struggled grittily all
the journey, but couldn’t pace it with his rival. Time
(24 52 3-5) was 5 4-ssec. slower than Read’s world’s record
(24 46 4-5), set up last year. Read’s falling off was
brought about by unusual roughness of the water and
the fact that, towards the finish, symptoms of cramp ap-
peared, otherwise new figures might "have been created
Cavill occupied 25 20 1-6 in his effort.
* » * •
The style in which M. Lewis won the Druids’
Wheel Race, at Melb. Exhibition Oval, showed plainly
enough what the Austral result would have been if the
other front-mark men had helped the sturdy Westralian
to make pace on the grass track. Handicap conditions
for Martin, A. C. Middleton, and M. Lewis were exactly
the same in both events, but the Druids’ Race was run to
kill the back-mark men, and they died away accordingly
Martin, who gave up the struggle in the sixth lap, must
have thanked his Austral stars when he saw Lewis sprint
away from A. C. Middleton and co., and pass the line a
four-lengths winner in 4min. 16sec.
• • • *
“■F.”: Melb. Bicycle Club “inquiry” into the
morals of last Austral, otherwise known as the Billmartin
Stakes, was the same old waste of time that bike racing
inquiries alwa} s were. Only two of the suggested de-
linquents were put on their oath, and all of them, oathed or
not, must have known that their replies couldn’t possibly
carry any weight. A man under suspicion of sporting
malpractice is the same sort of witness as a co-respondent
m a divorce case. The M. 8.0. committee asked each of
the summoned witnesses to say if he had tried his best to
win, and the answer was “Yes.” Whereupon the com-
mittee said, “ Are you a liar ? ” or words to that effect.
The answer was “No.” As to the rest of the evidence,
the tribunal referred it to the blessed League with the
remark that “an undesirable state of things had been
disclosed.”

King Edward commands Bishop Webber,
of Queensland, to preach at Sandringham. An-
other milestone on the road to an English
bishopric. When the Queen died, the Bishop at
once announced that the new Brisbane Cathedral
should be a memorial to her. There was “savvy”
there. When York visited Brisbane, the Bishop
rushed him to lay the foundation-stone. “Savvy”
again. And now he preaches to King Eduard.
Can such a man be kept out of an English see ?
Died, in Melb. Children’s Hospital, 2nd
Jan., Willie Taylor, a baby of 5, who, on 26th
Oct., fell from a G-ippsland train, sustaining injury
to the skull, and, in spite of two operations, re-
mained unconscious 68 days. This is not a record,
as Bishop Courtnay, of Jamaica, quotes, in “ The
Future State,” a case mentioned by Astley Cooper
of a sailor who fell from a yard-arm, and remained
unconscious above 13 months. He was then
trepanned, and recovered.

The cable by which Seddon offered his
eighth contingent for service in S’Africa, con-
sisted of about one line concerning troops and
about two dozen lines of what ought to be done to
people who disapproved of the war, and what
would have happened to them had they been in
Maoriland instead of in Britain. Almost im-
mediately afterwards Wellington Trades and
Labor Council met and said most disrespectful
things about the war, the despatch of any more
contingents, and King Dick himself, and talked
angrily about capitalistic murder and things like
that. And none of the serious things Seddon said
would happen to such people have ensued.
The holidays, with their festive moments,
have been laid aside for another twelve months,
and the nation has resumed its normal state of
toil, with drinks between the acts. And D.C.L.
whisky, which added so largely to the gaiety of
this nation during the recreation interval, has
resumed its cherished duty of adding to the pro-
sperity of its consumers by inspiring keen thought,
enterprise, and progressiveness. D.C.L., as the
Whisky of the Wise, has many important sides to
its always good character.*

The recent Towitta (S.A.) tragedy,
wherein an unaccountable man is alleged to have
broken into a farm-house late at night, and, for
no visible reason, cut a girl’s throat, was
attended all round by a lot of mystery and a
great deal more running for assistance than run-
ning at the alleged intruder. Two girls occupied
the cottage, but two brothers (aged 15 and 18)
slept in a building about 100 yards away. Mary
Schippan (aged 24) ran for her brother, August
Schippan, and told him about a strange ruffian in
the house. August Schippan ran half-a-mile for
Fred Henke. He came back with the informa-
tion that Fred Henke had advised him to run for
Alfred Lampert, Then apparently all three (the
two brothers and sister Mary) went back to the
house, waited there a little while without going
into the bedroom to see if the strange man
was still there, and then ran for Alfred Lampert.
He was the first person concerned who ran his
hardest in the right direction, but when he arrived
there was no man—only the dead body of 14-year-
old sister Bertha on the floor with her throat cut.
So far, it appears that nobody, save Mary
Schippan, saw anything of the man ; the dead
girl was not outraged ; nothing is said about any-
thing having been stolen ; the black tracker,
according to the telegraphed report, can find no
trace of any man at all, and the murder was
done with a knife belonging to the house, which
knife, stained with blood, is said to have been
afterwards put away in a safe. Altogether a
curious series of events.
Jones was forever pestering the other boarders
in his hash-foundry with his ideas of the usefulness of
corns as weather prophets. He had a pet corn, for which

The Discharged Prisoners Aid Society will
make a big mistake if it establishes an office to
which discharged prisoners must come for as-
sistance. The kind of prisoner most worth assist-
ing—the one who wants to forget all gaol associ-
ations and to start afresh—is just the one who
wouldn’t on any account advertise his misfortunes
by entering a door with the obnoxious sign-
board displayed above it. Prisoners should
be visited in gaol just before their discharge and
any possible arrangements made. An office may
be necessary for the society’s correspondence, but
the real society should be concealed in the hat
of its perambulating: agent, and he should be, if
possible, wholly devoid of the obtrusive air of
benevolence which gives some such agents away.
A good man who has had his nose broken at
cricket, and who wears a flannel shirt and a
miscellaneous hat, is the person required.

“ Uloola ” writes :
The young man, Alfred Buckley— or Alfred Peckham—
who was shot dead by the station-master while burgling
at Guildford (N.S.W.) railway-station, was a most ex-
emplary youth, being employed on Sundays as a sort of
churchwarden at the local Church of England. He
showed people to their seats, and went round with the
plate. Yet this apparently-innocent, bucolic boy went to
work on the “ burglar-proof” iron safe with the skill of a
professional cracksman. He industriously chiselled the
heads off the rivets at the top of the safe, working from
one corner along each side for some six inches, and the
portion of the safe-top—a sheet of iron half-an-inch
in thickness—thus released, was forced up and bent over,
leaving a hole through which both hands could have been
thrust. The ladder by which he reached the office win-
dow was made of a couple of thin saplings with rungs of
fencing wire. On two previous occasions similar ladders
were found in the scrub near Guildford station. The
same station has twice before been burgled, and no clue
as to the depredators was ever discovered. On the first
occasion a case of wine was “ lifted,” and the empty case
was afterwards found in a bush not far from Peckham’s ;
the second time letters were taken and opened and an
unsuccessful attack made on the safe.
The Sydney Ferries Company, which
divides 10 per cent, dividends with great regu-
larity, has had a dispute with its firemen
and deck - hands over the number of hours
worked and the rate of pay. The company
suggests 120 hours for a fortnight’s work at 70s.
for firemen—sevenpence per hour; and 40s. for
deck-hands—4d. per hour. The latter want 50s.
per fortnight, or one penny per hour more than
offered by the co. When one considers wages
like these, and also thinks of the great mass of
pastoral and agricultural employees who haven’t
wages even like these, the eight-hour and mini
mum wage movements which make something of
a show in the city seem like a small dab of white-
wash on a pretty big sepulchre.
The Law Times tells this yarn : The co-
respondent in a recent big divorce trial employed
a firm of solicitors, who worked up the defence so
skilfully that the petitioner lost his case. The
bill of costs was heavy, and the “co.” wrote to his
legal advisers to that effect, and, in the firm’s
opinion, was needlessly violent about the matter.
He wanted the various items set out in detail,
and declared he would have the bill taxed by the
court. The solicitors sent back an itemised
account which made the total bigger than before.
One item ran: “To attending you when you
admitted committing adultery with Mrs. X.,
6s. Bd.” The bill wasn’t sent in for taxation.

A TRYING CLIMATE
TANTALIZES
A Brisbane Liver.
RESTORED TO ITS DUTY.
Th® Cat* of
Mr. TIMOTHY CABSIDY.
(by a special reporter.
Everyone In South Brisbane knows
Glenross, where Mr. Timothy Cassidy lives,
in Merivale-street, near the West End
Brewery. The house appeals to the eye on
account of its spacious verandahs and the
vines which climb everywhere over the
trellis. Mr. Cassidy himself, though
favoured with such a delightful domicile,
has in the past been favoured with any-
thing but good health. Speaking to the
writer, Mr. Cass'dy said : —“That he could
only regard his late illness as a time of
positive danger. I may tell you, in the
first place,” said Mr. Cassidy, “ that it
was no Budden seizure, but rather in the
nature of a gradual break-down. You- feel
% trifle ‘ off at first, and you think nothing
of it; next week .you feel decidedly ill,
but you neglect it, thinking it a mere
nothing, which time will cure. That is a
freat mistake. That is the mistake I made,
thought that no medicine was necessary.”
“ What were your first symptoms ?” asked
the newspaper man, who was anxious to
get to the case itself.
“Sickness,” answered Mr. Cassidy.
“Sickness used to take me in the morn-
ings, and I can truthfully say I never ate
before I had vomited. Every morning you
could find me awake at four o’clock, some-
times not having slept a wink all night. I
would.tramp the floor for hours, sometimes
retching terribly. Perhaps, at twelve
o’clock in the day, I should make steps to
eat something, but this generally came
back after a short time. Not eating and
not sleeping I gradually fell an ay. My
whole nervous system broke down. It was
necessary for me to be very careful on rising
from a chair lest I should fall down. I
always pulled myself together on the se
occasions, for if I got up suddenly the room
would seem to swim round before me, and
I should stagger.”
fr The liver, I suppose ? ” said the re-
porter.
“Yes,” replied Mr. Cassidy, “but that
was only one out of many disagreeable
symptoms. Terrible headaches came over
me at times, the pain of which was so ex-
cjpMsiating as to force me to hold my head
between my hands and walk backwards and
forwards. Across the breast I often felt
sharp pangs like as though an iron band
were being tightened round my body. My
mouth was in a terrible state owing to my
stomach always being disordered. I often
saw floating specks before my eyes, and my
face became pale and yellow. This was
biliousness, but it was only one symptom of
nay complaint, which appears to have been
a complication in connection with the liver
amoLkidneys.”
“■How long did your illness last ? ” asked
the newspaper man.
_ “ Months and months. It was a dreadful
time, and I never think of that period of
my life if I can help it, consequently I can-
not give you the precise information you
desire on that point. You must know,
however, that when I lived in Ireland I
suffered in the same way, though in a lesser
degree. Indeed, I came to Australia solely
for my health, thinking the climate would
have set me up.”
‘ ‘ Could medical skill afford you no re-
lief ? * queried the reporter.
“ No,” replied Mr. Cassidy, “ neither
here nor at home. At home the sea voyage
was recommended to me, whilst out here
the chemists and doctors tried scores of
different kinds of medicine. But all was
without avail. Well, I went from bad to
worse. I kept up as long as I could, but
at last my spirit broke down. I became a
mere mass of shaking nerves. The slightest
sudden sound would upset me awfully. I
couldn’t eat, I couldn’t sleep, I couldn’t
read, I couldn’t smoke, I could do noth-
ing but groan and endure, clinging, as all
mortals will, to the one straw of hope he'd
out to me by the numerous medical men
upon whom I spent so much. At length
even this small hope seemed to go. I gave
the doctors up rather than they should give
me up, and I settled myself down to wait
for the inevitable. It was during the
last stage of my illness that I was induced,
much against my will, to try one more
remedy. A medicine I was told that had
worked some wonderfully strange cures in
‘ all gone ’ cases.”
“ And that remedy was V r
“ Clements Tonic. ”
“But for Cements Tonic I would not be
alive to-day, and I tell you this in all
seriousness. Clements Tonic,” said Mr.
Cassidy, in a weighty tone of voice, “ had a
fixed determinate effect soon after I com-
menced taking it. It was no up a; d down
busihess. It was not like brandy, which
just props you up for a moment to let you
down worse than before. Its work was
steady, sure, and progressive. All the
symptoms I suffered from began to slowly
decrease at the same time. Not, of course,
immediately, but in a few days. My head
felt clearer ; I was less sick. The pain in
my back became bearable. I was able to
doze off, and felt refreshed when I woke
up. My food was less odious to me, and
soon I could feel that my meals were doing
me good. And as time passed on and I Kept
taking Clements Tonic—and I took a fair
amount before I was quite cured—l gradu-
ally grew stronger. I walked erect as a
n w maD, for I could feel that I was cer-
tainly on the high road to recovery. At
length I was completely restored to health.
You see me now,” said Mr. Cassidy ; “ do I
look weak or nervous ? ”
“No, certainly not,” replied the re-
porter ; and, to tell the truth, Mr. Cass'.dy
looks wonderfully strong.
“ Well, then,” said Mr. Cassidy, “if you
will allow me to repeat what I said befot e I
should not be standing before you this
moment but for Clements Tonic.”
“In which case, I presume, your grati-
tude for your cure will prompt you to give
us your permission to publish what you&ite
just told me.”
“ Publish every word of it. The publio
ought to be informed of the facts.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Timothy Cassidy, of Merivale-street, South
Brisbane, in the State of Queensland, do hereby
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting' of three
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
three, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish the
same in any way ; and that Ido not receive any
payment for the same; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of The Oaths
Act 1867.
Declared at South Brisbane, this eleventh day of
February, one thousand nine hundred and one, be-
fore me,
W. J. TROUTON, J.P.

An old-age pension applicant in a N.S.W.
township told a story of hard luck. He had been
an employee on a station where it was customary
to allow the men 6 per cent, on any savings not
drawn at the end of the year. Lured on by the
big interest, he had left the bulk of his wages un-
touched for several years, and he alleged that
when the owner died the mortgagee stepped in
and refused to recognise the old man’s claim, and
he was turned away penniless.

“ D.W.” : “ Mixed ” bathing in the nude
is comparatively common in parts of Victoria. I
lfa,ve seen it many times with cyclists of both sexes
and m parties of as many as five along the beach at
Sandringham, and I remember three women and
two men being arrested for “bobbing up and
< i7-°w n ” a t 10 o’clock on Xmas morning on St.
Kilda beach, right in front of the Esplanade. At
the approach of a policeman the cabman who
accompanied the party drove off with all the
clothes, leaving the blushing constable in charge
of five “ altogethers ” until another cab could be
procured. Also once, when sculling up the Yarra
about a mile above Princes Bridge, I came upon
half-a-dozen larrikins and a damsel of about 17
seated on the bank and drinking beer from bottles
in the intervals of bathing, the girl and two of the
lads being a.s stark as statues and as unashamed.
The lady, in fact, called to me to join the com-
pany, and my refusal was hailed with much
language and pelting of mud.

Roast Pork.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chinese meat-hawkers round about Goulbum-
street, Sydney, ply their trade in the open, any
afternoon, unhindered by municipal by-laws.
They wear no blue apron, or smock, or pendant
steel as insignia of their calling. One peddles
raw beef; the other “ losum po’k.”
The beef man sets his basket on a crazy chair
between two dingy shop-doors, and sells under the
verandah. The pork-purveyor deposits his plant
in the street gutter, scorning the friendly shade.
Perhaps roast pork, looks attractive in the sun-
light.
American money-weight scales strike one as
very modern ; the Chinese used their equivalents
hundreds of years ago, and the Chinese butchers
use them to-day in Sydney to weigh out penn’orths
of raw beef and salt-pork. These scales are
simple. Just a thin stick a couple or three feet
long, like a ramrod, balanced from a bit of string
rove through a hole near the centre and circled at
intervals by gilt rings with fractional dots between
the circles. From one end is suspended, by small
chains, a sort of metal dish ; this holds the pro-
duce. The weight at the opposite end of the
wooden “ steelyards ” is a rough junk of lead about
as big as your fist, and as shapeless, tied to a I bit
of string looped loosely over the “ ramrod.” The
scales appear to weigh in fourpences, or equal
parts thereof —Id., 2d., 4d., and so on ; each com-
plete ring of gilt dots represents four penn orth of
meat. I don’t know if the scales are legal; but,
as the salesmen’s customers seem satisfied that
they get their money’s worth, why should the
inspector of weights and measures interfere ?
The beef looks like flesh torn off shins of
bullocks, and juicy bits of skirting. The
salesman is an apologetic person who seems
to fully realise the impropriety of obtruding him-
self or his common wares on public notice within
the charmed circle of Roast-Pork. He knows
that his place is in the shadows, and he accepts
the pennies of those who cannot afford to revel in
the delights half-cooked pig with a proper
modesty. , ~ .
The pork-peadler has the limelight $ his cus-
tomers are wealthy, and he wears the supercilious
smirk of the dealer in art-goods. He is a study
in lordly grease. His box and basket (one slung
each end of his bamboo, in transit) are saturated
with pork-fat, the hot sun making it fairly bubble
out of them in glistening sweat. The bubbles
don’t glisten long, however, for they are turned to
dull brown paste by the dust that blows for ever
in grimy Goulburn-street.
Butcher, box, basket, and bamboo are covered
with flies. The Chinese also swarm when the
pork-man comes along. It is nothing uncommon
to see 20 or 30 at a time crowding round the
hawker, waiting to be served with 4d., Bd., or a
shilling’s worth of quivering fat in slices, or
chopped up into little squares. As they wait they
watch every move of the butcher. The pork-
vendor uses the lid of his box as a chopping-block,
and the sharp chopper goes squash th rough the
hideous mess like a spade through a rotten potato.
To cut through tho hard-baked skin the butcher
strikes the back of the blade with the heel of his
hand, and, as the short, sharp chop through the
crisD crackling announces that steel has touched
wood, the pendant lips of the expectant customers
tremble, and are drawn in with a gasp of watery-
toothed anticipation.
-•* * *
The buyers grab their portion, pay their money,
and bolt for their dens ; many of them eating as
they run, and jabbering with joy between each
mouthful. The pork-peddler drops the coin into
his pocket, wipes his oily hands on his trousers,
straightens his back, draws the dirty wrist-band
of his pork-stained shirt-sleeve across his
perspiring top-lip, deftly squashes a fly with
his third finger in the corner of his eye, smiles
condescendingly on the surrounding onlookers, and
waits for the next customer.
There are tit-bits; pig’s heart and liver, baked
dry and brown, are to be had in penn’orths by
those who can afford it. The penn’orths are chips
about as big as a thumb. The sales of the brown
tit-bits are nothing to the sales of the dust-grimed,
fly-adorned, luscious pork.
These Goulburn-street Chinamen tearing into
the junks of “po’k” with their yellow tusks, or
throwing the juicy squares of half-cooked hog into
their generous mouths, and sucking them as a
greedy nursemaid sucks jujubes, supply a spectacle
that almost persuades a man to change his nose
and be a Jew.
Chinese pork must be approached with the
reverence due to the other ancient institutions of
the yellow race.
JOHN DRAYMAN.
“Adelaide”: Recently came across the
“mail-order” catalogue of Zion City general
stores, run in Chicago by the prophet Dowie. An
amazing book, with about the bulk of six
Reviews op Reviews. Such a combination of
business and religion has never been seen before;
the Salvarmy is not in the same street. “Every-
thing to eat, wear, and use,” says the cover, and
the index bears it out. Everything you can think
of (except, of course, playing-cards and whisky).
Also a good many other things ; as thus : “ fired
basket teas,” “ badger squeeze albums,” canned
succotash,” “misses’ mitts,” “Zion stick-pins,”
and “The lost 10 tribes.” John Alex. Dowie
writes an introduction, naively headed, ‘ IVIy
Father’s Business ”(!). He quotes much
Scripture, and with special approbation
the parable of the 10 talents, where the good ser-
vant was promoted for making 1000 per cent., and
the bad one was d d for making nothing. ‘‘ He
would not even put it in Zion Bank, where it
would have earned a fair interest,” says Dowie,
improving the occasion. “The profits of Zions
toil and of Zion’s trade should not go to Zion’s
enemies,” is the gist of the whole matter, and
“in the name of the Coming King” the faithful
are invited to buy, “ at prices and qualities which
cannot be surpassed,” vanilla pudding and zithers,
hymn-books and furniture, “short chemises
(honi soit) and “ substitutes for lard, Bertha
fascinators ” and “ dried blood.” Cheapness is the
main feature of the collection ; bicycles run from
three guineas to a maximum of five, and alleged
diamonds are dealt out to the virtuous at about as
many dollars as the wicked usually give pounds.
The short garment already alluded to is priced
12|d., which suggests sweating, but for six bob
you can get one. with a curved yoke, very fine
torchon lace, and—further details. “The most
elaborate chemise in the market,” remarks J.A.D.
from the wealth of his experience. Payment
should, if possible, be made through the Zion
Bank. Oh ! the Salvarmy has a lot to learn yet.
Keepers of the Life to Come.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Of all the quiet, gloomy children !" exclaimed
Letty Newbury, vigorously, as she contemplated
the basin of water in which she was about to
plunge her heated face; “ —but what else could
you expect? If you had known what she felt
about that unfortunate child before it made its
appearance ! ”
“Oh! well, you know,” replied her husband;
“ you must remember she was pretty badly off •
all those children already; her husband laid-up,
and this other one coming along ”
“The more need to give it a cheerful disposition,
since she couldn’t endow it with anything else. Oh !
it’s horrible the way women go on ; don’t wonder
you hesitate about giving us the franchise—we do
our own work so badly. And the sickly senti-
mentality ! —must make all baby’s clothes myself,
and ruin baby's health !—ruining her mind—and
its mind —by eternal whining and grumbling.”
Jack laughed, but there was a tender look m
his eyes as he laid his hand on her shoulder, and
spoke—softly. , . , ,
Her eyelids drooped to her flushing cheeks.
“No, not mine,” she answered, at once proudly
and shyly.
* * * *
He had to sail for Sydney a few months later,
which was unfortunate; however, the “girls”
came to keep Letty company. The “ girls ” were
her sisters —maidens of 40. There was also the
gracious mother-in-law sailing in at odd times to
see how Jack’s wife was getting on. In spite of
all this, Letty bore up bravely, and Jacks little
house was ever ringing with her voice in laughter
or song. .
Walls are thin in Australian homesteads, and
the foot of the telegram-boy sent a thrill to the
bedroom floor that adjoined the parlor. From her
window, Letty watched him depart. Then, after
an interval, she heard a low, continuous sound in
the parlor. They were sobbing with a murmur
of words, till both were broken by a cry: “ Oh,
Jack ! Oh, poor, poor Jack ! ”
A rapid footfall—Mrs. Newbury, sen., was
entering the parlor.
A murmur again—interjections — In a small
boat —capsized—brought home —drowned ! Oh,
Jack ! Jack ! ”
Again, and sounding far away: We must
keep it from Letty just now.” But she had no
idea who said this.
* * * *
Dead ! Drowned ! That brought a shuddering
realisation of fallen j aws and stiffened limbs.
Then came another realisation, not less vivid, of a
dependent life pleading for its own existence,
pleading against the madness of grief to which its
keeper would fain have yielded. And she listened,
and obeyed. A year ago, without crime, she
might have cast herself down on the bed, have
given way to this tempest of grief, have indulged
it till indulgence brought weariness, tears and
sleep; but to-day she was as one apart, bound by
Nature’s law to have, for a time, no griefs, no
emotions, no life of her own. That was what it
meant to be a mother.
Quietly, by strong force of will, she forced back
the terrible truth, to be realised later. He was
dead ; yet for a few days, hours perhaps, he must
still live—for her. She set herself to remember
all the happy, unemotional incidents. of their
short married life —their laughter, their humor.
What would he be doing now ? He rose before
her, bright-eyed, full of vigor. A vision of a
white, dead face floated past her, but she kept her
imagination fixed on the bright eyes. She must
act as if he were living and would return. There
was that shelf of his with the botanical specimens
which he had requested her to arrange ; for this
was their affection, that he could ask her to do
something for him, and trust her not to do it if
she felt too tired. The average wife prefers to be
a martyr, that her husband may seem a brute.
Her hand trembled; he was dead ! The room was
dotted with points of darkness.
She dropped her face on her hands to bring back
the blood to her head. She called up the vision
again ; she prayed that God would let her see it
often till her guardian duty was done.
She saw him on the deck of a homeward steamer;
his eyes met hers, as, whispering words of com-
fort, he put his arm round her. On dashed the
steamer, leaping higher on the waves, while the
seagulls screamed behind. The skies darkened ;
the seas darkened ; his arm slipped from her, and
she was alone, in the storm and the tempest and
the agony . . . days, weeks, centuries !
The yellow moon mocked her from its curtained
clouds; faces gathered above it, giant faces. . .
“ Put the lamp a little farther away, nurse,” from
one of them; and the moon was shifted to the
other end of the sky. And the vision of Jack ap-
peared once or twice again.
* * * *
“Your husband will be up to see you again
soon,” said the nurse, cheerfully. _
She looked up with eyes that did not brighten.
“ No, nurse, he doesn’t need to come any more,
now baby’s here—and safe.”
Then she wondered, because the Vision came
again, and seemed to make itself understood even
by the commonplace nurse. She was surprised
that God still troubled about the matter when all
necessity was over.
The Vision followed his mother into the next
room. “ She still looks at me strangely,” he
complained; “I wonder what made her faint the
day I came back ? ”
“ I fancy she must have heard the girls crying
at the news of the sudden death of that scapegrace
brother Jack of theirs. I warned them to keep it
from her.”
“You needn’t have; he was before her day. I
doubt if she ever saw him. No, it couldn’t have
been that; and it couldn’t have been my un-
expected appearance either, for she was half-
fainting before I caught her in my arms. Any-
how, she’s all right now.”
“A little feverish, but that will go off.”
“ And the baby seems a nice little chap.”
“A beautiful baby,” graciously assented the
mother-in-law.
C. CLYDR.
What a misfortune for Australian Repub-
licanism if there were in Britain a monarch like
Kaiser William, accurately described by one of
his countrymen as “the moving spirit of the
army, the director of foreign affairs ; the patron of
trade, industry, and agriculture, hierarch and
supreme critic of art.” Of him the latest anec-
dote : A friend, when the Kaiser was a boy, spoke
with compassion of his withered left arm. The
boy’s eyes fired. “Ah! ” he exclaimed, “ but it’s
not my sword arm ! ” William is a monarch who
believes in himself and in monarchy, not a dull,
tired man laying foundation-stone 3 in a dazed
sort of way for a living. Royalty as run by him
must be really dangerous to human liberty.

The Trekkers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lord looked down from Heaven above and
measured the earth below,
Away to the outer fringes—to the ioe and the
polar snow,
For He heard the cry of the trekkers that rose
with a mighty boom :
“ Too small is the world, O Jehovah ! Give room
to us, Lord, give room !
We have planted our banners in legions till the
East and the West have met,
But the pain of our hearts is gnawing and driving
us onward yet ;
And the outer fringe is gleaming with the glint of
our flags unfurled :
Give room to us, Lord, to plant them—Lord, open
to us a world !
We have trekked to the polar circles till the
night and day are one,
And we lay in the grip of the ice-god, in wait for
a six months’ sun ;
We have cruised with iceberg-consorts in the
glai’e of a Northern sky,
When the scythe-cut prow of the Death-ship has
flashed as she passed us by.
We have followed the sun in his passage, and hung
in his glaring track ;
We’ve lashed the waves of the world to foam with
our ceaseless beat and tack ;
We’ve given the lie to fancies sweet of islands
d ream-impearled,
And mapped the deep like a land-locked bay—and
we crave for another world !
“We’ve blazed the paths through a thousand isles
embedded in Eastern foam ;
We’ve hugged the crest of a proud-born wave that
over the world did roam ;
We’ve crushed the myths created in the half-
world days of yore,
And oaptured the dream-fed phantoms as we
swept from shore to shore.
We’ve measured the whole creation—mountain,
valley and plain;
We’ve plumbed the depths of the ooean-beds and
the wonders they contain.
Our broken ships and our tattered sails, on a
thousand beaches hurled,
Are fitting emblems of our prayer: ‘ Lord, give
us a shoreless world ! ’
“Look down on your sons, Jehovah 1 for how can
our lives be sweet
When Earth, that borders the realms of Space, is
marked by a million feet ?
We fly from the spreading legions that follow the
trails we blaze;
But give us a world to fly to ! make a bridge of
the Sun-god’s rays
For the passion that drives us forward ! You know
from your star-gemmed throne
We have trodden this planet mile by mile and
measured it zone by zone :
It is scarred with roads and highways. Let your
trekkers’ flags be unfurled
Once more, O Lord, in your honour ] Make
us a pathless world 1 ”
There’s an army camped on the fringes, an army
sullen and worn,
Waiting with hearts undaunted, and with pennons
dusty and torn;
Camped at the End of All Things, on the sands of
the Shore of Space,—
The vanguard of all the nations, the rebels of
every race.
’Tis the Army of the Trekkers: in its last Earth-
camp it is met,
Waiting, lingering, hoping that its prayer may be
answered yet—
That God, from out the legions of stars in His
far sky whirled,
Will spare but one for His Trekkers—will give to
His Trekkers a world !
N.S.W.
JAMES F. DWYER.
Quart-Pot and Billy-Can.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
No utensil is so generally used in the bush as the
billy-can; none is more widely distributed; none
better known in Australia. It is cheap, light,
useful, and a burden to no man. It goes with
every traveller; it figures in comedy and tragedy,
and has been the repository of the last words of
many a perished swagman. Often it is found with
the grim message scratched on the bottom, beside
the dead owner.
Billy is famous. Story-writers and poets have
immortalised him; he figures proudly in a hun-
dred tales, in a thousand poems. It would be
interesting to know how he originated, and who
first carried him. He is probably an evolution of
** Quart-pot ” —or is Quart-pot an overlander’s
improvement on Billy ?
For a horseman or cyclist on a short journey
who has no cooking to do, the quart-pot is the
handier. But where there is one quart-pot in the
bush there are ten billy-cans. The billy is carried
in all manner of hands—black, white, yellow and
brindle. Shearers, miners, drovers, knockabouts
and even the poorest deadbeats on the track’
carry it a-swing at their sides. It is swung under
wraggons, bullock-drays, hawkers’ vans—every
kind of vehicle that traverses the bush ; and it
jingles a tune to the Afghan from the back of his
camel. The dainty housewife in town not infre-
quently sets it on her polished stove, and it often
takes the place of the kettle in the cookie’s
home. Go into any aboriginal camp in the bush
and you 11 see billy-cans there, but seldom any
other utensil. There is scarcely a camp or hut in
Australia without one, and some have a dozen
And what an array they would make if they were
all stood m line ! There would be a hundred
miles of billy-cans ! And the quarts and pannikins
that are carried about would build a mountain.
Every traveller has a pannikin or two, and one
or two billies. Some have three—of varying sizes
to fit one in the other. The tea-billy and "meat-
billy are the most common ; the third is an
auxiliary. Some swagmen have a special water-
billy carried in the hand, with a tightly-fitting
drawn oyer it to keep it cool. The bag also
prevents the footman’s trousers being blackened
rrom contact, and the horseman’s pack from being
“They are of all sizes-from one to six
quarts. The most favored are the two-quart for
tea, and four-quart for meat; while the general
-round billy is the three-quart size. I have seen

boil their billies, for carelessness means waste of
time and injury to the billy. The old style was
to put a fork at each side of the fire, with a pole
SS n 0 t l| ang the billy from. The tripod
answered the same purpose. Another method
was to put two logs closely together, so that the
rnnml ? s * and oq them—which left very little

£ a *> rk > w’th the small end over the fire, and

the traveller had to be careful to keep his blaze

J\o such clumsy, time-wasting methods are em-

cross ’P ie . ce are Put up, and a chain or
looped wire, and hooks hung therefrom. The
traveller places two small sticks on the ground
vnth the ends in the fire, or rakes out a few coals,
and stands the billy on them, close to the fire. He
t b ' ,dd a Are round it, or jam it against the
wood. The new-chum does that, and his billy has
a bright, shiny glow. That is caused by hot
vapory smoke, the result of leaving insufficient
air-space. One notices, too, that the old hand
min r!fi bl Iy f° E' e , b n m ' whiie the inexperienced
man often puts his billy to the fire only partially
filled and consequently the rim, being unpro-
tected, is very soon burnt off. A half-filled billy
should never be placed against a fire, but stood or
hung over it. The old hand, again, lifts his quart
from the fire and can carry it a long distance, with
two sticks thrust crosswise through the handle so
that the ends grip the sides like a pair of tongs.
kk ° ks simple ; but it requires practice.
Billy stories are countless. Here are two •—An
old whaler was camped on the Severn River
hvmg on fish. He had only one billy. He first
made his tea, in it, which he poured into a panni-
kin then boiled his fish in it. He also had a small
block of wood the same size as the billy, on which
he sat and smoked. One evening at dusk he came
up from the river with a tomahawk in his hand
and making for what he took to be the block
stuck the tommy into it. A yell of dismay
escaped the old fellow when he found he had
driven it through the bottom of his billy. Yet
with a piece of calico through the gap, and the
edges battered down, he used it for months after.
A • kn ® w found a rusty billy, half-
embedded m mud, at the edge of a waterhole. It
was full of water, and on forcing the lid off, he
round a fish, about a pound weight, scurrying
around m it. It was shaped like a boomerang
and had undoubtedly got in through a small aper-
ture in the lid when very young. That billy, pro-
bably, performed its last duty when it boiled its
life-long prisoner.
Among some travellers billy-boiling is a medium
of gambling. The man of experience, looking
over an array of well-used billies, will lav
odds on one to boil first. Of course, some
are specially adapted for quick boiling, whilst
others are naturally slow.” A man with a quick
boiler is always ready to back it against any other.
He understands it, and can judge its boiling time
to within a few seconds. An old billy will boil
quicker than a new one. The water is also worth
considering. River water will boil quicker than
ram water, stagnant water quicker than running
water; whilst water that has once been boiled
and cooled will boil again quicker than any other
Yet, there is many a tedious wait for the billy
to boil, rejoicing of hungry ones when it
wi 1 ?* 8 tq. bubbl ®- Tbe old diggers on Ballarat
“ 0h ’ what would you
do if the billy boiled over ? ” when it was time to
make the tea. And what legends are wrapped
around the billy! Yarns are always being told
and bush songs are always being sung around a
million camp-fires while the billy boils.
N.S.W.
